Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
Judgment in Kalam: Religious and Rational
Sanusi defines the two types of religious judgment and the three rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classification of judgment in kalam into religious and rational, and the three subdivisions of rational judgment.

Core Idea
Judgment is threefold: rational, customary, and religious, each with its own subdivisions.

Before delving into theological proofs, Muslim theologians (mutakallimun) first establish a clear classification of judgments (ahkam). This passage from Sanusi introduces the fundamental types of judgment: religious (shar‘i) and rational (‘aqli), each with its own subdivisions. Understanding this taxonomy is essential for following kalam arguments about what is necessary, impossible, or possible for Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
The OCR is corrupt; some words are marked [?]. Focus on the overall structure.
2
Identify the two types of religious judgment
Notice that religious judgment is divided into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). The necessary is immediate from revelation; the theoretical requires reasoning.
The example 'one is a quarter of four' is a mathematical example, not a typical religious one.
3
Identify the three types of rational judgment
Rational judgment is divided into necessary (wājib), impossible (mustaḥīl), and possible (jā’iz). These three categories are the core of kalam reasoning about God and the world.
4
Understand the consequence of denial
The passage states that denying something that is necessarily known from religion leads to unbelief (kufr). This highlights the seriousness of rejecting established religious knowledge.
5
Review the overall classification
The text concludes that judgment is divided into three: rational, customary, and religious. This frames the whole discussion.
Customary judgment is based on habitual experience, though not detailed here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم الشرعي الضروري حكمنا بأن هذا حرام وهذا حلال [؟] والحكم الشرعي النظري حكمنا بأن الواحد ربع عشر الأربعين [؟] مثال الحكم العادي الضروري [؟] ومثال الحكم العقلي ما تقدم [؟] والحكم العقلي ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: واجب ومستحيل وجائز [؟] والذي يجب إنكاره الكفر [؟] وينقسم الحكم إلى ثلاثة أقسام: عقلي وعادي وشرعي [؟]
The necessary religious judgment is our judgment that this is forbidden and this is permissible. The theoretical religious judgment is our judgment that one is a quarter of four. An example of the customary necessary judgment is... And an example of the rational judgment is what preceded. And the rational judgment is divided into three divisions: necessary, impossible, and possible. And what necessitates denial of it is unbelief. And judgment is divided into three divisions: rational, customary, and religious.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage appears to define and classify types of judgment (al-hukm) in kalam. It first distinguishes two types of religious judgment (al-hukm al-shar‘i): necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). The necessary is known immediately from revelation (e.g., prayer is obligatory), while the theoretical requires reasoning (e.g., that one is a quarter of four, though this example is atypical). It then mentions customary judgment (al-‘ādī), which is based on habitual experience. Finally, it enumerates the three rational judgments (al-‘uqūlī): necessary (wājib), impossible (mustaḥīl), and possible (jā’iz). The text also notes that denying something that is necessarily known from religion leads to unbelief (kufr). The overall structure is a standard kalam classification: judgment is threefold—rational, customary, and religious.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي ضروري
حكم شرعي نظري
حكم عقلي
حكم عادي
واجب
مستحيل
جائز
كفر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'rational judgment' includes only logical or mathematical truths, while here it refers to the threefold division used in kalam for theological reasoning about God.
Correction: In kalam, rational judgment is not about mathematical propositions but about necessary, impossible, and possible attributes of beings, especially God.

Checkpoint
How many divisions of rational judgment does Sanusi mention?
A) Two: necessary and possible
B) Three: necessary, impossible, and possible
C) Three: rational, customary, and religious
D) Two: theoretical and necessary

Key Takeaway
Judgment in kalam is classified into rational, customary, and religious, with rational judgment further divided into necessary, impossible, and possible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat any religious obligation as optional or debatable when it is actually a necessary judgment (ḍarūrī) that demands your full submission? What step will you take today to align your practice with that certainty?
?
The rational judgment of 'possible' (jā’iz) can lead to complacency about God's attributes. In what specific area of your belief are you acting as if something about God is merely possible when it is actually necessary or impossible? How will you correct that?
?
If denying a necessary religious judgment leads to kufr, how seriously do you take learning the fundamentals of your faith? What is one necessary judgment you need to fully accept and live by starting now?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
The Three Rational Rulings: Wajib, Mustahil, Ja'iz
Sanusi defines the rational ruling and its three divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of rational judgment (wajib, mustahil, ja'iz) and how each is known either necessarily (daruri) or speculatively (nazari).

Core Idea
Rational judgments are either necessary, impossible, or possible, known by intuition or reasoning.

Before exploring arguments about God and creation, we need a clear vocabulary for what the mind can affirm or deny. Sanusi lays this foundation by defining rational judgment and dividing it into three exhaustive categories. Each category has two modes of knowledge: immediate intuition and reasoned inference. This passage provides the conceptual tools for all subsequent kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of rational judgment
Start with the opening sentence: 'As for the rational ruling... without depending on repetition or the positing of a positor.' Notice that the intellect works independently of custom and divine decree.
2
Identify the three categories
Find the three divisions: al-wujub (necessary), al-istihalah (impossible), al-jawaz (possible). Each is defined by what the mind can or cannot conceive.
These three are mutually exclusive and collectively exhaustive.
3
Distinguish daruri from nazari
For each category, Sanusi gives two examples: one known necessarily (without proof) and one known speculatively (through reasoning). Notice the pattern.
Daruri is self-evident; nazari requires argument.
4
Consider the implications
Ask yourself: Why is this threefold division important? The answer: all kalam arguments about God's attributes and actions rely on classifying claims into one of these three categories.
For example, God's existence is wajib; a partner for God is mustahil; rewarding the obedient is ja'iz.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ الْعَقْلِيُّ : فَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ مِنْ غَيْرِ تَوَقُّفٍ عَلَى تَكَرُّرٍ وَلا وَضْعِ وَاضِعِ . وَأَقْسَامُهُ ثَلاثَةٌ : الْوُجُوبُ ، وَالاسْتِحَالَةُ ، وَالْجَوَازُ . - فَأَلْوَاجِبُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ عَدَمُهُ ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةٌ ؛ كَالتَّحَيُّرِ لِلْحِرْمِ [؟] ، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً ؛ كَوُجُوبِ الْقِدَمِ لِمَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ - وَالْمُسْتَحِيلُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةً ؛ كَتَعَرِّي الْحِرْمِ عَنِ الْحَرَكَةِ وَالسُّكُونِ مَعاً ، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً ؛ كَالشَّرِيكِ لِمَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ - وَالْجَائِزُ : مَا يَصِحُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ وَعَدَمُهُ ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةً ؛ كَالْحَرَكَةِ لَنَا ، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً ؛ كَتَعْذِيبِ الْمُطِيعِ وَإِثَابَةِ الْعَاصِي
As for the rational ruling (al-hukm al-‘aqli): it is affirming or negating something without depending on repetition or the positing of a positor. Its divisions are three: necessity (al-wujub), impossibility (al-istihalah), and possibility (al-jawaz). The necessary: that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind; either necessarily (daruratan) like the impossibility of a body [؟] being devoid of both motion and rest, or speculatively (nazaran) like the necessity of pre-eternity for our Lord, exalted and majestic. The impossible: that whose existence is not conceivable in the mind; either necessarily like a body being devoid of both motion and rest simultaneously, or speculatively like a partner for our Lord. The possible: that whose existence and non-existence are both valid in the mind; either necessarily like motion for us, or speculatively like punishing the obedient and rewarding the disobedient.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the rational ruling (al-hukm al-‘aqli), which is a judgment made by the intellect independently of custom or divine decree. It does not depend on repetition or on a positor's act. The author then divides it into three categories. (1) The necessary (wajib): that which the mind cannot conceive as non-existent. This can be known necessarily (daruratan) by immediate intuition, e.g., the impossibility of a body being simultaneously moving and still (a contradiction). Or it can be known speculatively (nazaran) through reasoning, e.g., the necessity of God's pre-eternity. (2) The impossible (mustahil): that which the mind cannot conceive as existent. Again, either necessary (e.g., a body cannot be both moving and still at once) or speculative (e.g., the existence of a partner for God). (3) The possible (ja'iz): that which the mind can conceive as existent or non-existent. Either necessary (e.g., motion for us, which we can imagine both present and absent) or speculative (e.g., God punishing the obedient or rewarding the disobedient in the abstract, though He has promised otherwise). This threefold division is foundational for kalam arguments about God and the world.

Key Terms
حُكْم عَقْلِيّ
وُجُوب
اسْتِحَالَة
جَوَاز
ضَرُورَة
نَظَر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Revelation or custom is required for rational judgments.
Correction: Rational judgments are independent of repetition (custom) and divine decree (positor). They are based solely on intellect.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a necessary (daruri) rational impossibility according to the passage?
A) The existence of a partner for God.
B) A body being devoid of both motion and rest simultaneously.
C) God punishing the obedient.
D) The necessity of God's pre-eternity.

Key Takeaway
Rational judgments divide into necessary, impossible, and possible, each known either necessarily or speculatively, forming the basis of all kalam reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own existence, do you see yourself as 'possible' in the rational sense? What does that imply about your dependence on the Necessary Being, and how should that change the way you live today?
?
Sanusi says that punishing the obedient is 'possible speculatively' even though God has promised not to. Does this challenge your trust in God's promise, or does it deepen your understanding that His acts are based on His will, not compulsion? Examine your heart: do you rely more on God's attributes or on your own assumptions?
?
Think of a belief you hold about God or the afterlife that you have never rationally examined. Place it under one of Sanusi's three categories. Can you defend it as necessary or likely? Or is it merely possible? What step can you take today to ground that belief in clear reasoning?

The Four Types of Correlation (ربط)
Sanusi enumerates the four types of correlation between existence and non-existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of logical correlations between existence and non-existence and how they form the basis of causal reasoning in kalam.

Core Idea
Every causal relationship in kalam fits one of four existence/non-existence patterns.

Sanusi introduces four fundamental logical relationships between existence and non-existence. These are not just abstract categories — they are the building blocks for reasoning about God's attributes and actions. Understanding these four patterns will help you follow the structure of theological proofs throughout this course.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how each correlation is defined by pairing existence or non-existence with existence or non-existence.
The examples are from everyday experience: eating, satiety, hunger.
2
Identify each type
List the four types: existence-existence, non-existence-non-existence, existence-non-existence, non-existence-existence. For each, recall the example given.
3
Understand the logical pattern
Think about why these four are exhaustive. Any causal statement involves either the presence or absence of two things.
This is similar to truth tables in logic.
4
Apply to a new example
Try to create your own example for each type (e.g., light and darkness). This will reinforce your understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِوُجُودٍ : كَرَبْطِ وُجُودِ الشَّبَعِ بِوُجُودِ الْأَكْلِ · وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِعَدَمٍ : كَرَبْطِ عَدَمِ الشَّبَعِ بِعَدَمِ الْأَكْلِ وَرَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِعَدَمٍ : كَرَبْطِ وُجُودِ الْجُوعِ بِعَدَمِ الْأَكْلِ - وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِوُجُودٍ : كَرَبْطِ عَدَمِ الْجُوعِ بِوُجُودِ الْأَكْلِ
The connection of existence to existence: like the connection of the existence of satiety to the existence of eating. And the connection of non-existence to non-existence: like the connection of the non-existence of satiety to the non-existence of eating. And the connection of existence to non-existence: like the connection of the existence of hunger to the non-existence of eating. And the connection of non-existence to existence: like the connection of the non-existence of hunger to the existence of eating.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage enumerates the four types of causal correlation (الربط) between existence and non-existence in the context of rational judgments. The author presents each type with a concrete example from everyday experience. 1. Existence to existence: e.g., satiety exists because eating exists. 2. Non-existence to non-existence: e.g., no satiety because no eating. 3. Existence to non-existence: e.g., hunger exists because eating does not exist. 4. Non-existence to existence: e.g., no hunger because eating exists. These four patterns form the logical structure for all causal reasoning in kalam, particularly in arguments for God's existence and attributes.

Key Terms
ربط
وجود
عدم
شبع
أكل
جوع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These are simply examples of cause and effect in the physical world.
Correction: They are logical relations between existence and non-existence used in theological arguments; the examples are illustrative but the pattern is universal.

Checkpoint
Which correlation is 'the existence of hunger is connected to the non-existence of eating'?
A) Existence to existence
B) Non-existence to non-existence
C) Existence to non-existence
D) Non-existence to existence

Key Takeaway
All causal reasoning in kalam is built on four possible correlations between existence and non-existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a recurring difficulty in your life. Which of the four types of correlation have you been assuming—perhaps unconsciously—about its cause? How might considering the other three possibilities change your next step?
?
If every relationship between God's attributes and creation follows one of these patterns, which pattern best describes your current experience of God's mercy? What does that pattern suggest about something you might be neglecting or misconnecting in your worship?
?
This lesson shows that the absence of a problem (like hunger) can be connected to the presence of a good action (like eating). In your spiritual life, where have you been waiting for a problem to disappear instead of actively connecting it to a positive change you need to make?

Rational Judgments

The Three Rational Judgments: Necessary, Impossible, Contingent
Sanusi defines rational judgment and classifies it into three categories.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of rational judgment and its three divisions: necessary, impossible, and contingent, along with their self-evident and inferential subtypes.

Core Idea
Rational judgments are the building blocks of all theological reasoning.

This passage from Sanusi’s Umm al-Barahin establishes the foundational classification of rational judgments. Before we can argue about God, we must understand what the mind can affirm or deny. Sanusi defines rational judgment and then divides it into three categories, each with two subtypes.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
Read the definition of rational judgment: it is an affirmation or negation that does not depend on repetition or convention. This is an a priori judgment.
Contrast this with empirical judgments that require repeated observation.
2
Learn the three categories
Identify the three main divisions: necessary (الواجب), impossible (المستحيل), and contingent (الجائز). Note what each means for the mind's ability to conceive existence or non-existence.
3
Distinguish self-evident from inferential
Within each category, notice the two subtypes: self-evident (ضرورة) and inferential (نظرا). Self-evident cases are immediately clear; inferential cases require proof.
For example, a body having motion is self-evident, while God's eternity requires proof.
4
Apply to theological examples
Consider the examples Sanusi gives: God's oneness (necessary), a partner for God (impossible), punishment of the obedient (contingent). Reflect on why each fits its category.
This prepares for later arguments about God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ الْعَقْلِيُّ : فَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ مِنْ غَيْرِ تَوَقُّفٍ عَلَى تَكَرُّرٍ وَلا وَضْعِ وَاضِعٍ . وَأَقْسَامُهُ ثَلاثَةٌ : الْوُجُوبُ ، وَالاسْتِحَالَةُ ، وَالْجَوَازُ . - فَأَلْوَاجِبُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ عَدَمُهُ ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةٌ ؛ كَالتَّحَيُّرِ لِلْحِرْمِ ، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً ؛ كَوُجُوبِ الْقِدَمِ لِمَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ - وَالْمُسْتَحِيلُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةً ؛ كَتَعَرِّي الْحِرْمِ عَنِ الْحَرَكَةِ وَالسُّكُونِ مَعاً ، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً ؛ كَالشَّرِيكِ لِمَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ - وَالْجَائِزُ : مَا يَصِحُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ وَعَدَمُهُ ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةً ؛ كَالْحَرَكَةِ لَنَا ، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً ؛ كَتَعْذِيبِ الْمُطِيعِ وَإِثَابَةِ الْعَاصِي
As for the rational judgment, it is the affirmation or negation of a thing without dependence on repetition or the positing of a positor. Its divisions are three: necessity, impossibility, and contingency. So the necessary is that whose non-existence is inconceivable in the mind, either necessarily, like the possession of motion for a body, or inferentially, like the necessity of eternity for our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted. And the impossible is that whose existence is inconceivable in the mind, either necessarily, like a body being devoid of both motion and rest together, or inferentially, like a partner for our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted. And the contingent is that whose existence and non-existence are both valid in the mind, either necessarily, like motion for us, or inferentially, like the punishment of the obedient and the reward of the disobedient.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'rational judgment' (الحكم العقلي) and enumerates its three primary divisions: the necessary (الواجب), the impossible (المستحيل), and the contingent (الجائز). The definition states that a rational judgment is an affirmation or negation of a proposition that does not require repetition of experience (تكرار) nor the convention of a lawgiver (وضع واضع); i.e., it is purely a priori. Each division is then explained: the necessary is that whose non-existence is inconceivable by reason; it can be self-evident (ضرورة), like a body necessarily has motion, or acquired through proof (نظر), like God's eternity. The impossible is that whose existence is inconceivable; either self-evident, like a body being simultaneously motionless and at rest, or inferential, like the existence of a partner to God. The contingent is that whose existence and non-existence are both rationally possible; either self-evident, like our own motion, or inferential, like God punishing the obedient or rewarding the disobedient (since He is not obliged to do so). This threefold classification is the foundation of all kalam arguments.

Key Terms
الحكم العقلي
الوجوب
الاستحالة
الجواز
ضرورة
نظر
الواجب
المستحيل
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'necessary' here means logically necessary in a purely formal sense, like a tautology.
Correction: In kalam, necessary means that the mind cannot conceive its non-existence given sound reasoning; it can be self-evident or proven.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the definition of the necessary (الواجب) in rational judgment?
A) What is affirmed by revelation
B) Whose non-existence is inconceivable in the mind
C) What is always observed in nature
D) What depends on repetition for its certainty

Key Takeaway
Rational judgments are divided into three categories—necessary, impossible, and contingent—each established either by immediate reason or by proof, forming the basis of kalam reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you claim to believe in Allah, is your belief based on rational necessity (like the example of a body needing motion) or is it merely a contingent belief you could abandon under pressure? Which of the three categories best describes your current conviction?
?
The passage states that God’s punishing the obedient is only contingent (possible), not necessary. Does this truth unsettle you? How does it challenge any hidden sense that you 'deserve' paradise based on your deeds?
?
If everything about your life—your health, wealth, relationships—is contingent (its existence and non-existence are equally possible in reason), why do you cling to them as if they were necessary? How would your daily worries change if you truly internalized this?

Rational Judgments

Classification of Existents: Need for Locus and Specifier
Sanusi classifies existents into four types regarding need for a locus and specifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi classifies existents into four categories based on their need for a locus (mahall) and a specifier (mukhassis), and why this classification is foundational for distinguishing the Creator from creation.

Core Idea
Existents are defined by what they need — only Allah needs nothing.

Sanusi begins his fifth preface by laying out a fourfold classification of existents. This division is not arbitrary; it is the conceptual skeleton of all kalam arguments about the nature of God and creation. By examining what each kind of being requires, Sanusi establishes a clear line between the Necessary Being and all contingent things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the four categories
Find each of the four divisions listed by Sanusi. Pay attention to the criteria: need for a locus (mahall) and need for a specifier (mukhassis).
The categories are clearly marked with 'قسم'.
3
Understand the terms 'locus' and 'specifier'
A locus is the subject in which an accident inheres. A specifier is an agent that brings something into existence selectively. Note how these terms frame the classification.
4
Note the unique status of Allah's essence and attributes
Allah's essence needs neither locus nor specifier. His attributes need a locus (His essence) but no specifier. This distinguishes them from created entities.
This is a key doctrinal point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقدمة الخامسة : في الموجوداتِ . وَالْمَوْجُودَاتُ بِالنِّسْبَةِ إِلَى الْمَحَلَّ وَالْمُخَصِّصِ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَامٍ : - قِسْمٌ غَنِيٌّ عَنِ الْمَحَلَّ وَالْمُخَصَّص : وَهُوَ ذَاتُ مَوْلانَا جَل وَعَلا . - وَقِسْمٌ مُفْتَقِرٌ إِلَى الْمَحَلٌّ وَالْمُخَصَّص : وَهُوَ الأَعْرَاضُ . - وَقِسْمٌ مُفْتَقِرٌ إِلَى الْمُخَصَّص دُونَ الْمَحَلَّ : وَهُوَ الأَجْرَامُ . - وَقِسْمٌ مَوْجُودٌ فِي الْمَحَلَّ وَلَا يَفْتَقِرُ إِلَى مُخَصِّص وَهُوَ صِفَاتُ مَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ .
The Fifth Preface: On Existents. Existents with respect to the locus and the specifier are four divisions: A division independent of the locus and the specifier: and it is the Essence of our Master, Glorious and Exalted. And a division in need of the locus and the specifier: and it is the accidents. And a division in need of the specifier but not the locus: and it is the bodies. And a division existing in the locus and not in need of a specifier: and it is the attributes of our Master, Glorious and Mighty.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage is a classification of existents according to their relationship to a locus (mahall) and a specifier (mukhassis). The author divides them into four categories. First, that which is independent of both locus and specifier, which is the Essence of Allah. Second, that which is in need of both locus and specifier, i.e., accidents (a'rad). Third, that which is in need of a specifier but not a locus, i.e., bodies (ajram). Fourth, that which exists in a locus but is not in need of a specifier, i.e., the attributes of Allah. This classification establishes the unique status of the divine essence and attributes as distinct from created entities.

Key Terms
موجود
محل
مخصص
ذات
أعراض
أجرام
صفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that divine attributes are in need of a specifier like accidents, because they inhere in a locus (the divine essence).
Correction: However, divine attributes are eternal and do not require a specifier because they are necessary concomitants of the essence, not contingent like accidents.

Checkpoint
According to the Fifth Preface, which category of existents is in need of a specifier but not a locus?
A) The Essence of Allah
B) Accidents
C) Bodies
D) Attributes of Allah

Key Takeaway
The fourfold classification of existents based on need for locus and specifier establishes the absolute independence of Allah's essence and the dependence of all created things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If only Allah is independent of all need, what in your life do you secretly treat as if it were independent of Him—your health, your wealth, your relationships? How can you actively remind yourself of your absolute need for Him today?
?
The attributes of Allah are eternal and uncaused, woven into His very essence. Does your confidence in His promises reflect this permanence, or is it shaken by temporary circumstances? Commit to one act of trust today that shows you believe His attributes are unchanging.
?
Everything in the universe except Allah needs a specifier—including you. Have you fully felt the weight of your contingency? Take one minute right now to say aloud, 'I need Allah to exist at every moment,' and ask yourself if your heart truly depends on Him.

The Three Types of Judgment: Religious, Rational, Customary
Sanusi classifies judgments into three types based on epistemic source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three-fold division of judgments (ahkam) into religious (shar'i), rational ('aqli), and customary ('adi) based on their epistemic source, and be able to identify examples of each.

Core Idea
Judgments are classified by how we know them: revelation, reason, or repeated experience.

In this foundational classification, Sanusi teaches us that every judgment (hukm) we make—whether affirming or negating something—can be traced back to one of three sources: divine revelation, pure reason, or customary observation. This passage establishes the criteria for distinguishing these three types and provides clear examples. Understanding this division is essential for navigating later theological discussions.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi first states the division, then explains the criterion, and finally gives examples.
2
Identify the three categories and their definitions
The passage presents three types of judgments. For each, note how Sanusi defines it based on its source of knowledge: revelation (shar'), reason ('aql), or repeated experience ('adah).
The definition comes in the middle part of the passage after the initial division.
3
Analyze the examples
Sanusi gives two examples for each type: one in affirmation and one in negation. Check that you understand why each example belongs to its respective category.
For customary judgment, note that it depends on repeated observation, not pure reason.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني : انقسام الحكم إلى شرعي ، وعقلي ، وعادي ] وَيَنْقَسِمُ إِلَىٰ ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : شَرْعِيُّ ، وَعَادِيٌّ ، وَعَقْلِي . أنَّ الحكم الذي هو إثباتُ أمرٍ أو نفيه يتنوع إلى ثلاثةِ أقسام؛ بمعنى : الأنواع وهي الثلاثةُ المذكورة؛ لأنَّ الثبوت أو النفي اللذين في الحُكْمِ : إِمَّا أَنْ يُسندا إلى الشرع بحيثُ لا يُمكنُ أن يُعلَما إلا منه، أو لا، والثاني إما أن يكتفي العقل في إدراكِهِ مِنْ غيرِ احتياج إلى تكرر واختبار، أو لا. فالأولُ : الشرعي : كقولنا في الإثبات : الصلوات الخمس واجبةٌ، وقولنا في النفْيِ : صوم يوم عاشوراء ليس بواجب . والثاني : العقلي : كقولنا في الإثباتِ : العشرة زوج، وقولنا في النفي : العشرة ليس بفرد . والثالث : العادي : كقولنا في الإثبات : النار محرقة، وقولنا في النفي : الماء ليس بحارق . (۱) قدَّم العادي على العقلي مع كونه أقوى منه؛ لاشتراكه مع الشرعي في مطلق الإسناد. مفاد «بناني» (ص ۷)
Meaning: the division of judgment into religious (shar‘i), customary (‘adi), and rational (‘aqli). And it divides into three divisions: religious, customary, and rational. That the judgment which is the affirmation or negation of a matter is diversified into three divisions, meaning types, and they are the three mentioned. Because the affirmation or negation that are in the judgment: either they are attributed to the Law such that they cannot be known except from it, or not. And the second: either the intellect is sufficient in its perception without needing repetition and experimentation, or not. So the first: religious, like our statement in affirmation: the five prayers are obligatory, and our statement in negation: fasting on the day of Ashura is not obligatory. The second: rational, like our statement in affirmation: ten is even, and our statement in negation: ten is not odd. The third: customary, like our statement in affirmation: fire is burning, and our statement in negation: water is not burning. (1) He presented the customary before the rational even though it is stronger than it, because of its sharing with the religious in absolute attribution. – According to Banani (p. 7).
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage classifies judgments (ahkam) into three types based on their epistemic source. The author first states the division: shar‘i (religious), ‘aqli (rational), and ‘adi (customary). He explains that the affirmation or negation in a judgment is either attributed to the Law (shar‘) such that it cannot be known except from revelation – that is shar‘i; or it is not. If not, then either the intellect (al-‘aql) suffices to perceive it without needing repeated experience and experiment – that is ‘aqli; or it requires repeated observation and cannot be deduced by pure reason – that is ‘adi. Examples: shar‘i – affirming the obligation of the five prayers, negating the obligation of fasting Ashura; ‘aqli – affirming that ten is even, negating that ten is odd; ‘adi – affirming that fire burns, negating that water burns. A marginal note by Banani clarifies why the author mentions ‘adi before ‘aqli: because ‘adi shares with shar‘i the characteristic of being based on something external (revealed law or custom), while ‘aqli is independent of such attribution.

Key Terms
شرعي
عقلي
عادي
حكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that 'fire burns' is a rational judgment because it seems obvious.
Correction: Sanusi classifies it as customary because it is known through repeated experience, not pure reason alone.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, which type of judgment is 'fire burns'?
A) Religious
B) Rational
C) Customary
D) None of the above

Key Takeaway
Judgments are classified by their epistemic source: revelation for religious, reason for rational, and repeated experience for customary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hold a belief about the world, do you ever pause to ask whether it is based on revelation, reason, or mere custom? Which of these three takes the biggest place in your daily life?
?
Consider a belief you've never questioned—like that fire burns. How would your life change if you realized that even such 'obvious' facts are only customary and could be otherwise by divine power? Would your trust in God increase?
?
If you truly accepted that rational judgments are certain and customary judgments are only probable, would you be willing to let go of any comfortable assumptions that are not rationally or divinely grounded? What would be the hardest to give up?

Rational Judgments

The Division of Judgment into Shar‘ī, ‘Aqlī, and ‘Ādī
Sanusi defines ḥukm and enumerates its three types with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three types of judgment (ḥukm) in Islamic theology: religious, rational, and customary, and how they are distinguished by their source of knowledge.

Core Idea
Judgments are either from revelation, pure reason, or repeated experience.

Sanusi begins by defining what a judgment (ḥukm) is—any affirmation or negation of one thing concerning another—and immediately shows that judgments can be classified into three distinct types based on how we come to know them. This foundational classification underlies all later theological reasoning. Let's read his concise definitions and examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of ḥukm
Start by reading the opening sentence in Arabic and its translation. Notice that ḥukm is simply the act of affirming or negating something about something else.
This is a broad definition that covers all propositions.
2
Identify the three types
The passage divides ḥukm into three: sharʿī, ʿaqlī, and ʿādī. Read the explanation of their basis—whether the judgment comes from revelation, pure intellect, or repeated observation.
The order in the Arabic list is sharʿī, ʿādī, ʿaqlī, but later explained as sharʿī, ʿaqlī, ʿādī.
3
Examine the examples
For each type, Sanusi gives examples of both affirmation and negation. Pay attention to how each example fits its category.
The examples make the abstract definitions concrete.
4
Reflect on the basis of division
The key distinction is the source of knowledge: revelation for sharʿī, pure reason without need for repetition for ʿaqlī, and repeated experience for ʿādī. Consider how this classification is exhaustive.
This classification is used throughout kalam to categorize arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإثبات أمرٍ لأمرٍ أو نفيه عنه هو المُسمَّى حُكْماً ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق . يعني : انقسام الحكم إلى شرعي ، وعقلي ، وعادي . وَيَنْقَسِمُ إِلَىٰ ثَلَاثَةِ أَقْسَامٍ : (۱) شَرْعِيُّ ، وَعَادِيٌّ ، وَعَقْلِي . أنَّ الحكم الذي هو إثباتُ أمرٍ أو نفيه يتنوع إلى ثلاثةِ أقسام ؛ بمعنى : الأنواع وهي الثلاثةُ المذكورة ؛ لأنَّ الثبوت أو النفي اللذين في الحُكْم : إمَّا أَنْ يُسندا إلى الشرع بحيثُ لا يُمكنُ أن يُعلَما إلا منه ، أو لا ، والثاني إما أن يكتفي العقل في إدراكِهِ مِنْ غيرِ احتياج إلى تكرر واختبار ، أو لا . فالأولُ : الشرعي : كقولنا في الإثبات : الصلوات الخمس واجبةٌ ، وقولنا في النفْيِ : صوم يوم عاشوراء ليس بواجب . والثاني : العقلي : كقولنا في الإثباتِ : العشرة زوج ، وقولنا في النفي : السبعة ليست بزوج ، وقولنا في النفي أيضاً : الضدَّانِ لا يجتمعان . والثالث : العادي : كقولنا في الإثباتِ : شرابُ السَّكَنجَبين مسكن للصفراء ، وقولنا في النفي : الفطيرُ مِنَ الخبز ليس بسريع الانهضام .
So affirming something of something or negating it from it is what is called ḥukm (judgment), and with Allah Almighty is success. Meaning: The division of ḥukm into sharʿī (religious), ʿaqlī (rational), and ʿādī (customary). And it divides into three divisions: (1) sharʿī, ʿādī, and ʿaqlī. That ḥukm, which is affirming something or negating it, varies into three divisions; meaning, the types—and they are the three mentioned; because the affirmation or negation that are in the judgment: either they are attributed to the Sharīʿah such that they cannot be known except from it, or not; and the second either the intellect is sufficient in perceiving it without need for repetition and experiment, or not. So the first: sharʿī: like our saying in affirmation: the five prayers are obligatory, and our saying in negation: fasting the day of ʿĀshūrā is not obligatory. And the second: ʿaqlī: like our saying in affirmation: ten is even, and our saying in negation: seven is not even, and our saying also in negation: two opposites cannot coexist. And the third: ʿādī: like our saying in affirmation: drink of sakangabīn (oxymel) is calming for bile, and our saying in negation: flatbread is not quick to digest.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage. The author begins by defining ḥukm (judgment) as the affirmation or negation of one thing concerning another. He then announces that ḥukm divides into three types: religious (sharʿī), rational (ʿaqlī), and customary (ʿādī). He explains the basis of this division: in every judgment, the affirmation or negation is either traced back to revelation (so it cannot be known except from revelation), or it is not. If it is not from revelation, then either the intellect suffices to perceive it without repeated experience or testing (that is rational), or it depends on repeated observation and custom (that is customary). He then gives examples for each type. For sharʿī: affirmation that the five daily prayers are obligatory, negation that fasting on ʿĀshūrā is obligatory. For ʿaqlī: affirmation that ten is even, negation that seven is even, and negation that two opposites can coexist. For ʿādī: affirmation that drinking oxymel calms bile, negation that flatbread is quickly digested.

Key Terms
حكم
شرعي
عقلي
عادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that ʿādī judgments are the same as rational judgments or natural laws.
Correction: Sanusi distinguishes them: ʿaqlī judgments are known by pure reason without need for repetition, while ʿādī judgments depend on repeated observation and custom.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the basis for distinguishing a rational (ʿaqlī) judgment from a customary (ʿādī) judgment?
A) Rational judgments can be known without revelation, while customary judgments require revelation.
B) Rational judgments are known by the intellect alone without need for repetition, while customary judgments require repeated experience.
C) Rational judgments are always about numbers, while customary judgments are about medicine.
D) Rational judgments are always affirmative, while customary judgments are negative.

Key Takeaway
The three types of judgment—religious, rational, and customary—cover all possible affirmations and negations based on their source of knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you believe something to be true, do you ever pause to ask yourself: Did I learn this from revelation, reason, or custom? How would your life change if you examined each belief this way?
?
Have you ever accepted a 'customary' judgment as a certainty, forgetting that it is based on repeated experience rather than absolute truth? What would it mean for you to hold such judgments more lightly?
?
If the five daily prayers are a sharʿī judgment, what does that demand of you in terms of submission to revelation—even when your reason or habits might argue otherwise? Are you willing to obey without demanding proof from the other two sources?

Subdivisions of ʿĀdī: Verbal and Practical
Sanusi subdivides ʿādī into qawlī and fiʿlī with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi subdivides the customary (ʿādī) judgment into verbal (qawlī) and practical (fiʿlī) categories.

Core Idea
Customary judgments fall into two types: words and actions.

Sanusi has already introduced three types of rational judgment: sharʿī, ʿaqlī, and ʿādī. Now he refines the category of ʿādī by splitting it into two subcategories: those that are verbal (qawlī) and those that are practical (fiʿlī). This helps us classify habitual judgments more precisely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage where Sanusi divides ʿādī into qawlī and fiʿlī.
Note the examples given.
2
Identify the two subdivisions
Find 'عادي قولي' and 'عادي فعلي' in the text.
3
Understand the examples
The verbal subdivision includes grammatical rules; the practical includes the earlier examples of oxymel and flatbread.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم ينقسم هذا العادي إلى قسمين : عادي قولي : كرفع الفاعل ، ونصب المفعول ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ الأحكام اللغويَّةِ أو النحوية . وعادي فعلي : كالمثالين المذكورين . وكلُّ واحدٍ مِنْ هذه الأقسام الثلاثة - وهي الشرعي والعقلي و العادي - ينقسم إلى قسمين : ضروري ونظري . فالضروري : ما يدركُ ثبوتُهُ أو نفيه بلا تأمل . والنظري : ما لا يدرك عادةً إلا بالتأمل . فمثال الحكم الشرعي الضروري : حكمنا بأنَّ الصلاة واجبة ، والزنا محرَّم ، ونحو ذلك .
Then this customary (ʿādī) divides into two divisions: customary verbal (qawlī): like raising the subject, and accusative of the object, and similar grammatical or linguistic judgments. And customary practical (fiʿlī): like the two examples mentioned. And each one of these three divisions—which are sharʿī, ʿaqlī, and ʿādī—divides into two divisions: necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). The necessary: that whose affirmation or negation is perceived without reflection. And the theoretical: that which is not usually perceived except through reflection. So an example of the necessary sharʿī judgment: our judgment that prayer is obligatory and adultery is forbidden, and the like.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage continues the classification. First, the author subdivides ʿādī (customary) into two: 1) qawlī (verbal), which includes grammatical rules like raising the subject and accusative of the object; and 2) fiʿlī (practical), which are the examples given earlier (drink of oxymel calming bile, flatbread not digesting quickly). Then he applies a universal subdivision to all three types (sharʿī, ʿaqlī, ʿādī): each is divided into ḍarūrī (necessary, immediate) and naẓarī (theoretical, requiring reflection). Ḍarūrī is known without any deliberation, while naẓarī requires thought. He gives an example of a ḍarūrī sharʿī judgment: knowing that prayer is obligatory and adultery is forbidden—these are known without further reflection because they are clear from revelation.

Key Terms
عادي قولي
عادي فعلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Sharʿī and ʿaqlī
B) Qawlī and fiʿlī
C) Ḍarūrī and naẓarī
D) Positive and negative

Key Takeaway
The customary judgment is not monolithic; it includes both verbal and practical types.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

According to this lesson, even your habitual words fall under God's customary pattern (ʿāda). Does this change how you view your careless speech? Will you now treat your words as accountable habitual acts?
?

Is your daily speech as aligned with truth as your practical habits are with natural patterns? What verbal custom (like gossip or exaggeration) do you need to break, knowing it is not random but a divinely-ordered possibility?
?
Sanusi divides ‘ādī into verbal and practical. Which type do you unconsciously rely on more: the habit of expecting certain outcomes in your actions, or the habit of speaking in patterns you've learned? How might consciously acknowledging both as part of God's habit change your mindfulness in everyday life?

Rational Judgments
beginner

Universal Division: Necessary and Theoretical
Sanusi divides each of the three types into necessary and theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the universal division of all three rational judgments into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī).

Core Idea
Every rational judgment is either known immediately or only through reflection.

Having defined the three types of rational judgment (sharʿī, ʿaqlī, ʿādī), Sanusi now applies a further distinction that cuts across all three: each can be either necessary (ḍarūrī) or theoretical (naẓarī). This is a key epistemological tool in kalam, distinguishing between immediate knowledge and reasoned inference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic from 'وكل واحد من هذه الأقسام الثلاثة' to the end.
2
Define ḍarūrī and naẓarī
Notice Sanusi's definitions: ḍarūrī is known without reflection, naẓarī requires reflection.
3
Apply to religious examples
He gives an example of a necessary religious judgment: knowing that prayer is obligatory.
This shows that even religious rulings can be immediate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And customary practical (fiʿlī): like the two examples mentioned. The necessary: that whose affirmation or negation is perceived without reflection. And the theoretical: that which is not usually perceived except through reflection. So an example of the necessary sharʿī judgment: our judgment that prayer is obligatory and adultery is forbidden, and the like.

Commentary
This passage continues the classification. First, the author subdivides ʿādī (customary) into two: 1) qawlī (verbal), which includes grammatical rules like raising the subject and accusative of the object; and 2) fiʿlī (practical), which are the examples given earlier (drink of oxymel calming bile, flatbread not digesting quickly). Then he applies a universal subdivision to all three types (sharʿī, ʿaqlī, ʿādī): each is divided into ḍarūrī (necessary, immediate) and naẓarī (theoretical, requiring reflection). ضروري
نظري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Only rational judgments can be necessary (ḍarūrī).
Correction: Sanusi shows that religious judgments (sharʿī) can also be known immediately without reflection, as in the obligation of prayer.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a necessary (ḍarūrī) sharʿī judgment given by Sanusi?
A) The obligation of prayer
B) The prohibition of oxymel
C) Grammatical rules
D) The effect of flatbread

Key Takeaway
Both religious and rational judgments can be known either immediately or through reflection, and the necessary/theoretical division applies universally.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one religious belief you hold that you consider 'necessary'—you've never doubted it. Is your conviction genuinely rooted in certainty, or have you simply never examined it? If it were challenged, would your faith stand?
?
Are there teachings of Islam that you know require deep study (theoretical) but you've avoided because they're difficult? What is your excuse, and will you continue to neglect your responsibility to know them?
?
When you perform an act of worship like prayer, do you ever pause to reflect on why it is obligatory? If your knowledge remains at the 'necessary' level without deepening, are you truly worshiping with understanding, or just following habit?

Rational Judgments
beginner
6 min
The Three Types of Judgment and Their Division into Necessary and Theoretical
Sanusi classifies judgments into three types and divides each into necessary and theoretical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's classification of judgments into legal, rational, and customary, and how each is further divided into necessary and theoretical categories.

Core Idea
All judgments fall into three types and two categories of certainty.

Sanusi presents a foundational classification that organizes how we think about rulings and knowledge. He distinguishes three broad types of judgment and then shows that each type can be known either intuitively or through reflection. This structure is essential for understanding later theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of judgment
Read the passage and note the three types: legal (sharʿī), rational (ʿaqlī), and customary (ʿādī). The author begins by defining the third type, giving examples of affirmation and negation.
The first two types are covered earlier in the text; here Sanusi focuses on the customary.
2
See the subtypes of customary judgment
Notice that customary judgment is further divided into verbal customary (e.g., grammatical rules) and practical customary (e.g., medical examples). This shows the breadth of the category.
3
Grasp the necessary vs. theoretical division
Understand the definitions: necessary (ḍarūrī) is known without reflection, theoretical (naẓarī) requires reflection. Then examine the examples given for each combination of type and division.
This division applies to all three types, not just one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثالث : العادي : كقولنا في الإثباتِ : شرابُ السَّكَنجَبين مسكن للصفراء ، وقولنا في النفي : الفطيرُ مِنَ الخبز ليس بسريع الانهضام. ثم ينقسم هذا العادي إلى قسمين : عادي قولي : كرفع الفاعل ، ونصب المفعول ، ونحو ذلكَ مِنَ الأحكام اللغويَّةِ أو النحوية . وعادي فعلي : كالمثالين المذكورين. وكلُّ واحدٍ مِنْ هذه الأقسام الثلاثة - وهي الشرعي والعقلي و العادي - ينقسم إلى قسمين : ضروري ونظري . فالضروري : ما يدركُ ثبوتُهُ أو نفيه بلا تأمل . والنظري : ما لا يدرك عادةً إلا بالتأمل . فمثال الحكم الشرعي الضروري : حكمنا بأنَّ الصلاة واجبة ، والزنا محرَّم ، ونحو ذلكَ . و مثالُ الحكم الشرعي النظري : حكمنا بأنَّ اقتضاء الطعامِ مِنْ ثمن الطعام لا يجوز ، وأن الزعفران ليس بربوي . ومثال الحكم العقلي الضروري : حكمنا بأنَّ النفْي والثبوت لا يجتمعان . ومثال الحكم العقلي النظري : حكمنا بأنَّ الواحد ربعُ عشرِ الأربعين . ومثال الحكم العادي الضروري : حكمنا بأنَّ النار محرقة ، وأنَّ الثوبَ ساتر ، ونحو ذلك . ومثال الحكم العادي النظري : ما تقدَّمَ مِنْ مثالِ السَّكنجبين والخبز الفطير ، وأكثر أحكام أهل الطب عاديَّةٌ نظرية .
And the third: the customary, as our statement in affirmation: 'Drink of oxymel is a sedative for bile,' and our statement in negation: 'Unleavened bread from wheat is not quick to digest.' Then this customary is divided into two types: verbal customary, like raising the subject, and setting the object in the accusative, and similar grammatical or linguistic rulings; and practical customary, like the two examples mentioned. And each one of these three divisions – namely the legal, the rational, and the customary – is divided into two parts: necessary and theoretical. The necessary is that whose affirmation or negation is perceived without reflection. The theoretical is that which is not usually perceived except through reflection. An example of a necessary legal judgment: our judgment that prayer is obligatory and that fornication is forbidden, and so on. An example of a theoretical legal judgment: our judgment that demanding payment in kind from the price of food is not permissible, and that saffron is not a ribawi item. An example of a necessary rational judgment: our judgment that negation and affirmation cannot coexist. An example of a theoretical rational judgment: our judgment that one is a quarter of a tenth of forty. An example of a necessary customary judgment: our judgment that fire is burning, that a garment is concealing, and so on. An example of a theoretical customary judgment: that which has preceded from the example of oxymel and unleavened bread, and most judgments of medical practitioners are theoretical customary.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a classification of judgments into three types (legal, rational, customary) and then each into necessary and theoretical. The author first defines the third type, the customary judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī), giving examples: affirmative (oxymel soothes bile) and negative (unleavened bread digests slowly). Then he splits the customary into two subtypes: verbal customary (e.g., grammatical rules like raising the subject) and practical customary (the two examples just given). Next he states that each of the three types—legal, rational, customary—is further divided into necessary (ḍarūrī) and theoretical (naẓarī). He defines necessary as what is perceived without reflection, and theoretical as what is not usually perceived except through reflection. He then provides examples for each combination: necessary legal (prayer obligatory, fornication forbidden), theoretical legal (specific fiqh details), necessary rational (contradiction impossible), theoretical rational (arithmetic: one is a quarter of a tenth of forty), necessary customary (fire burns, clothes cover), theoretical customary (the earlier medical examples and most medical rulings). The examples illustrate how the same classification applies across the three domains, clarifying the scope of each term.

Key Terms
حكم شرعي
حكم عقلي
حكم عادي
ضروري
نظري
عادي قولي
عادي فعلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that customary judgments are not subject to the necessary/theoretical division.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that each of the three types—including customary—is divided into necessary and theoretical, and he gives examples for each.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes a necessary (ḍarūrī) judgment from a theoretical (naẓarī) judgment according to this passage?
A) Necessary judgments are always legal, while theoretical are rational.
B) Necessary judgments are perceived without reflection, while theoretical require reflection.
C) Necessary judgments are always affirmative, while theoretical are negative.
D) Theoretical judgments are only found in customary matters.

Key Takeaway
Sanusi classifies judgments into three types (legal, rational, customary) and each into necessary (known without reflection) and theoretical (known through reflection), a framework key to kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that prayer is obligatory — the lesson calls this a necessary legal judgment. But do you act on it with the same unreflective certainty that you act on 'fire burns'? If not, what does that say about the gap between your knowledge and your action? This week, before each prayer, pause and remind yourself: 'This is as certain as fire.'
?
Consider a belief you hold about your own character — for example, that you are patient or generous. Is it a necessary judgment (known without thought) or a theoretical one (based on reflection and perhaps limited evidence)? If it's theoretical, are you open to correction? How often do you test such judgments against your actual behavior?
?
The lesson divides judgments into necessary and theoretical. The Fire of the Hereafter is a necessary truth in Islam — yet do you live as if it is as immediately certain as the fire that burns? What one change in your daily routine would reflect that certainty?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
Examples of Necessary and Theoretical Judgments
Sanusi exemplifies the six types of judgments with concrete cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how to classify judgments as necessary or theoretical within the legal, rational, and conventional categories, using concrete examples from Sanusi.

Core Idea
Judgments are either self-evident (daruri) or require rational effort (nazari).

Sanusi has just defined the three types of judgment sources—shar‘i, ‘aqli, and ‘ādi. Now he grounds these abstract categories in memorable examples. By seeing how a specific ruling like 'fire burns' is a necessary conventional judgment, you will learn to recognise the pattern yourself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage once
Read the Arabic source and its translation to get an overview of the examples Sanusi provides.
Notice the pattern: each example is introduced with 'مثال الحكم...'.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage gives examples from shar‘i, ‘aqli, and ‘ādi. Underline which examples belong to which category.
3
Distinguish necessary from theoretical
Within each category, Sanusi gives one necessary (daruri) and one theoretical (nazari) example. Compare each pair. Why is the impossibility of two contradictories necessary but the mathematical calculation theoretical?
Necessary judgments are self-evident; theoretical ones require rational inference.
4
Synthesize the lesson
After understanding each example, try to come up with your own example for a missing type (e.g., a necessary shar‘i judgment).
Check if your example fits the pattern of being self-evident from the source.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و مثالُ الحكم الشرعي النظري : حكمنا بأنَّ اقتضاء الطعامِ مِنْ ثمن الطعام لا يجوز ، وأن الزعفران ليس بربوي (١) لأنه ليس بطعام أصلاً، وهذا عند السادة المالكية ومثال الحكم العقلي الضروري : حكمنا بأنَّ النفْي والثبوت لا يجتمعان ومثال الحكم العقلي النظري : حكمنا بأنَّ الواحد ربعُ عشرِ الأربعين . ومثال الحكم العادي الضروري : حكمنا بأنَّ النار محرقة ، وأنَّ الثوبَ ساتر ، ونحو ذلك ومثال الحكم العادي النظري : ما تقدَّمَ مِنْ مثالِ السَّكنجبين والخبز الفطير ، وأكثر أحكام أهل الطب عاديَّةٌ نظرية .
And an example of a theoretical legal ruling: our ruling that demanding payment for food from its price is not permissible, and that saffron is not ribawi (1) because it is not food at all, and this according to the Malikite masters. And an example of a necessary rational ruling: our ruling that negation and affirmation cannot coexist. And an example of a theoretical rational ruling: our ruling that the one is a quarter of a tenth of forty. And an example of a necessary conventional ruling: our ruling that fire burns, and that the garment covers, and similar. And an example of a theoretical conventional ruling: what has preceded from the example of vinegar-based oxymel and unleavened bread, and most rulings of physicians are theoretical conventional.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage provides examples of the four types of judgments classified along two axes: the source (legal, rational, conventional) and the manner of knowledge (necessary, theoretical). Sanusi first gives an example of a theoretical legal judgment: the ruling that demanding the price of food from the same food is not permissible, and that saffron is not ribawi because it is not a foodstuff according to the Malikis. He then gives a necessary rational judgment: the impossibility of two contradictories. Then he gives a theoretical rational judgment: a mathematical calculation. Then a necessary conventional judgment: fire burns, garments cover. Finally, a theoretical conventional judgment: examples from medicine such as the properties of oxymel and unleavened bread. These examples ground the abstract categories in concrete cases.

حكم عقلي
حكم عادي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Conventional (عادي) judgments are like scientific laws that are metaphysically necessary.
Correction: Conventional judgments refer to normal patterns of creation that are not metaphysically necessary; they are based on habitual observation, not logical necessity.

Checkpoint
Which example does Sanusi give for a necessary conventional judgment?
A) The impossibility of negation and affirmation coexisting
B) Fire burns
C) The ruling that saffron is not ribawi

Key Takeaway
The same judgment can be classified differently depending on its source (legal, rational, conventional) and whether it is known necessarily or through reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage says that the law of non-contradiction is a necessary rational judgment. Have you ever held conflicting beliefs about Allah (e.g., that He is both just and unjust)? What would it take for you to examine your own aqeedah for such contradictions?
?
Sanusi states that most medical judgments are theoretical conventional. When you rely on a doctor's prescription, do you recognize that it is based on patterns and not absolute certainty? How does this awareness affect your reliance on Allah's ultimate healing?
?
The example of a theoretical legal ruling relates to a specific Malikite opinion. When you encounter a ruling that seems difficult, do you trust that the scholars derived it through thorough reasoning (nazari), or do you dismiss it? What does your reaction reveal about your commitment to seeking knowledge?

Rational Judgments
beginner
5 min
The Definition of Customary Judgment and Its Conditions
Sanusi defines customary judgment and explains its two qualifying conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of customary judgment (al-hukm al-'adi) and why the conditions of possibility of non-occurrence and lack of influence are crucial for distinguishing it from rational and causal connections.

Core Idea
Customary judgments are not necessary or causal—they are based on repeated observation and can be broken by divine power.

This lesson introduces one of the key concepts of Islamic theology: the customary judgment. Imam al-Sanusi defines it and clarifies two essential qualifications that protect against a dangerous misunderstanding that could lead to denying miracles and the afterlife. Understanding this definition is foundational for distinguishing between what is rationally necessary, what is legally required, and what is merely habitual.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once. Focus on the three components: affirmation of a connection between two things, by means of repetition, and with the two conditions: possibility of non-occurrence and lack of influence.
Notice that the phrase 'in existence or non-existence' applies to both matters, covering four combinations.
2
Identify what is excluded
Sanusi explicitly excludes rational connections (e.g., knowledge makes a subject knowing) and legal connections (e.g., sunset obligates noon prayer). These do not depend on repetition.
This shows that customary judgment is distinct from logical necessity and divine command.
3
Understand the purpose of the two conditions
The conditions 'possibility of non-occurrence' and 'lack of influence' are not part of the real definition. They are added to correct the mistaken belief that customary connections are either necessary (like rational) or causal (one affecting the other).
The author warns that this misunderstanding led some to deny resurrection, life in the grave, and eternal punishment because these break the usual pattern.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ الْعَادِيُّ : فَهُوَ عِبَارَةٌ عَنْ إِثْبَاتِ الرَّبْطِ بَيْنَ أَمْرِ وَأَمْرٍ وُجُوداً أَوْ عَدَماً بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكَرُرِ ، مَعَ صِحَّةِ التَّخَلْفِ وَعَدَمِ تَأْثِيرِ أَحَدِهِمَا فِي الْآخَرِ الْبَتَّةَ .
يَعْنِي : أَنَّ الْحُكْمَ الْعَادِيَّ : هُوَ إِثْبَاتُ الرَّبْطِ بَيْنَ وُجُودِ أَمْرٍ أَوْ عَدَمِهِ ، وَبَيْنَ وُجُودِ أَمْرٍ آخَرَ أَوْ عَدَمِهِ . فَقَوْلَنَا : ( وُجُوداً أَوْ عَدَماً ) رَاجِعٌ لِكُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنَ الْأَمْرَيْنِ ، لَا لِأَحَدِهِمَا فَقَطْ ؛ إِذْ لَوْ كَانَ كَذَلِكَ لَمَا دَخَلَ تَحْتَ هَذَا الْكَلَامِ جَمِيعُ الْأَقْسَامِ الْأَرْبَعَةِ الْآتِيَةِ .
وَاحْتَرَزَ بِقَوْلِهِ : ( بِوَاسِطَةِ التَّكَرُرِ ) مِنَ الرَّبْطِ بَيْنَ أَمْرَيْنِ عَقْلاً أَوْ شَرْعاً ؛ كَالرَّبْطِ الْعَقْلِيِّ بَيْنَ قِيَامِ الْعِلْمِ بِمَحَلٍّ وَبَيْنَ كَوْنِ ذَلِكَ الْمَحَلِّ عَالِماً ، وَكَالرَّبْطِ الشَّرْعِيِّ الَّذِي بَيْنَ زَوَالِ الشَّمْسِ وَوُجُوبِ صَلَاةِ الظُّهْرِ مَثَلاً ، فَهَذَانِ الرَّبْطَانِ لَا يُسَمَّى وَاحِدٌ مِنْهُمَا عَادِيّاً ؛ لِعَدَمِ تَوَقُّفِهِ عَلَى تَكَرُّرٍ .
وَأَمَّا قَوْلُنَا : ( مَعَ صِحَّةِ التَّخَلُّفِ ، وَعَدَمِ تَأْثِيرِ أَحَدِهِمَا فِي الْآخَرِ الْبَتَّةَ ) فَلَمْ نَذْكُرْهُ لِبَيَانِ حَقِيقَةِ الْحُكْمِ الْعَادِيِّ ، بَلْ لِلتَّنْبِيهِ عَلَى تَحْقِيقِ عِلْمٍ وَدَفْعِ جَهَالَةٍ ابْتُلِيَ بِهَا الْأَكْثَرُ فِي الْأَحْكَامِ الْعَادِيَّةِ ؛ حَتَّى تَوَهَّمُوا أَنَّهُ لَا مَعْنَى لِلرَّبْطِ الَّذِي حَصَلَ فِي الْحُكْمِ الْعَادِيِّ إِلَّا رَبْطُ اللُّزُومِ الَّذِي لَا يُمْكِنُ مَعَهُ انْفِكَاكٌ كَاللُّزُومِ الْعَقْلِيِّ ، أَوْ رَبْطُ التَّأْثِيرِ مِنْ أَحَدِهِمَا فِي الْآخَرِ ، فَنَبَّهْنَا بِهَذِهِ الْجُمْلَةِ عَلَى أَنَّ الرَّبْطَ الَّذِي حَصَلَ فِي الْحُكْمِ الْعَادِيِّ إِنَّمَا هُوَ رَبْطُ اقْتِرَانٍ وَدَلَالَةٍ جُعْلِيَّةٍ ، لَا رَبْطَ لُزُومٍ عَقْلِيٍّ وَلَا رَبْطَ تَأْثِيرٍ مِنْ أَحَدِهِمَا فِي الْآخَرِ . فَأَشَرْنَا إِلَى عَدَمِ الرَّبْطِ فِيهِ بِطَرِيقِ اللُّزُومِ الَّذِي يُشْبِهُ اللُّزُومَ الْعَقْلِيَّ بِقَوْلِنَا : ( مَعَ صِحَّةِ التَّخَلُّفِ ) وَفِيهِ تَنْبِيَةٌ عَلَى جَهَالَةِ مَنْ فَهِمَ أَنَّ الرَّبْطَ فِي الْعَادِيَّاتِ بِطَرِيقِ اللُّزُومِ الَّذِي لَا يَصِحُّ مَعَهُ التَّخَلُّفُ ، فَأَنْكَرَ بِسَبَبِ هَذِهِ الْجَهَالَةِ الْبَعْثَ ، وَإِحْيَاءَ الْمَوْتَى فِي الْقَبْرِ ، وَالْخُلُودَ فِي النَّارِ مَعَ اسْتِمْرَارِ الْحَيَاةِ ؛ لِأَنَّ ذَلِكَ كُلَّهُ عِنْدَهُمْ عَلَى خِلَافِ الْعَادَةِ الْمُسْتَمِرَّةِ فِي الشَّاهِدِ ، وَالرَّبْطُ الْمُتَقَرِّرُ فِيهَا لَا يَصِحُّ فِيهِ التَّخَلُّفُ عِنْدَهُمْ . وَأَشَرْنَا إِلَى عَدَمِ الرَّبْطِ فِيهِ بِطَرِيقِ التَّأْثِيرِ بِقَوْلِنَا : ( وَعَدَمِ تَأْثِيرِ أَحَدِهِمَا فِي الْآخَرِ الْبَتَّةَ ) .
وَقَدْ يُقَالُ : إِنَّ ذِكْرَ هَذَيْنِ الْقَيْدَيْنِ فِي تَعْرِيفِنَا الْحُكْمَ الْعَادِيَّ إِنَّمَا ...
As for the customary judgment: it is an expression of affirming the connection between a thing and a thing, in existence or non-existence, by means of repetition, together with the possibility of non-occurrence and the absolute lack of influence of one of them upon the other. That is: the customary judgment is the affirmation of the connection between the existence or non-existence of a thing and the existence or non-existence of another thing. Our statement '(in existence or non-existence)' refers to each of the two matters, not to only one of them; for if it were otherwise, not all of the following four divisions would fall under this discussion. And he guarded by his statement 'by means of repetition' against the connection between two matters rationally or legally; like the rational connection between the presence of knowledge in a subject and that subject being knowing, and like the legal connection between the disappearance of the sun and the obligation of the noon prayer, for example; these two connections are not called 'customary' because they do not depend on repetition. As for our statement 'together with the possibility of non-occurrence, and the absolute lack of influence of one of them upon the other': we did not mention it to clarify the reality of the customary judgment, but rather to draw attention to the verification of knowledge and to refute an ignorance that afflicts most people concerning customary judgments; so much so that they imagined that there is no meaning for the connection that occurs in the customary judgment except a connection of necessity that cannot be separated from it, like rational necessity, or a connection of influence from one of them upon the other. So we alerted by this sentence that the connection that occurs in the customary judgment is but a connection of accompaniment and instituted indication, not a connection of rational necessity nor a connection of influence from one of them upon the other. So we pointed to the absence of connection in it by way of necessity that resembles rational necessity by our statement 'with the possibility of non-occurrence', and in it is a warning against the ignorance of one who understood that the connection in customary matters is by way of necessity such that non-occurrence is impossible with it, so he denied, because of this ignorance, the resurrection, the reviving of the dead in the grave, and everlastingness in the Fire with the continuation of life; because all that, in their view, contradicts the continuous habit in the witnessed world, and the connection established therein does not admit non-occurrence, in their view. And we pointed to the absence of connection in it by way of influence by our statement 'and the absolute lack of influence of one of them upon the other.' And it may be said: 'The mention of these two conditions in our definition of the customary judgment is only...'
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage defines the customary judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī) and explains its two crucial qualifications. The author begins by stating the definition: it is affirming a connection between two things (in existence or non-existence) by means of repetition, but with two conditions: (1) the possibility of non-occurrence (ṣiḥḥat al-takhalluf) and (2) the absence of causal influence (taʾthīr). He then elaborates that the phrase 'in existence or non-existence' (wujūdan aw ʿadaman) applies to both of the two matters, not just one, so that all four possible combinations (both existent, both non-existent, one existent and the other non-existent, etc.) are included. He explains that the condition 'by means of repetition' (bi-wāsiṭat al-takarur) excludes rational connections (like knowledge necessarily making the subject knowledgeable) and religious/legal connections (like the sun's setting obligating noon prayer), because those do not depend on repeated observation. Then he clarifies that the two remaining conditions (possibility of non-occurrence and lack of influence) are not part of the real definition, but are added to correct a widespread misunderstanding: people mistakenly think that the customary connection is either a necessary logical connection (as in rational necessity) or a causal connection (one thing influencing the other). By adding 'with the possibility of non-occurrence', he shows that the connection is not a necessary one; by adding 'and the absolute lack of influence', he shows it is not a causal relation. He gives an example of the dangerous consequence of the misunderstanding: some people deny bodily resurrection, life in the grave, and eternal punishment in Hellfire because these contradict the usual course of habit they observe, and they mistakenly think habit is necessary and cannot be broken. The passage ends with the beginning of an objection: someone might say that these two conditions are part of the definition itself. The answer to this objection appears in the following material not included in this page window.

Key Terms
الحكم العادي
الربط
التكرر
صحة التخلف
عدم التأثير
ربط الاقتران
ربط الدلالة الجعلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The connection in customary judgments is either a necessary logical relation (like rational necessity) or a causal relation (one thing influencing another).
Correction: In reality, the customary connection is merely a connection of accompaniment and instituted indication (rabṭ iqtirān wa dalāla juʿliyya), not necessary or causal. It allows for the possibility of non-occurrence and lacks causal influence.

Checkpoint
What is the primary purpose of adding the conditions 'possibility of non-occurrence' and 'lack of influence' to the definition of customary judgment?
A) To complete the real definition of customary judgment
B) To distinguish customary judgment from rational and religious connections
C) To correct the mistaken belief that the customary connection is either necessary or causal
D) To show that repetition is essential for all types of connections

Key Takeaway
Customary judgments are based on repetition, not necessity or causality, and can be superseded by divine power—a key premise for accepting miracles and the afterlife.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that the way things normally happen (like the sun rising or fire burning) is a fixed law that God cannot or will not break? What does this lesson say that assumption reveals about your understanding of Allah's power?
?
Think of a habit in your daily life—something you rely on without thinking. Would your trust in Allah be shaken if that pattern suddenly ceased? Are you ready to say 'I believe it can be otherwise' simply because He wills it?
?
This passage warns that misunderstanding customary judgments can lead to denying the resurrection and the afterlife. Have you truly internalized that your daily experience is not a limit on what Allah can do? What will you do today to remind yourself of His absolute power over every habit?
