The Connection in Customary Judgment: Association, Not Necessity
Sanusi explains the nature of the connection in 'adi judgment and introduces the two restrictions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the connection in customary judgments is one of conventional association, not logical necessity or causal influence, and why Sanusi includes two restrictions to clarify this.

Core Idea
Customary connections are not necessary; they are conventional signs that God may break.

Sanusi begins by explaining that the two restrictions in the definition of customary judgment are not merely for defining it, but to correct a widespread misunderstanding. Many people wrongly think that the connection in customary events is either logically necessary or causally effective, leading them to deny religious truths that contradict observed habits. This lesson unpacks the true nature of the 'adi connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi structures his correction.
The passage is a single argument, not a list.
2
Identify the two mistaken views
People thought the connection in customary judgments must be either logical necessity (luzum 'aqli) or causal influence (ta'thir). Find where Sanusi mentions these.
3
Understand Sanusi's correction
Sanusi says the connection is only association (iqtiran) and conventional indication (dalala ja'liyya). The two restrictions – possibility of disconnection and no influence – drive this home.
These restrictions prevent misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ألبتة ) فلم نذكره لبيان حقيقةِ الحكم العادي (١) ، بل للتنبيه على تحقيقِ علم ودفع جهالة ابتُلي بها الأكثرُ في الأحكام العادية ; حتى توهموا أنَّه و لا معنى للربط الذي حصل في الحكم العادي إلا ربط اللزوم الذي لا يمكنُ معَهُ انفكاةٌ ; كاللزومِ العقليّ ، أو ربط التأثيرِ مِنْ أحدهما في الآخر ، فنبَّهنا بهذهِ الجملة على أنَّ الربط الذي حصل في الحكم العادي إنَّما هو ربط اقتران ودلالة جعليَّةٌ ، لا ربط لزوم عقلي ، ولا ربط تأثيرِ مِنْ أحدهما في الآخرِ فأشرنا إلى عدم الربط فيهِ بطريقِ اللزوم الذي يشبه اللزوم العقليَّ بقولنا : ( معَ صحَّةِ التخلَّفِ ) وفيه تنبية على جهالةِ مَنْ فهمَ أَنَّ الربط في العاديات بطريق اللزوم الذي لا يصح معَهُ التخلُّفُ ، فأنكر بسببِ هذه الجهالة البعثَ ، وإحياء الموتى في القبر ، والخلود في النارِ معَ استمرار الحياةِ ; لأنَّ ذلكَ كلَّهُ عندَهم على خلاف العادة المستمرة في الشاهدِ ، والربطُ المتقرّرُ فيها لا يصح فيه التخلُّفُ عندَهم ! وأشرنا إلى عدمِ الربط فيه بطريقِ التأثير بقولنا : ( وعدم تأثير أحدهما في الآخر ألبتة ((٢) . وقد يُقالُ : إِنَّ ذكر هذين القيدين في تعريفنا الحكم العادي إنَّما هو لإفادة معرفتِهِ ; بناءً على أن الجهلَ بصفة حقيقة وإثبات ضدها أنَّ لتلك الحقيقة .. مُوجِبٌ للجهل بها ، وهو مذهب أبي عمران الفاسي رضي الله تعالى عنه في المسألة المشهورة بالخلافِ ; وهي الجهلُ بصفاتِ المولى تبارك وتعالى وإثباتُ ضدّها لهُ ممَّا لا يليقُ بهِ جلَّ وعلا ; كإثباتِ الجسمية له والجهة ونحو ذلك مما هو هو مستحيل عليه تبارك وتعالى .. هل يصدقُ على مُعتقِدِ ذلكَ أَنَّهُ جاهل بالمولى تبارك وتعالى أم لا ؟(۱)
Absolutely) We did not mention it to explain the reality of the customary judgment, but rather to draw attention to the realization of knowledge and to repel ignorance with which most people were afflicted regarding customary judgments, so much so that they imagined that there is no meaning for the connection that occurs in the customary judgment except a connection of necessity that cannot be separated, like logical necessity, or a connection of influence of one on the other. So we alerted by this sentence that the connection that occurs in the customary judgment is only a connection of association and a conventional indication, not a connection of logical necessity, nor a connection of influence of one on the other. So we pointed to the absence of connection in it by way of necessity that resembles logical necessity by our statement: 'with the possibility of disconnection.' And in it is a warning about the ignorance of those who understood that the connection in customary matters is by way of necessity that cannot be separated, and due to this ignorance they denied resurrection, reviving the dead in the grave, and eternity in the Fire while life continues, because all of this, according to them, is contrary to the continuous custom in this world, and the connection established in it cannot be separated according to them! And we pointed to the absence of connection in it by way of influence by our statement: 'and no influence of one on the other absolutely.' And it might be said: The mention of these two restrictions in our definition of the customary judgment is only to provide knowledge of it, based on the fact that ignorance of the true nature of a thing and affirming its opposite necessitates ignorance of that thing, and this is the school of Abu 'Imran al-Fasi, may Allah be pleased with him, in the famous issue of controversy, which is: ignorance of the attributes of the Lord, blessed and exalted, and affirming their opposite for Him, which is not befitting for Him, may He be glorified and exalted, such as affirming corporeality for Him, direction, and the like of what is impossible for Him, blessed and exalted. Does the one who believes that count as ignorant of the Lord, blessed and exalted, or not?
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is an explanation and justification of the two restrictions included in the definition of 'adi (customary) judgment. Sanusi begins by clarifying that he did not mention these restrictions merely to explain the reality of 'adi judgment, but rather to draw attention to a specific knowledge and to refute a widespread ignorance. Many people mistakenly thought that the connection in a customary judgment must be either a necessary logical connection (luzum 'aqli) that cannot be separated, or a causal influence (ta'thir) of one thing on another. Sanusi corrects this by stating that the connection is only one of association (iqtiran) and conventional indication (dalala ja'liyya), not logical necessity or causal efficacy. He then elaborates on the first restriction: he pointed to the absence of necessary connection by the phrase 'with the possibility of disconnection' (ma'a sihhat al-takhalluf). This warns against those who think the connection in customary matters is necessary and unbreakable, leading them to deny religious truths like resurrection and eternal life in the Fire, since these contradict observed custom. Sanusi emphasizes that the connection in customary events can be broken by God. The second restriction is signaled by 'and no influence of one on the other absolutely' (wa 'adam ta'thir ahadihima fi al-akhar al-batta), indicating that neither thing has causal power over the other. After explaining these restrictions, Sanusi anticipates a potential question: why mention them? He answers by citing the view of Abu 'Imran al-Fasi, who held that ignorance of the true nature of a thing, combined with affirming its opposite, constitutes ignorance of the thing itself. Thus, to avoid ignorance about 'adi judgment, its true nature must be clarified. Sanusi then raises the famous controversial issue: if someone attributes impossibilities to God (e.g., corporeality, direction), does that person count as ignorant of God? The passage ends with this rhetorical question, implying that the answer is yes, and therefore the restrictions are necessary to prevent ignorance about the nature of 'adi judgment.

Key Terms
الحكم العادي
الربط
اللزوم العقلي
التأثير
الاقتران
الدلالة الجعلية
صحَّة التخلَّف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Customary connections are like logical or causal necessity.
Correction: They are only conventional associations that can be broken by God.

Checkpoint
What type of connection does the 'adi (customary) judgment involve according to Sanusi?
A) Logical necessity (luzum 'aqli)
B) Causal influence (ta'thir)
C) Association (iqtiran) and conventional indication (dalala ja'liyya)
D) Both A and B

Key Takeaway
The 'adi connection is a conventional indication, not a necessary or causal one, so it can be broken by God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever assumed that the way things normally work in this world is fixed and cannot be changed by God? How does Sanusi's explanation of customary judgments challenge that assumption, and what change do you need to make in your reliance on God versus on habitual patterns?
?
Do you truly believe that nothing in creation has any independent causal power, or do you sometimes act as if removing a cause removes the effect? When you fall sick, do you think the medicine heals you, or do you recognize God as the sole healer? How will you remind yourself of the second restriction ('no influence') today?
?
The people Sanusi criticizes denied resurrection because it seemed contrary to custom. Is there any aspect of your belief that you find hard to accept because it seems to contradict your daily experience? How will you actively combat that doubt using Sanusi's point about the possibility of disconnection?

Why the Restrictions Matter: Refuting Ignorance
Sanusi provides the rationale for including the two restrictions, citing Abu 'Imran al-Fasi.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale for including the two restrictions in the definition of customary judgment: to prevent ignorance about the nature of the judgment, analogous to ignorance about God's attributes.

Core Idea
The restrictions prevent ignorance by clarifying the true nature of customary judgment.

After explaining the two restrictions, Sanusi anticipates a question: why mention them at all? He answers by citing the principle of Abu 'Imran al-Fasi, that ignorance of a thing's true reality, combined with affirming its opposite, constitutes ignorance of the thing itself. This lesson explores that rationale and its theological parallel.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the rhetorical question and the reference to Abu 'Imran al-Fasi.
This is a brief but dense passage.
2
Identify the purpose of the restrictions
Sanusi says the restrictions are to provide knowledge of the judgment, based on the idea that ignorance of its true nature leads to ignorance of the thing itself.
3
Understand the analogy
The parallel is drawn to the issue of ignorance of God's attributes: if someone affirms an impossible attribute (like corporeality), are they ignorant of God? The implication is yes, so defining 'adi judgment with restrictions prevents similar ignorance.
This connects theology and methodology.

Ignorance of God's Attributes is Ignorance of God
Sanusi states the principle that ignorance of an attribute is ignorance of the described, and applies it to divine attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why ignorance of any attribute of Allah constitutes ignorance of Allah Himself and leads to disbelief, based on the principle established by Abu Imran al-Fasi.

Core Idea
Ignorance of one of Allah's attributes is ignorance of Allah.

This lesson introduces a fundamental principle in Islamic theology: that knowing Allah requires knowing His attributes. If someone mistakenly denies or wrongly attributes something to Allah, they are not just mistaken about that attribute—they are ignorant of Allah Himself. This principle, championed by the scholar Abu Imran al-Fasi, has serious implications. Let's see how Sanusi presents it.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own words come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi references a well-known dispute.
2
Identify the key claim
The passage states that ignorance of a true attribute and affirmation of its opposite implies ignorance of the described. Pinpoint this statement.
This is the central principle.
3
Understand the dispute
The dispute is about whether someone who attributes impossible qualities to Allah (like corporeality) is considered ignorant of Allah. Identify the two sides.
Abu Imran al-Fasi's view is mentioned.
4
Learn the ruling
The stronger view is that such a person is indeed ignorant of Allah, and that ignorance of Allah is disbelief (kufr). Process the gravity of this.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هو لإفادة معرفتِهِ ؛ بناءً على أن الجهلَ بصفة حقيقة وإثبات ضدها أنَّ لتلك الحقيقة .. مُوجِبٌ للجهل بها ، وهو مذهب أبي عمران الفاسي رضي الله تعالى عنه في المسألة المشهورة بالخلافِ ؛ وهي الجهلُ بصفاتِ المولى تبارك وتعالى وإثباتُ ضدّها لهُ ممَّا لا يليقُ بهِ جلَّ وعلا ؛ كإثباتِ الجسمية له والجهة ونحو ذلك مما هو هو مستحيل عليه تبارك وتعالى .. هل يصدقُ على مُعتقِدِ ذلكَ أَنَّهُ جاهل بالمولى تبارك وتعالى أم لا ؟ والأظهر : أَنَّهُ جاهل بهِ جلَّ وعلا كما اختار أبو عمران رحمهُ اللهُ تعالى ، والجهل به تعالى كفر ، فعلى هذا : مَنْ جهلَ صفة الحكم العادي بأنَّهُ ربط اقتران جعليّ يصح فيهِ التخلُّفُ ، واعتقد لجهلِهِ أنَّ الربط فيه ربط تأثير أو ربط لزوم لا يمكنُ فِيهِ التخلُّفُ .. فإنَّهُ يصدقُ عليهِ أنَّه جاهل بالحكم العادي بناءً على هذا القولِ الأظهرِ ؛ أنَّ الجهل بالصفة جهل بالموصوف ، وإسقاط هذين القيدين إذاً مِنْ تعريف الحكم العادي .. قد يُخِلُّ بمعرفتِهِ ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
It is for the purpose of attaining knowledge of Him; based on the fact that ignorance of a true attribute and affirmation of its opposite... a cause for ignorance of it, and it is the doctrine of Abu Imran al-Fasi, may Allah be pleased with him, in the well-known disputed issue: which is ignorance of the attributes of the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, and affirmation of what is not befitting to Him, Mighty and Majestic, such as attributing corporeality to Him and direction and the like of what is impossible for Him, Blessed and Exalted — does the one who believes that truly apply to him that he is ignorant of the Lord, Blessed and Exalted, or not? And the more apparent [view] is that he is ignorant of Him, Mighty and Majestic, as Abu Imran, may Allah have mercy on him, chose, and ignorance of Him, Exalted, is disbelief. Based on this: whoever is ignorant of the attribute of the regular judgment (al-hukm al-ʿādī) — that it is a connection of conventional conjunction in which disjunction is possible, and believes out of his ignorance that the connection is a connection of influence or a connection of necessary entailment in which disjunction is not possible — then it applies to him that he is ignorant of the regular judgment, based on this more apparent view: that ignorance of the attribute is ignorance of the described. Dropping these two restrictions from the definition of the regular judgment may then impair knowledge of it. And with Allah is the success.
Source pages p. 132

Commentary
This passage is a theological ruling on the relationship between ignorance of an attribute and ignorance of the one described. It begins by stating the purpose of the discussion and then references a famous dispute led by Abu Imran al-Fasi: whether someone who attributes to God what is impossible (like corporeality or direction) is considered ignorant of God. The author then presents the stronger opinion (al-azhar) that such a person is indeed ignorant of God, and that ignorance of God is kufr (disbelief). He then applies this principle to the concept of the 'regular judgment' (al-hukm al-ʿādī), which is the customary connection between cause and effect established by God. Whoever is ignorant of the true nature of this judgment — that it is a conventional conjunction where disjunction is possible — and instead believes it is a necessary or causal connection, is also ignorant of the attribute of the regular judgment. This is because ignorance of the attribute is ignorance of the described (i.e., the attribute itself). Consequently, dropping the two key restrictions (conventionality and possibility of disjunction) from the definition of the regular judgment impairs one's knowledge of it. The passage ends with a prayer.

Key Terms
الجهل بالصفة
الجهل بالموصوف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that making mistakes about God's attributes does not affect one's knowledge of God.
Correction: The passage affirms that ignorance of any attribute of God entails ignorance of God, and that is disbelief.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on someone who attributes corporeality to Allah?
A) He is still a believer.
B) He is ignorant of Allah and a disbeliever.
C) He is sinful but not a disbeliever.
D) It is a minor mistake.

Key Takeaway
Ignorance of any true attribute of Allah is ignorance of Allah, and that ignorance constitutes disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever casually thought of Allah in terms borrowed from creation, even for a moment? If so, what does this lesson demand you to do right now to purify your belief?
?
If ignorance of one attribute of Allah is ignorance of Allah Himself, how seriously have you taken learning His Names and Attributes? Will you commit today to studying one new attribute deeply and reflecting on its implications for your life?
?
This lesson declares that believing impossible attributes about Allah is kufr. Search your heart: is there any subtle anthropomorphism you hold about Allah? What concrete step will you take to correct it and draw closer to true knowledge of Him?

The Regular Judgment and Its Correct Understanding
Sanusi defines the regular judgment and shows that ignorance of its correct nature is ignorance of the attribute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the correct definition of the regular judgment (al-hukm al-ādī) and why misunderstanding it leads to ignorance of this divine attribute.

Core Idea
The regular judgment is a conventional connection where disjunction is possible.

This lesson applies the principle we learned earlier—ignorance of an attribute is ignorance of the described—to a specific attribute: the regular judgment. Sanusi defines this attribute and shows how a mistaken understanding of it results in ignorance of that attribute, which by the same principle is a serious matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the definition of the regular judgment.
2
Identify the two key restrictions
The passage defines the regular judgment as a conventional conjunction (ربط اقتران جعلي) where disjunction (التخلُّف) is possible. Find these two elements.
These are the defining features.
3
Contrast with wrong views
The passage mentions two wrong understandings: a connection of influence (ربط تأثير) and a connection of necessary entailment (ربط لزوم), where disjunction is impossible.
These are the misconceptions to avoid.
4
Understand the consequence
Dropping the two restrictions from the definition impairs knowledge of the regular judgment. This is because ignorance of the attribute is ignorance of the described.
Connects back to the principle from lesson 1.

Source Text

The Certainty of Regular Knowledge and Its Rational Possibility
Sanusi clarifies the certainty of regular knowledge and rational possibility of its opposite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why knowledge of cause-effect relationships is certain yet its opposite is rationally possible.

Core Idea
Regular knowledge is certain, but reason allows its opposite.

Sanusi presents an important note clarifying the epistemological status of our everyday cause-effect knowledge. While we are certain that fire burns and bread nourishes, reason does not deem it impossible for the opposite to occur. This passage explains how certainty and rational possibility coexist.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get the author's direct statement.
2
Identify the two claims
Notice the two-part statement: (1) knowledge of the regular relationship is necessary and certain; (2) when disjunction occurs, we do not prevent it rationally.
The second part does not negate the first.
3
Understand the citation
Read the quote from al-Farahari, which reinforces that regular knowledge is certain and necessary, yet reason judges its opposite as not impossible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تنبيه مهم : العلاقة العادية بين السبب والمسبَّب العلم بها ضروري ، لا يجوز الشكُ فيها ، ولكن عند وقوع الانفكاك لا نمنع منه عقلاً ، ولهذا قال العلامة الفرهاري في ( النبراس ) ( ص ٥٨١ ) : ( قال المحققون : العلم العادي علم يقيني ضروري ، جرت عادة الله بخلقه في العاقل مع حكم العقل بأن نقيضه غير محال ) ، فتجويز المنافي لا يدفع العلم اليقيني
Important note: The regular relationship between cause and effect: knowledge of it is necessary, it is not permissible to doubt it, but when disjunction occurs, we do not prevent it rationally. And for this reason, the scholar al-Farahari said in al-Nibraas (p. 581): 'The scholars of verification said: Regular knowledge is certain necessary knowledge, God's custom with His creation continues in the rational [person] with the ruling of reason that its opposite is not impossible.' So, permitting the contradictory does not remove certain knowledge.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an important note (tanbih muhim) clarifying the epistemological status of the 'regular judgment' (al-hukm al-ʿādī). The author affirms that our knowledge of the regular cause-effect relationship is necessary and certain; it is not permissible to doubt it. However, if a disjunction (infikāk) occurs — i.e., a cause appears without its usual effect or vice versa — we do not rationally consider it impossible. The author cites al-Farahari, who quotes the scholars of verification (al-muḥaqqiqūn) stating that this type of knowledge (al-ʿilm al-ʿādī) is certain and necessary, based on God's custom, but reason nevertheless judges that its opposite (the disjunction) is not impossible. Therefore, allowing the possibility of the opposite does not negate the certainty of that knowledge.

Key Terms
العلاقة العادية
السبب والمسبَّب
العلم العادي
الانفكاك
تجويز المنافي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might mistakenly believe that because the regular relationship is certain, its opposite must be impossible.
Correction: The note clarifies that it is rationally possible even if it never actually occurs, because God's custom is not a rational necessity.

Checkpoint
According to the note, what is the nature of our knowledge of the regular relationship between cause and effect?
A) It is probable
B) It is certain and necessary
C) It is merely customary
D) It is doubtful

Key Takeaway
Regular knowledge is certain and necessary, yet it remains rationally possible for the opposite to occur, because God's custom is not a rational necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if the regular patterns in your life are guaranteed by nature, or do you recognize they are maintained by God's custom and could be broken at any moment? How would your daily reliance on cause and effect change if you truly believed that God could disjoin them at any second?
?
This lesson says it is not permissible to doubt the cause-effect relationship. Is there any area of your life where you doubt God's custom? How does that doubt affect your trust in Allah's provision?
?
If God's custom is certain but its opposite is possible, what does that teach you about the nature of tawakkul? Are you living with the certainty of means but the possibility of their failure? How does that shape your heart's attachment to outcomes?

The Four Divisions of Customary Judgment
Enumerates four types and their logical derivation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four types of customary connection and the logical reasoning that yields exactly these four divisions.

Core Idea
The four customary connections arise from the interplay of existence and non-existence.

Sanusi begins by introducing the four divisions of the customary judgment. These are not arbitrary; they follow from the simple fact that any two things can each be considered as existing or not existing. By multiplying these possibilities, we get exactly four types of connection. In this lesson, we will see the four types with clear examples and understand why there are four.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the four examples given for each type of connection.
The examples involve satiety, eating, and hunger.
2
Identify the logical derivation
Notice that the author explains why there are exactly four divisions by multiplying the two possibilities (existence and non-existence) of each of the two related things.
This is a combinatorial argument.
3
Reflect on the importance
Ask yourself why this classification matters for kalam. It provides a precise vocabulary for discussing customary relationships.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَقْسَامُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ : رَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِوُجُودِ : كَرَبْطِ وُجُودِ الشَّبَعِ بِوُجُودِ الْأَكْلِ ، وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِعَدَمٍ : كَرَبْطِ عَدَمِ الشِّبَعِ بِعَدَمِ الأَكْلِ ، وَرَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِعَدَمٍ : كَرَبْطِ وُجُودِ الْجُوعِ بِعَدَمِ الْأَكْلِ ، وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِوُجُودٍ : كَرَبْطِ عَدَمِ الْجُوعِ بِوُجُودِ الْأَكْلِ. قَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَنَّ الرَّبْطَ بَيْنَ أَمْرَيْنِ فِي الْحُكْمِ الْعَادِيِّ يَصِحُّ فِي وُجُودِ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا وَعَدَمِهِ، فَلَزِمَ انْقِسَامُهُ إِلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَقْسَامٍ؛ مِنْ ضَرْبِ اثْنَيْنِ - وَهُمَا وُجُودُ أَحَدِ الْأَمْرَيْنِ وَعَدَمُهُ - فِي اثْنَيْنِ؛ وَهُمَا وُجُودُ الْأَمْرِ الْآخَرِ وَعَدَمُهُ. فَإِذَا كَانَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ سَبَبًا عَادِيًّا لِلْآخَرِ .. ارْتَبَطَ وُجُودُهُ بِوُجُودِهِ، وَعَدَمُهُ بِعَدَمِهِ. وَإِذَا كَانَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ شَرْطًا عَادِيًّا لِلْآخَرِ .. ارْتَبَطَ عَدَمُهُ بِعَدَمِهِ، وَلَا يَرْتَبِطُ وُجُودُهُ بِوُجُودِ الْآخَرِ وَلَا عَدَمِهِ. وَإِذَا كَانَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ مَانِعًا عَادِيًّا مِنْ وُجُودِ الْآخَرِ .. ارْتَبَطَ وُجُودُ الْمَانِعِ بِعَدَمِ الْآخَرِ، وَلَا يَرْتَبِطُ عَدَمُهُ بِعَدَمِ الْآخَرِ وَلَا وُجُودِهِ.
And its divisions are four: the connection of existence with existence, like the connection of the existence of satiety with the existence of eating; and the connection of non-existence with non-existence, like the connection of the non-existence of satiety with the non-existence of eating; and the connection of existence with non-existence, like the connection of the existence of hunger with the non-existence of eating; and the connection of non-existence with existence, like the connection of the non-existence of hunger with the existence of eating. You have known that the connection between two things in the customary judgment is valid in the existence of each one of them and its non-existence, so its division into four parts followed necessarily from multiplying two—which are the existence of one of the two things and its non-existence—by two, which are the existence of the other thing and its non-existence. So if one of the two things is a customary cause for the other, then its existence is connected to its existence, and its non-existence to its non-existence. And if one of the two things is a customary condition for the other, then its non-existence is connected to its non-existence, and its existence is not connected to the existence of the other nor to its non-existence. And if one of the two things is a customary obstacle to the existence of the other, then the existence of the obstacle is connected to the non-existence of the other, and its non-existence is not connected to the non-existence of the other nor to its existence.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines the four divisions of the customary judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī). The author first lists the four types of connection (rabṭ): existence with existence, non-existence with non-existence, existence with non-existence, and non-existence with existence, each with an example. He then explains that because any connection can involve either existence or non-existence of each of the two related things, multiplying the two possibilities yields exactly four types. Next, he applies this to three customary relationships: cause (sabab), condition (sharṭ), and obstacle (māniʿ). A cause produces two connections: existence of cause with existence of effect, and non-existence of cause with non-existence of effect. A condition produces only one: non-existence of condition with non-existence of conditioned; the existence of the condition does not necessitate anything regarding the conditioned. An obstacle produces one: existence of obstacle with non-existence of the hindered thing; the absence of the obstacle does not necessitate anything. The fourth connection (non-existence with existence) is not accounted for here; it will be derived in the next lesson.

Key Terms
الحكم العادي
ربط
وجود
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth connection (non-existence with existence) is unnecessary or redundant.
Correction: The fourth connection follows logically from multiplying existence and non-existence of the two things; it is a necessary division even if not always explicitly used.

Checkpoint
How many divisions of the customary judgment are there?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The four divisions of the customary judgment are derived by considering the existence and non-existence of two related things, yielding exactly four possible connections.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the four customary connections do you most often assume in your daily life? Reflect on a situation where you wrongly assumed a connection (or lack thereof) and what the consequences were.
?
Your spiritual habits—like prayer, charity, or remembrance—are linked to states of the heart (e.g., peace, hardness). Can you identify which of the four divisions might explain an unexpected spiritual dryness you've experienced?
?
The passage shows that all four combinations are logically possible. When have you acted as if only one or two possibilities existed, ignoring the others? How might acknowledging all four change your reliance on customary patterns in your relationship with Allah?

Cause, Condition, and Obstacle in Customary Judgment
Applies the four divisions to cause, condition, and obstacle

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how customary cause, condition, and obstacle each map to specific connections among the four divisions.

Core Idea
Cause, condition, and obstacle each produce a distinct pattern of customary connections.

After establishing the four possible customary connections, Sanusi now applies them to three key relationships: cause, condition, and obstacle. Each has a unique signature: a cause gives two connections, a condition gives one, and an obstacle also gives one but of a different type. In this lesson, we will see how each relationship determines which connections hold.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three relationships
Read the passage and note the definitions of customary cause, condition, and obstacle. Look for the key terms: sabab ʿādī, sharṭ ʿādī, and māniʿ ʿādī.
Each relationship is introduced with 'If one of the two things is a customary...'
2
Note the connections for each
For cause, two connections are given: existence with existence and non-existence with non-existence. For condition, only one: non-existence with non-existence. For obstacle, one: existence with non-existence.
Notice that condition and obstacle each give only one connection, but of opposite types.
3
Understand the pattern
Ask yourself why cause gives two, while condition and obstacle give one. The commentary explains that cause necessitates both presence and absence, while condition only necessitates absence and obstacle only prevents presence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَقْسَامُهُ أَرْبَعَةٌ : رَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِوُجُودِ : كَرَبْطِ وُجُودِ الشَّبَعِ بِوُجُودِ الْأَكْلِ ، وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِعَدَمٍ : كَرَبْطِ عَدَمِ الشِّبَعِ بِعَدَمِ الأَكْلِ ، وَرَبْطُ وُجُودٍ بِعَدَمٍ : كَرَبْطِ وُجُودِ الْجُوعِ بِعَدَمِ الْأَكْلِ ، وَرَبْطُ عَدَمٍ بِوُجُودٍ : كَرَبْطِ عَدَمِ الْجُوعِ بِوُجُودِ الْأَكْلِ. قَدْ عَرَفْتَ أَنَّ الرَّبْطَ بَيْنَ أَمْرَيْنِ فِي الْحُكْمِ الْعَادِيِّ يَصِحُّ فِي وُجُودِ كُلِّ وَاحِدٍ مِنْهُمَا وَعَدَمِهِ، فَلَزِمَ انْقِسَامُهُ إِلَى أَرْبَعَةِ أَقْسَامٍ؛ مِنْ ضَرْبِ اثْنَيْنِ - وَهُمَا وُجُودُ أَحَدِ الْأَمْرَيْنِ وَعَدَمُهُ - فِي اثْنَيْنِ؛ وَهُمَا وُجُودُ الْأَمْرِ الْآخَرِ وَعَدَمُهُ. فَإِذَا كَانَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ سَبَبًا عَادِيًّا لِلْآخَرِ .. ارْتَبَطَ وُجُودُهُ بِوُجُودِهِ، وَعَدَمُهُ بِعَدَمِهِ. وَإِذَا كَانَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ شَرْطًا عَادِيًّا لِلْآخَرِ .. ارْتَبَطَ عَدَمُهُ بِعَدَمِهِ، وَلَا يَرْتَبِطُ وُجُودُهُ بِوُجُودِ الْآخَرِ وَلَا عَدَمِهِ. وَإِذَا كَانَ أَحَدُ الْأَمْرَيْنِ مَانِعًا عَادِيًّا مِنْ وُجُودِ الْآخَرِ .. ارْتَبَطَ وُجُودُ الْمَانِعِ بِعَدَمِ الْآخَرِ، وَلَا يَرْتَبِطُ عَدَمُهُ بِعَدَمِ الْآخَرِ وَلَا وُجُودِهِ.
And its divisions are four: the connection of existence with existence, like the connection of the existence of satiety with the existence of eating; and the connection of non-existence with non-existence, like the connection of the non-existence of satiety with the non-existence of eating; and the connection of existence with non-existence, like the connection of the existence of hunger with the non-existence of eating; and the connection of non-existence with existence, like the connection of the non-existence of hunger with the existence of eating. You have known that the connection between two things in the customary judgment is valid in the existence of each one of them and its non-existence, so its division into four parts followed necessarily from multiplying two—which are the existence of one of the two things and its non-existence—by two, which are the existence of the other thing and its non-existence. So if one of the two things is a customary cause for the other, then its existence is connected to its existence, and its non-existence to its non-existence. And if one of the two things is a customary condition for the other, then its non-existence is connected to its non-existence, and its existence is not connected to the existence of the other nor to its non-existence. And if one of the two things is a customary obstacle to the existence of the other, then the existence of the obstacle is connected to the non-existence of the other, and its non-existence is not connected to the non-existence of the other nor to its existence.
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines the four divisions of the customary judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī). The author first lists the four types of connection (rabṭ): existence with existence, non-existence with non-existence, existence with non-existence, and non-existence with existence, each with an example. He then explains that because any connection can involve either existence or non-existence of each of the two related things, multiplying the two possibilities yields exactly four types. Next, he applies this to three customary relationships: cause (sabab), condition (sharṭ), and obstacle (māniʿ). A cause produces two connections: existence of cause with existence of effect, and non-existence of cause with non-existence of effect. A condition produces only one: non-existence of condition with non-existence of conditioned; the existence of the condition does not necessitate anything regarding the conditioned. An obstacle produces one: existence of obstacle with non-existence of the hindered thing; the absence of the obstacle does not necessitate anything. The fourth connection (non-existence with existence) is not accounted for here; it will be derived in the next lesson.

Key Terms
سبب عادي
شرط عادي
مانع عادي
وجود
عدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A condition's existence always leads to the existence of the conditioned.
Correction: The passage states that the existence of a condition is not connected to the existence or non-existence of the conditioned. Only the non-existence of the condition is connected to the non-existence of the conditioned.

Checkpoint
Which customary relationship produces exactly two of the four customary connections?
A) Cause
B) Condition
C) Obstacle
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Cause, condition, and obstacle each establish specific patterns of customary connections, with cause linking both existence and non-existence, while condition and obstacle each link only one pair.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on customary causes daily—when you eat, you expect to be nourished. But how often do you forget that these patterns are not necessary but customary, sustained by Allah? Does your trust in Allah outweigh your trust in the habitual order? What would need to change in your heart for your reliance on causes and conditions to become a reflection of tawakkul?
?
Consider a time when you were certain that removing an obstacle (like a bad habit or a difficult person) would guarantee success. How does this lesson's teaching that the non-existence of an obstacle is not connected to the existence of the outcome challenge your assumption? Where in your life are you placing too much confidence in the absence of barriers rather than in Allah's will?
?
The lesson distinguishes between cause and condition: a condition's absence prevents, but its presence does not produce. Think of a spiritual practice—like prayer or charity—that you treat as a cause for divine reward. Might it actually be a condition? How does misidentifying it affect your relationship with Allah? What would happen if you shifted from expecting results to simply fulfilling the condition?

The Fourth Connection: Objection and Answer
Sanusi resolves the objection that only three connections are necessary by showing cause and condition imply the fourth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the fourth connection (existence connected to non-existence) is logically implied by the customary cause and condition, and how Sanusi answers the objection.

Core Idea
The fourth connection follows logically from cause and condition through their opposites.

Sanusi now addresses a potential objection: if there are only three types of customary relationships (cause, condition, obstacle), why is a fourth connection needed? The objector argues that the three relationships produce only three connections; the fourth is missing. Sanusi shows that the fourth connection arises from the logical implications of the cause and condition. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, focusing on the objector's claim that the divisions are only three. Then read the translation.
The objector lists three connections: existence-existence, non-existence-non-existence, and non-existence-existence.
2
Identify the missing fourth connection
The objector says there is no implication for connection of existence with non-existence. Understand why this seems missing.
3
Follow Sanusi's answer
Sanusi explains that the cause and condition together imply the fourth connection. Note how the non-existence of a cause entails the existence of the opposite of the effect.
Use the logical steps: non-existence of cause → non-existence of effect → existence of opposite of effect.
4
Understand the example
Reflect on the example of eating nourishing food and satiety to see how the cause-ship works.
The example illustrates the cause: eating leads to satiety, and its non-existence leads to non-satiety (the opposite).
5
Synthesize the lesson
Summarize how the fourth connection is not arbitrary but a logical consequence. Try to explain it in your own words.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قلت : مقتضى ما ذكرتُم أن تكونَ الأقسام ثلاثة: ارتباط وجود بوجود وذلك في السبب العادي، وارتباط عدم بعدم وذلك في السبب أيضاً وفي الشرط، وارتباط عدم بوجود وذلك في المانع العادي، وبقي فإنه لا مقتضى له من هذه الثلاثة ارتباط وجود بعدم والربط العادي منحصر فيها، فمن أين جاءكم هذا القسم الرابع؟ قلت: المقتضي لهذا القسم الرابع - وهو ارتباط وجود بعدم - السبب والشرط العاديان؛ وذلك أنك قد عرفت أن عدم السبب يقتضي عدم المسبب، وعدم الشرط يقتضي عدم المشروط، ومن لازم اقتضاء عدم السبب لعدم المسبب اقتضاء عدمه لوجود نقيض المسبب، فلزم ارتباط وجود نقيض المسبب لعدم السبب، وافهم مثل هذا في اقتضاء عدم الشرط لوجود نقيض المشروط، فيكون وجود نقيض المشروط مرتبطاً بعدم الشرط. مثال السبب العادي: أكل الطعام المقتات بالنسبة إلى الشبع.
For you might say: The implication of what you mentioned is that the divisions are three: connection of existence with existence — that is in the customary cause; connection of non-existence with non-existence — that is in the cause also and in the condition; and connection of non-existence with existence — that is in the customary obstacle. And there remains from these three no implication for connection of existence with non-existence, and the customary connection is confined to them, so from where did this fourth division come to you? I say: What implies this fourth division — which is connection of existence with non-existence — is the customary cause and condition. That is because you have known that the non-existence of the cause entails the non-existence of the effect, and the non-existence of the condition entails the non-existence of the conditioned; and from the necessity of the entailment of the non-existence of the cause for the non-existence of the effect follows the entailment of its non-existence for the existence of the opposite of the effect; so the connection of the existence of the opposite of the effect to the non-existence of the cause necessarily follows. And understand the like of this in the entailment of the non-existence of the condition for the existence of the opposite of the conditioned; thus the existence of the opposite of the conditioned is connected to the non-existence of the condition. Example of the customary cause: eating nourishing food with respect to satiety.
Source pages p. 135

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer argument. The objector notes that the three types of customary relationships — cause, condition, and obstacle — seem to produce only three of the four connections: cause gives existence-existence and non-existence-non-existence; condition gives non-existence-non-existence (already covered); and obstacle gives non-existence-existence. The fourth connection (existence-non-existence) is missing, and since the customary connection is limited to these three relationships, the fourth appears unjustified. The author answers that the fourth connection actually follows from the cause and condition. Specifically, if the non-existence of a cause entails the non-existence of its effect, then it also entails the existence of the opposite (naqīḍ) of that effect, because the non-existence of the effect is identical to the existence of its opposite. Therefore, the existence of the opposite of the effect is necessarily connected to the non-existence of the cause. The same applies to the condition: the non-existence of the condition entails the non-existence of the conditioned, and hence the existence of the opposite of the conditioned. Thus the fourth connection emerges, and the example of eating and satiety illustrates the cause-ship.

Key Terms
سبب عادي
شرط عادي
مانع عادي
نقيض
مسبب
مشروط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fourth connection (existence linked to non-existence) is arbitrary or unnecessary; the three connections from cause, condition, and obstacle are sufficient.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the fourth connection follows logically from the cause and condition when considering the opposites of effects and conditioned things.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what implies the fourth connection (existence connected to non-existence)?
A) The obstacle alone
B) The cause and condition
C) The cause alone
D) The condition alone

Key Takeaway
The fourth connection (existence linked to non-existence) is derived from the logical entailments of cause and condition, not an arbitrary addition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face an unexpected outcome despite fulfilling all apparent causes, do you reflect on Allah's omnipotence and the fact that He can bring about the opposite effect? How can this lesson deepen your reliance on Him rather than on causes alone?
?
Consider a time when you were certain about the absence of something leading to a specific result. Did you ever stop to think that the absence logically implies the presence of its opposite? How does this awareness change your perception of Allah's decree?
?
The fourth connection shows that even non-existence is linked to existence through logical necessity in Allah's custom. Are you mindful that every absence in your life (e.g., lack of health, wealth, companionship) is also a divine sign pointing to something present? Will you now look for the hidden blessings in what you lack?

What is a Rational Judgment?
Sanusi defines rational judgment as affirmation or negation without reliance on repetition or convention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of rational judgment and how it differs from empirical and conventional judgments.

Core Idea
Rational judgment is purely intellectual, independent of habit or agreement.

Before we can discuss the categories of rational judgment, we must first understand what a rational judgment itself is. Sanusi begins with a crisp definition that sets the foundation for all later theological reasoning. This passage clarifies the scope and nature of rational judgment, distinguishing it from other types of judgment.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice that Sanusi defines rational judgment by what it depends on and what it does not depend on.
The definition is short but packed with meaning.
2
Understand the two exclusions
Identify the two things rational judgment does NOT depend on: repetition (takarrur) and the imposition of an imposer (waḍʿ wāḍiʿ). Ask yourself why each exclusion is necessary.
Repetition relates to habit or custom; imposition relates to convention.
3
Contrast with other judgments
Think of examples: a rational judgment is 'the whole is greater than its part' (intellectual), an empirical judgment is 'fire burns' (based on repetition), a conventional judgment is 'this word means that' (based on human agreement).
This contrast solidifies the definition.
4
Reflect on why this matters
Consider that theological truths about Allah are rational judgments, not based on experience or social agreement. This definition grounds the entire study of kalam.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ حد الحكم العقلي ] وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ الْعَقْلِيُّ : فَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ مِنْ غَيْرِ تَوَقُّفٍ عَلَى تَكَرُّرٍ وَلا وَضْعِ وَاضِعِ
[Limit of the rational judgment] As for the rational judgment: it is affirming a thing or negating it without depending on repetition or the imposition of an imposer.
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage provides the definition (ḥadd) of rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī). Sanusi defines it as the act of affirming (ithbāt) or negating (nafy) something — i.e., making a positive or negative statement — without dependence on repetition (takarrur) or the imposition of an imposer (waḍʿ wāḍiʿ). The phrase 'without dependence on repetition' excludes judgments based on habit or custom (like causal judgments). The phrase 'without the imposition of an imposer' excludes conventional judgments (like language). Thus, rational judgment is purely intellectual: it relies solely on the intellect's own perception of necessary, possible, or impossible relations. This definition distinguishes rational judgments from empirical (habitual) and conventional judgments.

Key Terms
حكم عقلي
إثبات
نفي
توقف
تكرار
وضع واضع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think rational judgment can be based on habit or social agreement.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly excludes dependence on repetition (habit) and imposition (convention), showing that rational judgment is purely intellectual.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's definition, what does rational judgment NOT depend on?
A) The intellect
B) Repetition or an imposer
C) Affirmation or negation
D) Necessity

Key Takeaway
Rational judgment is the intellect's affirmation or negation, independent of habit or human agreement.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a belief you hold about your faith that you have never questioned. Is it based on what you have heard repeatedly, or on a rational judgment you made after careful thought? If it is based on repetition, what would it mean for you to examine it with your intellect today?
?
In a recent moral decision, did you follow what others around you agreed was right (convention), or did you rely on your own rational judgment? Describe the situation. If you chose convention, are you willing to reconsider that choice in light of Sanusi's definition?

Defining Rational Judgment
Sanusi defines rational judgment and responds to an objection about repetition in habitual judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's definition of rational judgment and how it differs from habitual judgment, including the role of repetition.

Core Idea
Rational judgment requires no repetition or convention—only the intellect.

Sanusi begins by precisely defining rational judgment, the foundation of kalam. He explains that this type of judgment depends solely on the intellect, not on repeated experience or social conventions. This definition sets the stage for understanding the nature of theological arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and examples
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the key terms 'affirmation', 'negation', 'without repetition', and 'without institution of an institutor'.
2
Understand the genus and differentia
Observe how Sanusi identifies 'affirmation or negation' as the genus and 'without depending on repetition' as the differentia that excludes habitual judgments.
3
Examine the objection and reply
Consider the objection that we accept habitual judgments from experts without personal repetition. See how Sanusi replies that the repetition still underlies the judgment through the expert's experience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَمَّا الْحُكْمُ الْعَقْلِيُّ : فَهُوَ إِثْبَاتُ أَمْرٍ أَوْ نَفْيُهُ مِنْ غَيْرِ تَوَقُّفٍ عَلَى تَكَرُّرٍ وَلا وَضْعِ وَاضِعٍ
إنَّما أُضيف هذا الحكم إلى العقلِ وإِنْ كانَتِ الأحكامُ كلُّها لا تُدرَكُ إِلا بالعقلِ ؛ لأنَّ مُجرَّدَ العقل بدونِ فكرةٍ أو معها كافٍ في إدراك هذا الحكم
فقوله : ( إثبات أمر ) مثاله : الواحد نصفُ الاثنينِ .
وقوله : ( أو نفيه ( مثالُهُ : الثلاثة ليست نصفاً للأربعة .
وهذا القيد وهو : ( إثبات أمرٍ أو نفيه ( جنس للحد .
وقوله : ( مِنْ غيرِ توقُّف على تكرُّرِ ) فصل أخرج الحكم العادي ؛ كقولنا : شرابُ السَّكَنجبين يُسكِّنُ الصفراء ؛ فإنَّ هذا الحكم لم يثبت له إلا بواسطة التكرُّرِ والتجربة ، حتى عُرِفَ أَنَّهُ ليسَ باتفاقي
فإن قلت : ها نحنُ نثبتُ هذا الحكم للسَّكنجبين تقليداً للأطباء وإن لم يتكرر عندنا ولا جربناه
قلتُ : إِنَّما أثبتنا فيهِ هذا الحكم بواسطة التجربة التي صدقنا فيها الأطباء ، وليسَ مِنْ شرطِ التكرُّرِ والتجربة في الحكم العادي أن يكونا مِنْ كُلِّ أحدٍ ، بل هو المستند لثبوتِ الحكم العادي وإنْ حصلَ مِنَ البعض الموثوق بتجربته .
As for the rational judgment: it is affirming a thing or negating it without depending on repetition or the institution of an institutor. This judgment is attributed to the intellect even though all judgments are only perceived by the intellect, because mere intellect without reflection or with it is sufficient to perceive this judgment. So His statement: 'affirming a thing' - its example: one is half of two. And His statement: 'or negating it' - its example: three is not half of four. And this restriction, which is 'affirming a thing or negating it', is the genus for the definition. And His statement: 'without depending on repetition' is a differentia that excludes the habitual judgment, like our saying: 'Drink of oxymel soothes the yellow bile'; for this judgment is only established for it through repetition and experience, until it is known that it is not accidental. For if you say: 'We establish this judgment for oxymel by following the doctors even if it has not been repeated for us and we have not experienced it.' I say: 'We only established this judgment in it through the experience in which we trusted the doctors, and it is not a condition of repetition and experience in habitual judgment that they come from every individual; rather, it is the foundation for establishing the habitual judgment even if it occurs from someone whose experience is reliable.'
Source pages p. 136

Commentary
This passage defines rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) and distinguishes it from habitual judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿādī). The definition states that rational judgment is affirming or negating a proposition without relying on repetition or the convention of an institutor (waḍʿ wāḍiʿ). The author explains why this judgment is attributed specifically to the intellect: because even though all judgments are perceived by reason, this type requires only reason, whether accompanied by reflection or not. He illustrates with examples: 'one is half of two' (affirmation) and 'three is not half of four' (negation). The phrase 'affirming or negating' serves as the genus (jins) of the definition. The phrase 'without depending on repetition' is a differentia (faṣl) that excludes habitual judgments, which depend on repetition and experience. An example is the statement that oxymel soothes yellow bile, known through repeated experience. The passage then addresses an objection: if someone says that we accept habitual judgments from doctors without ourselves having repeated the experience, the answer is that we still rely on experience—the experience of trusted doctors—so repetition is not required from each individual personally; it suffices that the judgment is based on reliable testimony of experience.

الحكم العقلي
الحكم العادي
الإثبات
النفي
التكرر
التجربة
وضع الواضع
الجنس
الفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that habitual judgments are not based on repetition if we accept them from experts.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the repetition still underlies the judgment through the expert's experience, so it remains habitual.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes rational judgment from habitual judgment according to this passage?
A) Rational judgment requires reflection
B) Rational judgment does not depend on repetition or convention
C) Habitual judgment is always uncertain
D) Rational judgment is only about numbers

Key Takeaway
Rational judgment is defined by its independence from repetition and convention, relying solely on the intellect, while habitual judgment depends on repeated experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you accept a religious ruling from a scholar, do you treat it as a rational judgment or a habitual one? How does that distinction affect the level of certainty you have in that ruling? Name one specific ruling you currently follow out of habit and commit today to investigating its rational foundation.
?
The passage shows that even habitual judgments rely on trust in others' repeated experiences. Which core Islamic belief do you hold primarily because you trust the reports of the Prophet (tawātur) rather than through your own rational demonstration? Are you content with that level of certainty for such a weighty matter? Resolve today to study one rational proof from kalam for that belief.
?
Sanusi defines rational judgment as independent of repetition and convention. Can you identify a single belief about your own existence (e.g., 'I exist' or 'the past is real') that you accept without any repetition or testimony? If you are honest, do you actually accept it because the intellect alone affirms it, or because it has been confirmed repeatedly? Let this reflection humble you and drive you to seek Allah's guidance in distinguishing between intellect and habit in your faith.

Rational vs. Habitual Judgment
Sanusi defines rational judgment and distinguishes it from habitual judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of rational judgment and how it differs from habitual (customary) judgment.

Core Idea
A rational judgment is known by reason alone, without needing repetition.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a key distinction: although all judgments are perceived by the intellect, rational judgments are unique because they require nothing beyond reason itself. In this passage, he carefully defines rational judgment and contrasts it with habitual judgment, which depends on repetition and experience. Understanding this difference is foundational for all subsequent theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice the author's precise wording and the structure of the definition.
2
Identify the genus and differentia
Find the genus (affirmation/negation of a thing) and the differentia (no dependence on repetition). Understand how these two parts form the definition of rational judgment.
The examples help clarify: 'one is half of two' is an affirmation; 'three is not half of four' is a negation.
3
Examine the objection and answer
Read the objection about relying on doctors for habitual judgments. See how the author defends that the basis of habitual judgment is still repetition and experience, even if not personally witnessed.
Pay attention to the footnote on the relationship between tajriba and takarrur.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنَّما أُضيف هذا الحكم إلى العقلِ وإِنْ كانَتِ الأحكامُ كلُّها لا تُدرَكُ إِلا بالعقلِ ؛ لأنَّ مُجرَّدَ العقل بدونِ فكرةٍ أو معها كافٍ في إدراك هذا الحكم فقوله : ( إثبات أمر ) مثاله : الواحد نصفُ الاثنينِ . وقوله : ( أو نفيه ( مثالُهُ : الثلاثة ليست نصفاً للأربعة . وهذا القيد وهو : ( إثبات أمرٍ أو نفيه ( جنس للحد . وقوله : ( مِنْ غيرِ توقُّف على تكرُّرِ ) فصل أخرج الحكم العادي ؛ كقولنا : شرابُ السَّكَنجبين يُسكِّنُ الصفراء ؛ فإنَّ هذا الحكم لم يثبت له إلا بواسطة التكرُّرِ والتجربة ، حتى عُرِفَ أَنَّهُ ليسَ باتفاقي [ التجربة والتكرر هما مستندا إثباتِ الحكم العادي ] فإن قلت : ها نحنُ نثبتُ هذا الحكم للسَّكنجبين تقليداً للأطباء وإن لم يتكرر عندنا ولا جربناه (۱) قلتُ : إِنَّما أثبتنا فيهِ هذا الحكم بواسطة التجربة التي صدقنا فيها الأطباء ، وليسَ مِنْ شرطِ التكرُّرِ والتجربة في الحكم العادي أن يكونا مِنْ كُلِّ أحدٍ ، بل هو المستند لثبوتِ الحكم العادي وإنْ حصلَ مِنَ البعض الموثوق بتجربته (۲) . (۱) بين التجربة والتكرر عموم وخصوص من وجه ؛ فالأمر الذي تكرر دون أن تختبره لا يقال له : تجربة ، والتجربة من غير تكرر لا يقال عنها : تكرر والتجربة مع التكرُّر هي موضع الجمع بينهما (۲) الواو في قوله : ( وإن حصل ( للعطف على مقدر ؛ أي : إن حصل التكرُّر :
This judgment is attributed to the intellect even though all judgments are perceived only by the intellect, because mere intellect without reflection or with it suffices to perceive this judgment. So his saying: (affirmation of a thing) its example: the one is half of two. And his saying: (or its negation) its example: three is not half of four. And this restriction, i.e., (affirmation of a thing or its negation), is the genus of the definition. And his saying: (without depending on repetition) is a differentia that excludes the customary judgment, like our saying: the drink of sakjabin soothes the yellow bile; for this judgment is not established for it except through repetition and experience, until it is known that it is not accidental. [Experience and repetition are the bases for establishing the customary judgment.] If you say: we establish this judgment for sakjabin by imitating the physicians even though it has not been repeated for us nor have we experienced it (1). I say: we only established this judgment for it through the experience of the physicians whom we have trusted, and it is not a condition of repetition and experience in the customary judgment that they come from everyone, rather it is the basis for the establishment of the customary judgment even if it comes from some [person] whose experience is trustworthy (2). (1) Between experience and repetition there is generality and specificity in one respect; for the thing that is repeated without you testing it is not called experience, and experience without repetition is not called repetition; and experience with repetition is the locus of their conjunction. (2) The waw in his saying: (and even if it comes) is for conjunction with an omitted [phrase]; i.e., even if repetition has occurred:
Source pages p. 137

Commentary
This passage defines the rational judgment (al-hukm al-'aqli) and distinguishes it from the customary judgment (al-hukm al-'adi). The author begins by explaining why this specific judgment is attributed to the intellect, even though all judgments require the intellect: because mere intellect, whether with or without reflection, suffices to grasp this judgment without needing external factors like repetition. The definition has a genus (jins): the affirmation or negation of a thing (ithbat amr aw nafyih), exemplified by 'one is half of two' and 'three is not half of four'. The differentia (fasl) is 'without depending on repetition' (min ghayr tawaqquf 'ala takarrur), which excludes the customary judgment. The example of a customary judgment is that drinking sakjabin soothes yellow bile, which is known only through repetition and experience, not by pure reason. The author then addresses an objection: we might accept this judgment from doctors without personal experience. The answer clarifies that we rely on the experience of trusted physicians, and it is not required that everyone personally experience or repeat it; the basis of the customary judgment is reliable testimony. The footnotes further explain the relationship between experience (tajriba) and repetition (takarrur): they partially overlap, and the conjunction of both is the proper basis for customary judgments.

الحكم العادي
التكرر
التجربة
الجنس
الفصل

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Rational judgments are those that do not require any intellect at all.
Correction: All judgments require intellect, but rational judgments are distinguished by not requiring repetition or experience.

Checkpoint
What distinguishes the rational judgment from the customary judgment?
A) The rational judgment depends on repetition, while the customary does not.
B) The rational judgment does not depend on repetition, while the customary does.
C) Both depend on repetition.
D) Neither depends on repetition.

Key Takeaway
A rational judgment is one affirmed or negated by reason alone, without relying on repetition or experience.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one belief you hold about the world that you have never personally tested but accept because others have repeatedly told you it is true. Is this rational or customary in Sanusi's sense? What would it take for you to treat it as a rational judgment?
?
The passage says rational judgments are known by the intellect alone. Do you know, with the same certainty, that God exists by rational necessity, or have you been relying on repetition and habit? If today you lost every external reinforcement, would your certainty remain?
?
You trust doctors for medical advice based on their experience. Do you trust the scholars of Islam for theological truths in the same way? If so, are you accepting their judgments as customary rather than rational? What does that imply about the strength of your faith?

Distinguishing Rational and Legal Judgment
Sanusi distinguishes the rational judgment from the legal judgment and resolves an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between rational and legal judgment, and why legal judgment is considered a temporal relation of God's eternal speech.

Core Idea
Legal judgment is not God's eternal speech itself, but its temporal connection to our actions.

Sanusi continues refining the definition of rational judgment by excluding legal judgment. He anticipates a key confusion: if legal judgment is God's speech, how can it be 'placed' or 'made'? The answer reveals a subtle distinction between God's eternal attribute and its relation to contingent acts. This passage sharpens our understanding of what makes a judgment 'rational' versus 'legal'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal English translation. Notice the structure: a differentia, an objection, and an answer.
The passage is dense; reading both languages helps grasp the technical terms.
2
Identify the differentia
Find the second differentia that excludes legal judgment: 'and not the placing of an establisher' (wa la wad' wadi'). Understand how this differentia separates rational from legal judgment.
Legal judgment requires a 'placer' (God's command), while rational judgment does not.
3
Analyze the objection and answer
Examine the objection: How can legal judgment be placed/made if it is God's eternal speech? Then follow Sanusi's answer: what is meant is the temporal relation (ta'alluq tanjizi) of that speech to human actions, not the speech itself.
The distinction between eternal speech and its temporal relation is crucial.
4
Understand the two relations
Contrast the 'intellectual relation of eligibility' (ta'alluq 'aqli salahi) which is eternal, with the 'actualized relation' (ta'alluq tanjizi) which is temporal. Only the latter is called 'legal judgment' in this context.
This resolves the objection and clarifies terminology.
5
Reflect on terminology
Note Sanusi's comment that calling the temporal relation 'legal judgment' is common among jurists and usulists. This helps avoid confusion when reading other works.
Consistency with scholarly usage is important.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولا وضع واضع ) فصل آخرُ أخرجَ به الحكم الشرعي . [ بيانُ المراد بالحكم الشرعي ، وارتباطه بالوضع والجعل ] فإن قلت : كيف يصح أن يُقال في الحكم الشرعي : إِنَّهُ حصل بالوضعِ والجعلِ ، وهو خطابُ اللهِ تعالى وكلامه القديم ، والقديم ليس بموضوع ولا مجعول ؟ قلتُ : المراد بالحكم الشرعي هنا : التعلُّق التنجيزي لخطابِ اللهِ تعالى القديم المتعلّق بأفعال المكلَّفين بعد وجودهم وتوفر شرائط التكليف فيهم ، وهذا التعلُّقُ ليس بقديم ، والقديمُ إِنَّما هو كلامُ اللهِ تعالى وتعلقه العقلي الصلاحي بأفعال المكلفين في الأزلِ ، وإطلاق الحكم الشرعي على التعلُّقِ التنجيزي الحادثِ .. مشهورٌ عند الفقهاء والأصوليين ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق
His saying: (and not the placing of an establisher) is another differentia that excludes the legal judgment. [Explanation of the intended meaning of legal judgment and its connection to placing (wad') and making (ja'l).] If you say: How can it be valid to say concerning the legal judgment that it occurs by placing and making, while it is the address of God Most High and His eternal speech, and the eternal is not placed nor made? I say: What is meant by legal judgment here is the actualized relation (ta'alluq tanjizi) of God's eternal address concerning the acts of the legally responsible after their existence and the fulfillment of the conditions of obligation in them, and this relation is not eternal; the eternal is only God's speech and its intellectual relation of eligibility (ta'alluq 'aqli salahi) to the acts of the responsible in pre-eternity; and calling the temporal actualized relation 'legal judgment' is well-known among the jurists and usulists. And success is from God Most High.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage provides the second differentia (fasl) that excludes the legal judgment (al-hukm al-shar'i) from the definition of the rational judgment. The differentia is 'and not the placing of an establisher' (wa la wad' wadi'). The author then explains the intended meaning of legal judgment and its connection to placement (wad') and making (ja'l). The objection is raised: How can the legal judgment be said to occur by placement and making, when it is the eternal speech of God, which is not placed or made? The answer distinguishes between the eternal speech itself and its temporal relation to obligations. The legal judgment in question is the actualized relation (ta'alluq tanjizi) of God's eternal address to the acts of morally responsible beings after they exist and meet the conditions of obligation. This relation is not eternal; only God's speech and its eternal relation of eligibility (ta'alluq 'aqli salahi) are eternal. Calling this temporal relation 'legal judgment' is a well-known usage among jurists and usulists. The passage concludes with a prayer for divine success.

الوضع
الجعل
التعلق التنجيزي
التعلق العقلي الصلاحي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the legal judgment is itself the eternal divine speech.
Correction: The legal judgment in this context is actually a temporal relation of that speech to human actions, not the speech itself.

Checkpoint

A) The eternal speech of God Himself.
B) The actualized relation of God's eternal address to actions after obligation conditions are met.
C) The intellectual relation of eligibility in pre-eternity.
D) The action of a human legislator.

Key Takeaway
The legal judgment in the sense used here is a temporal relation of God's eternal speech, not the speech itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often consider rulings about your actions as if they are fixed and timeless commands from God. But this lesson says the legal judgment is a temporal relation—connected to your existence and conditions. Does that make you feel more accountable for your actions here and now, knowing that God's command is directly tied to your specific moment in time? What practical changes do you need to make to align your life with that current relation?
?
If God's eternal speech never changes, but its connection to your deeds is renewed every moment, are you living as though you are being addressed right now? Or do you assume the rulings only applied to past generations? In what area of your life have you been treating God's command as irrelevant to your present circumstances?
?
This lesson distinguishes between what is eternal (God's speech) and what is temporal (the actualized relation). How often do you confuse your own temporal interpretations or cultural practices with God's eternal word? What is one belief or action you need to re-examine to ensure you are following the eternal speech, not a temporary custom?

The Three Rational Judgments: Wujub, Istihalah, Jawaz
Sanusi enumerates the three divisions of rational judgment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three categories of rational judgment (wujub, istihalah, jawaz) and their foundational role in Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Every rational judgment is either necessary, impossible, or possible.

Sanusi begins his discussion of rational judgment by dividing it into three fundamental categories. These categories—necessity, impossibility, and possibility—form the basis of all theological reasoning. Before we can apply them, we must clearly understand what each means.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the English translation. Notice that the author explicitly states three divisions.
The passage has a corrupted section after 'تقديره : إثباتُ', but the core enumeration is clear.
2
Identify the three categories
Locate the terms الوُجُوبُ (wujub), الاسْتِحَالَةُ (istihalah), and الْجَوَازُ (jawaz). Understand their basic meanings: necessity, impossibility, possibility.
These are the three divisions of rational judgment (الحكم العقلي).
3
Understand the implied construct
The author notes that an omitted genitive construct (مضاف) is needed in this statement. Reflect on why the phrase requires an implied word like 'affirmation of' to complete the meaning.
The corrupted text after 'إثباتُ' is likely an error and can be disregarded for the main teaching.
4
Reflect on application
Consider how these three categories apply to God and creation. For example, God's existence is wujub, non-existence is istihalah, and the existence of contingent things is jawaz.
This shows that the categories are not only for contingent matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَقْسَامُهُ ثَلاثَةٌ : الْوُجُوبُ ، وَالِاسْتِحَالَةُ ، وَالْجَوَازُ لا بدَّ مِنْ حذفِ مضاف في هذا الكلام ، تقديره : إثباتُ والتجربة من كل واحد ممن يثبت الحكم العادي، وإن حصل من بعد للموثوق بتجربته .
And its divisions are three: necessity (al-wujub), impossibility (al-istihalah), and possibility (al-jawaz). There must be an omitted genitive construct in this statement, its implied [word] being: affirmation of ... and experience from everyone who establishes the customary judgment, even if it comes later from one whose experience is trustworthy.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three divisions of the rational judgment (al-hukm al-'aqli) as necessity (wujub), impossibility (istihalah), and possibility (jawaz). The author notes that in this statement an implied genitive construct (mudaf) must be understood, but the continuation is unclear due to apparent textual corruption that mixes in elements from the earlier discussion on the customary judgment. The core point, however, is clear: the rational judgment is divided into three categories. The first, necessity, means that the intellect necessarily affirms or denies something (e.g., God must exist). The second, impossibility, means the intellect deems something impossible (e.g., God being non-existent). The third, possibility, means the intellect sees no inherent necessity or impossibility (e.g., the existence of a contingent being). The corrupted text after 'affirmation of' (ithbatu) is likely an error and should be disregarded for understanding the main division.

Key Terms

الاستحالة
الجواز
الحكم العقلي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The three rational judgments apply only to contingent matters.
Correction: Wujub and istihalah also apply to God: His existence is wujub, His non-existence is istihalah. Thus, the categories apply to both necessary and contingent beings.

Checkpoint
What are the three divisions of the rational judgment mentioned by the author?
A) Wujub, ja'iz, haram.
B) Wujub, istihalah, jawaz.
C) Wajib, mustahil, mumtani'.
D) Wajib, mandub, mubah.

Key Takeaway
The rational judgment is divided into necessity (wujub), impossibility (istihalah), and possibility (jawaz), which apply to both God and creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is your belief in God's existence a matter of necessity (wujub) or mere possibility (jawaz)? If it is only possible, then you have doubt. If it is necessary, then your faith is firm. Which is it, and what will you do to move from possibility to necessity?
?
Consider an attribute like God's mercy: do you treat it as necessary in your daily life, trusting that He will be merciful, or do you live as if it is merely possible, leaving you anxious? Examine your heart and commit to acting on the necessity of His mercy.
?
The lesson says rational judgments are three, but do you ever treat some of God's attributes as impossible when they are actually possible? For example, do you doubt that God can forgive your worst sins? That would be a false impossibility. Confess any such doubt and resolve to affirm His power as possible and His mercy as necessary.
