Rational Judgments
beginner
4 min
The Three Divisions of Rational Judgment
Sanusi enumerates the three rational judgments and clarifies the grammatical/logical need for a supplement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the three rational judgments (necessity, impossibility, possibility) are categories of what is judged, not the judgments themselves, and why a grammatical supplement is required when listing them as divisions.

Core Idea
Rational judgments are about categories, not the categories themselves.

Sanusi begins by giving the three divisions of rational judgment: necessity, impossibility, and possibility. But he immediately pauses to clarify a subtle point: these three are not themselves the judgments but rather the objects of judgment. This grammatical and logical nuance is essential for understanding how the division works and why it is exhaustive.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the English translation. Notice how Sanusi states the division, then qualifies it with a grammatical correction.
The passage has an embedded footnote about future definitions.
2
Understand the grammatical supplement
Sanusi explains that 'necessity, impossibility, possibility' are not the judgments themselves but rather what is judged. To make the division proper, we must assume an omitted genitive construction: 'affirmation of necessity' etc. Identify why this is necessary.
The condition of division requires the name of the divided thing to apply to each part.
3
Grasp the logical basis
The three categories are exhaustive because every object of rational judgment either accepts both existence and non-existence (possible), only existence (necessary), or only non-existence (impossible). This is the foundation of all rational judgments.
4
Reflect on the footnote
The author notes that he will define these terms without repeating their sources, since knowing the derivative implies the root.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَأَقْسَامُهُ ثَلاثَةٌ : الْوُجُوبُ ، وَالِاسْتِحَالَةُ ، وَالْجَوَازُ . لا بدَّ مِنْ حذفِ مضاف في هذا الكلام ، تقديره : إثباتُ الوجوبِ ، وإثباتُ الاستحالة ، وإثباتُ الجواز ، ولكَ أنْ تحذفَ المضاف إليهِ في لفظ ( أقسامُهُ ) ويكون التقديرُ : وأقسام مُتعلَّقِهِ . وإنَّما احتجنا إلى هذا الحذفِ لأنَّ الحكم العقليَّ ليس هو نفس هذه الثلاثة المذكورة ، فلا تكون أقساماً لهُ ؛ لأنَّ مِنْ شرط القسمة صدْقَ اسم المقسوم على كلِّ واحدٍ مِنْ أقسامِهِ ، ولا يصدق على الوجوب أوِ الاستحالة أو الجواز اسمُ الحكم ، وإنَّما يصدق عليها أنَّها محكوم بها ، وقرينة الحذفِ جلية . ووجه انحصارِ الحكم العقلي في هذه الثلاثةِ : أَنَّ كلَّ ما يحكمُ بِهِ العقل : - إما أن يقبل الثبوت والانتفاء جميعاً . - أو يقبل الثبوت فقط . - أو يقبل الانتفاء فقط . فالأول : هو الجائز ، والثاني : هو الواجب ، والثالث : هو المستحيل . (وسيعرفها المصنف دون تعريف مصادرها السابق ذكرها؛ لأن معرفة المشتق يلزم منها معرفة المصدر؛ لأن معرفة الأخصّ يلزم منها معرفة الأعم).
And its divisions are three: necessity, impossibility, and possibility. It is necessary to assume an omitted genitive construct in this statement, its estimate being: the affirmation of necessity, the affirmation of impossibility, and the affirmation of possibility. And you may omit the annexed term in the phrase 'its divisions' so that the estimate becomes: the divisions of its object. We needed this omission only because the rational judgment is not itself these three mentioned, so they cannot be divisions of it; for one of the conditions of division is that the name of the divided thing applies to each of its parts, but the name 'judgment' does not apply to necessity, impossibility, or possibility; rather, they are said to be judged by it. And the clue for the omission is clear. And the reason for the restriction of rational judgment to these three is that everything that the intellect judges: either accepts both existence and non-existence, or accepts existence only, or accepts non-existence only. The first is the possible, the second is the necessary, and the third is the impossible. (And the author will define them without defining their sources mentioned previously, because knowing the derivative necessitates knowing the root, since knowing the more specific necessitates knowing the more general.)
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage enumerates the three divisions of rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-‘aqlī) and provides a logical justification. First, the author states that the divisions are three: necessity (wujūb), impossibility (istiḥālah), and possibility (jawāz). However, he immediately notes that a grammatical supplement is required because these three are not the judgments themselves but rather the objects or contents of judgment. The condition of a proper division is that the name of the divided item applies to each part, but 'necessity' is not called a 'judgment'; rather, it is something that is judged. Therefore, the intended meaning is 'the affirmation of necessity, affirmation of impossibility, and affirmation of possibility' or, if the annex is removed, 'the divisions of the object of judgment'. Then he explains the basis for the restriction to three: every object of rational judgment either accepts both existence and non-existence (possible), only existence (necessary), or only non-existence (impossible). This is the fundamental classification of all rational judgments. A footnote adds that the author will later define these terms without repeating their etymological sources, since knowing the derivative implies knowledge of the root.

Key Terms
حكم عقلي
وجوب
استحالة
جواز
قسمة
مضاف
متعلق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the three rational judgments themselves are necessity, impossibility, and possibility.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that these are categories of what is judged, not the judgments themselves. The judgments are affirmations of these categories.

Checkpoint
Why does a grammatical supplement need to be assumed when saying 'the divisions of rational judgment are three: necessity, impossibility, and possibility'?
A) Because the three are not judgments but objects of judgment
B) Because the judgment itself is eternal
C) Because the number three is incomplete
D) Because Arabic grammar requires a construct chain

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments—necessity, impossibility, and possibility—are categories of what is judged, requiring a grammatical supplement for proper division.

Rational Judgments

The Three Rational Judgments and Their Exhaustive Division
Sanusi clarifies terminology and gives logical basis for three rational judgments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) and why they form an exhaustive division based on the admission of existence and non-existence.

Core Idea
Rational judgment is not the categories themselves; the categories are what we judge by.

Sanusi begins by clarifying a subtle point of terminology: the 'divisions' he mentions are not divisions of the judgment itself but of its subject. He then provides a logical argument showing why only three rational judgments are possible—a foundational principle in kalam.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the grammatical discussion about 'its divisions'.
2
Understand the grammatical clarification
Sanusi explains why the divisions are not of the judgment itself but of its subject. Note the condition for a proper division: the name of the divided must apply to each division.
This is a key point to avoid misunderstanding.
3
Grasp the logical basis for three judgments
Sanusi gives a two-part argument: everything reason judges either admits both existence and non-existence, or only one. This yields the categories: possible, necessary, impossible.
This is the core of the lesson.

الوجوبِ، وإثباتُ الاستحالة، وإثباتُ الجواز، ولك أن تحذف المضاف إليه في لفظ (أقسامه) ويكون التقدير: وأقسام متعلّقه. وإنما احتجنا إلى هذا الحذف لأن الحكم العقلي ليس هو نفس هذه الثلاثة المذكورة، فلا تكون أقساماً له؛ لأن من شرط القسمة صدق اسم المقسوم على كل واحد من أقسامه، ولا يصدق على الوجوب أو الاستحالة أو الجواز اسم الحكم، وإنما يصدق عليها أنها محكوم بها، وقرينة الحذف جلية. ووجه انحصار الحكم العقلي في هذه الثلاثة: أن كل ما يحكم به العقل: إما أن يقبل الثبوت والانتفاء جميعاً، أو يقبل الثبوت فقط، أو يقبل الانتفاء فقط. فالأول: هو الجائز، والثاني: هو الواجب، والثالث: هو المستحيل.
Of necessity, and establishing impossibility, and establishing possibility. And you may omit the mudāf ilayh in the expression 'its divisions' so the implied meaning is: 'and the divisions of its subject.' We needed this omission because the rational judgment (al-ḥukm al-ʿaqlī) is not itself these three mentioned, so they are not divisions of it; because one condition of division (al-qisma) is that the name of the divided applies to each of its divisions, and the name 'judgment' does not apply to necessity, impossibility, or possibility; rather it applies to them that they are judged by. And the evidence of the omission is clear. And the reason for the limitation of rational judgment to these three is: everything that reason judges: either it admits both existence (thubūt) and non-existence (intifāʾ) together, or it admits existence only, or it admits non-existence only. The first is the possible (al-jāʾiz), the second is the necessary (al-wājib), and the third is the impossible (al-mustaḥīl).
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines the three categories of rational judgment: necessary, impossible, and possible. First, Sanusi addresses a grammatical point about the phrase 'its divisions' (aqsāmuhu) and argues that it is best understood as 'divisions of its subject' because the rational judgment (ḥukm ʿaqlī) is not itself these three terms. He justifies this by noting that for a proper division, the name of the divided must apply to each division, but 'judgment' does not apply to necessity, impossibility, or possibility—rather these are objects of judgment. Then he gives the reason for the limitation: everything reason judges can be conceived as either (1) admitting both existence and non-existence (the possible), (2) admitting only existence (the necessary), or (3) admitting only non-existence (the impossible). This is a logical partition covering all possibilities.

Key Terms
واجب (wājib)
مستحيل (mustaḥīl)
جائز (jā'iz)
حكم عقلي (ḥukm ʿaqlī)
قسمة (qisma)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the judgment itself is one of these three categories (necessary, impossible, possible).
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that the judgment is not the same as the categories; rather the categories are the objects of judgment. The judgment is the act of judging, while necessity, impossibility, and possibility are what we judge things to be.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, what is the condition for a proper division (qisma)?
A) The divided term must be a genus.
B) The name of the divided must apply to each of its divisions.
C) The divisions must be three.
D) The divisions must be based on existence and non-existence.

Key Takeaway
The three rational judgments (necessary, impossible, possible) are exhaustive because any object of reason either admits existence only, non-existence only, or both.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your belief in God, do you truly affirm that His existence is necessary (wājib), meaning that it is impossible for Him not to exist? Or does your heart secretly treat it as merely possible, as if God might not be there? Examine your daily life: does your worship reflect a lived certainty of His necessary existence?
?
The category of 'possible' includes everything you own, every relationship, every ambition. What is one thing you have been treating as necessary (thinking you cannot live without it) but is actually only possible? Will you release it and acknowledge its contingency before God today?
?
Sanusi warns that rational judgment (the act of reasoning) is not the same as the categories it judges. Have you ever confused your own reasoning about God or truth with the objective reality of what is true? How might this confusion lead to pride in your intellect rather than humility before the truth?

Definition of the Necessary and Its Two Kinds
Sanusi defines the necessary and divides it into necessary by necessity and by consideration.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the necessary (al-wājib) as that whose non-existence is inconceivable, and its division into necessary by necessity and by consideration.

Core Idea
The necessary is what the mind cannot deny; some are immediate, others need proof.

Sanusi begins by establishing the foundational definition of the necessary (al-wājib) in rational theology. Before discussing God's attributes, we must understand what it means for something to be rationally necessary. This passage defines the necessary and distinguishes between two types: those known without reflection and those known through deliberation.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and its division
Read the Arabic passage and its translation slowly. Notice the two categories: by necessity and by consideration.
The definition is very compact.
2
Understand the two kinds
Focus on the examples: occupying space for a body (necessary by necessity) and God's pre-eternity (necessary by consideration). Ask yourself why each is necessary.
The commentary explains the reasoning.
3
Clarify the concept of 'conceivability'
Sanusi distinguishes between logical conception (which is possible for the necessary before judgment) and intellectual acceptance (which is impossible). Understand this nuance.
This is key to avoid confusion.
4
Explore the kinds of necessary things
The passage lists three types: existent (like God's essence), established but not existent (like attributes of knowledge), and negative attributes (like oneness). Reflect on how these all meet the definition.
Some necessary things are not 'existents' in the usual sense.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ حد الواجب ]
فَالْوَاجِبُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ عَدَمُهُ ; إِمَّا ضَرُورَةً ; كَالتَّحَيُّزِ لِلْجِرْمِ ، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً ; كَوُجُوبِ الْقِدَمِ لِمَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ .
يعني : أنَّ حقيقة الواجب العقليّ : هو ما لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمه ; أي : لا يدركُ في العقل عدمه إمَّا ضرورةً ; أي : ابتداءً بلا تأمل ; كالتحيز للجرْمِ ; وهو أخذُهُ قدرَ ذاتِهِ مِنَ الفراغ، فإنَّ ثبوت هذا المعنى له لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل ضرورةً نفيه، ونظير هذا في الوجوبِ الضروري : كونُ الاثنين أكثر مِنَ الواحد . وإما نظراً ; أي : بعد التأمل ; كثبوتِ القِدَمِ لمولانا تبارك وتعالى ; يعني : لا يقبله ولا يصدق به، أما التصور بالمعنى المنطقي فلا محيد عنه ; إذ عدم الواجب متصوّر قطعاً قبل الحكم عليه بالاستحالة ; إذ حكمك على الشيء فرع عن تصوره، وقوله : ( عدمه ) يعني : عدم ثبوته ; إذ من الواجب ما هو موجود ; كذاته تعالى وصفات المعاني، ومنه ما هو ثابت وليس بموجود ; كالعالمية والقادرية، ومنه ما هو ثابت من حيث الوصف بالعدم ; كالوحدانية والقدم ; إذ الصفات العدمية لا وجود لها أصلاً .
[Definition of the Necessary]
The necessary (al-wājib) is that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect; either by necessity (ḍarūratan), like occupying space (al-taḥayyuz) for a body, or by consideration (naẓaran), like the necessity of pre-eternity (al-qidam) for our Lord, may He be exalted and glorified.
He means: the reality of the rational necessary is that which is not conceivable in the intellect to be non-existent; i.e., the intellect does not perceive its non-existence. Either by necessity, i.e., initially without reflection, like occupying space for a body; that is, its taking up an amount of space equal to its own essence, for the existence of this attribute for it is not conceivable in the intellect to be negated by necessity. An analogy for this in the necessary by necessity is that two is more than one. Or by consideration, i.e., after reflection, like the establishment of pre-eternity for our Lord, Blessed and Exalted; meaning, it does not accept it nor believe in it. As for conceptualization (al-taṣawwur) in the logical sense, there is no avoiding it; for the non-existence of the necessary is definitely conceivable before judging it as impossible; because your judgment of a thing is dependent on your conceptualization of it. His saying '(its non-existence)' means: the non-existence of its existence; for among the necessary is that which is existent, like His Essence and the attributes of meanings (ṣifāt al-maʿānī), and among it is that which is established but not existent, like 'being knowing' (al-ʿālimiyya) and 'being powerful' (al-qādiriyya), and among it is that which is established from the perspective of description by non-existence, like oneness (al-waḥdāniyya) and pre-eternity (al-qidam); for the attributes of non-existence (al-ṣifāt al-ʿadamiyya) have no existence at all.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the necessary (al-wājib) in rational theology. Sanusi first states the definition: the necessary is that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the intellect. He then divides it into two types: (1) necessary by necessity (ḍarūrī), which the mind immediately knows cannot be non-existent without any reflection—like a body occupying space, as it is impossible to conceive of a body that does not occupy space; (2) necessary by consideration (naẓarī), which requires reflection to know that its non-existence is impossible—like God's pre-eternity, which is proven by argument. The explanation clarifies that 'conceivability' here means intellectual acceptance, not mere logical conception (since one can logically conceive of the non-existence of the necessary before judging it as impossible). Sanusi then unpacks the phrase 'its non-existence' to mean non-existence of its existence, because some necessary things are existent (e.g., God's essence), some are relational attributes that are established but not existent (e.g., being knowing), and some are negative attributes that are descriptions of non-existence (e.g., oneness and pre-eternity).

Key Terms
واجب
ضرورة
نظر
تحيز
قدم
صفات عدمية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The necessary must be an existent being or thing.
Correction: Some necessary attributes are non-existent in themselves, like the attributes of negation (e.g., oneness and pre-eternity).

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, the necessary (al-wājib) is defined as:
A) That which must exist.
B) That which is inconceivable for the intellect to be non-existent.
C) That which is always existent.
D) That which is impossible to be non-existent.

Key Takeaway
The necessary (al-wājib) is rationally undeniable; it includes immediate truths like spatial occupancy and reasoned truths like God's pre-eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The passage states that the non-existence of the necessary is not conceivable in the intellect. Yet many people live as if Allah's existence is optional. Where in your own heart do you treat Allah's existence as merely probable rather than absolutely certain? What would change tonight if you truly felt that fact as undeniable?
?
Sanusi divides the necessary into immediate and reflective. Is your belief in Allah's pre-eternity merely inherited (immediate in your environment) or the result of genuine reflection? If you have not truly reasoned it out, what is one step you can take this week to move from blind acceptance to intellectual certainty?
?
The lesson distinguishes between logical conceivability and intellectual impossibility. Consider a doubt you have faced about a divine attribute. Was it merely a logically conceivable possibility, or did it reflect a deeper failure to submit your intellect to the truth? Will you now commit to rejecting such doubts as rationally invalid?

Definition of the Necessary (al-Wajib) and its Divisions
Sanusi defines the necessary being and distinguishes essential from accidental necessity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the necessary being in Islamic theology and the distinction between essential and accidental necessity.

Core Idea
The necessary being is that whose non-existence is inconceivable—either immediately or after reflection.

Sanusi begins by defining the foundational concept of the necessary (al-wajib). This definition is crucial for all theological reasoning about God's attributes. The passage then distinguishes between what is necessary in itself (essential) and what becomes necessary due to God's will (accidental). This lesson will unpack this distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the necessary
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation to get the full definition. Notice that Sanusi says the necessary is that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind.
2
Identify the two modes of necessity
The passage explains that inconceivability can occur in two ways: by necessity (daruratan) without reflection, or by theoretical reflection (nazaran). Find the examples given for each mode.
The example for necessity is that a body occupies space; for reflection, it is God's pre-eternity.
3
Distinguish essential from accidental necessity
The passage then clarifies that the definition applies to the essential necessary (al-wajib al-dhati). Then it introduces the accidental necessary (al-wajib al-'aradi), which is possible in itself but necessary due to God's will. Note the example of Abu Jahl's punishment.
Accidental necessity depends on God's will as revealed through the Prophet.
4
Reflect on the role of reason and revelation
Consider how the passage shows that reason alone can establish essential necessity, while accidental necessity relies on revelation. The footnote further explains that accidental judgments are mediated by the Law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[ حد الواجب ] فَأَلْوَاجِبُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ عَدَمُهُ ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةً ؛ كَالتَّحَيُّزِ لِلْجِرْمِ ، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً ؛ كَوُجُوبِ الْقِدَمِ لِمَوْلَانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ . يعني : أنَّ حقيقة الواجب العقليّ : هو ما لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمه ؛ أي : لا يدركُ في العقل عدمه (۱) إمَّا ضرورةً ؛ أي : ابتداءً بلا تأمل ؛ كالتحيز للجرْمِ ؛ وهو أخذُهُ قدرَ ذاتِهِ مِنَ الفراغ، فإنَّ ثبوت هذا المعنى له لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل ضرورةً نفيه ، ونظير هذا في الوجوبِ الضروري : كونُ الاثنين أكثر مِنَ الواحد . (1) وإما نظراً ؛ أي : بعد التأمل ؛ كثبوتِ القِدَمِ لمولانا تبارك وتعالى ، فإنَّهُ لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل نفيه عنه جل وعلا ، لكن بعد التأمل فيما يترتَّبُ على نفيهِ مِنَ المستحيلات ؛ كالدور ، والتسلسل ، وتعددِ الآلهة ، وتخصيص كلّ واحدٍ منهم بنوع من الممكنات بلا مُخصص ، ونظير هذا في الوجوب النظري : كونُ الواحدِ رُبعَ عُشْرِ الأربعين وهذا الواجب المعرَّفُ هو الواجب الذاتي . وأما الواجبُ العَرَضي - وهو ما يجبُ لتعلقِ إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى بهِ ؛ كتعذيب أبي جهل - : فإنَّهُ بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ جائز يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمه ، وبالنظر إلى ما أخبرَ بهِ الصادق المصدوق صلوات الله وسلامه عليهِ مِنْ إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى لعذابه .. هو واجب لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمه (۱) وإنَّما لم يحتج إلى تقييد الواجب بالذاتي ؛ لأنَّه عند الإطلاقِ لا يُحمَلُ إلا على الذاتي ، ولا يُحمَلُ على العَرَضي إلا بالتقييدِ ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق (۱) تعذيب أبي جهل أخزاه الله : واجب عرضي بواسطة الشرع المؤيد بنور العقل وجائز عرضي عند فاسد الاعتقاد الذي يعتقد جواز دخول الكفَّار الجنة ، ومستحيل عرضي عند الدهرية الذين لا يؤمنون بيوم الحساب ، وبه تعلم : أن الشرع وضع ، وأن ما يعرض للحكم العقلي إنما يعتبر بالعادة أو الشرع الصحيح ، وعروض ما سواهما كالساقط .
The Necessary: that whose non-existence is not conceivable in the mind; either necessarily, like occupying space for a body, or by reflection, like the necessity of pre-eternity for our Lord, Majestic and Mighty. Meaning: the reality of the rational necessary is that whose non-existence is not conceived in the mind; i.e., the mind does not perceive its non-existence (1) either by necessity, i.e., initially without reflection, like occupying space for a body, which is its taking up an amount of void equal to its essence; for the affirmation of this meaning for it is not conceivable in the mind to negate it necessarily. And an analogue of this in necessary obligation is that two is more than one. (1) Or by reflection, i.e., after contemplation, like the affirmation of pre-eternity for our Lord, Blessed and Exalted; for its negation concerning Him, Glorious and Exalted, is not conceivable in the mind, but after reflecting on the impossibilities that follow from its negation, such as circularity, infinite regress, multiplicity of gods, and the specification of each of them with a type of possible things without a specifier. An analogue of this in theoretical obligation is that one is a quarter of a tenth of forty. And this defined necessary is the essential necessary. As for the accidental necessary – which is what is necessary due to the attachment of Allah's will to it, such as the punishment of Abu Jahl – it is, with respect to its essence, possible, its existence and non-existence being sound in the mind; but with respect to what the Truthful and Believed (peace and blessings upon him) informed of, i.e., Allah's will for his punishment, it is necessary, its non-existence not being conceivable in the mind (1). And there was no need to qualify the necessary as essential because, when unqualified, it is only applied to the essential, and is not applied to the accidental except with qualification. And with Allah is success. (1) The punishment of Abu Jahl (may Allah disgrace him) is accidental necessary by way of the Law supported by the light of reason, and accidental possible with one of corrupt belief who considers it permissible for disbelievers to enter Paradise, and accidental impossible with the materialists who do not believe in the Day of Reckoning. And by this you learn that the Law is established, and that what accrues to the rational judgment is only considered by custom or the correct Law, and the accrual of anything else is like being dropped.
Source pages p. 140

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of the necessary (al-wajib) in rational theology and then distinguishes between two types: the necessary by essence (al-wajib al-dhati) and the necessary by accident (al-wajib al-'aradi). The author begins by stating that the necessary is that whose non-existence is not conceivable (la yutawwar) in the mind. This inconceivability can occur in two ways: necessarily (daruratan) or theoretically (nazaran). The necessary by necessity is immediately obvious without reflection, like the fact that a body occupies space—it is inconceivable for a body not to have extension. The necessary by theoretical reflection requires contemplation, such as the pre-eternity (qidam) of God. One cannot conceive of God being non-eternal, but only after considering the absurdities that would follow from denying His pre-eternity, such as infinite regress or circular causality. The passage then clarifies that this definition covers only the essential necessary (al-wajib al-dhati), i.e., that which is necessary in itself. Then it introduces the accidental necessary (al-wajib al-'aradi), which is something that in itself is possible (ja'iz), but becomes necessary due to an external factor, specifically the will of God as revealed through the Prophet. An example is the punishment of Abu Jahl: in itself, it is possible, but because God has willed it and informed us through the truthful Prophet, it is necessary that it occur (from the perspective of divine decree). The author explains that the unqualified term 'necessary' refers only to the essential necessary; the accidental necessary must always be qualified as such. The footnote adds further nuance: the accidental necessary is mediated by the Law (shar'), and its opposites (possible, impossible) can also be accidental depending on one's belief system. This passage is a classic kalam definition and classification, showing the interplay between reason and revelation.

الواجب الذاتي
الواجب العرضي
ضرورة
نظر
التحيز
القدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the necessary means only what is necessary in itself, ignoring accidental necessity.
Correction: This passage shows that there is also accidental necessity arising from divine will and revelation, though essential necessity is the primary meaning.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of the essential necessary (al-wajib al-dhati)?
A) It is known only through revelation.
B) Its non-existence is inconceivable in the mind, either immediately or after reflection.
C) It is possible in itself but becomes necessary due to God's will.
D) It is something that exists only in the mind.

Key Takeaway
The necessary being is defined as that whose non-existence is inconceivable, and this applies essentially to God, while other things can be necessary accidentally by God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's existence is necessary by reflection, have you personally reflected on the absurdities that would follow if He did not exist? If not, how can you claim certainty in your belief, and when will you undertake that reflection?

The accidental necessity of Abu Jahl's punishment shows that some things become necessary by God's will. Does this make you examine your own relationship with God—are you comfortable with the idea that your ultimate destiny is already decreed? How does this affect your daily choices and sense of responsibility?
?
The passage distinguishes between obvious necessity (like a body occupying space) and reflective necessity (like God's pre-eternity). Which type of certainty do you have about God? Is it based on deep reflection or just inherited belief? What specific step will you take this week to move from blind acceptance to reflective certainty?

The Accidental Necessary and the Rule of Unqualified Usage
Sanusi defines the accidental necessary and explains the rule for unqualified usage of 'necessary'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the accidental necessary and why the term 'necessary' when used without qualification refers only to the essential necessary.

Core Idea
Unqualified 'necessary' means essential, not accidental.

Sanusi has already introduced the three rational judgments. Now he turns to a finer distinction: the 'accidental necessary' (al-wājib al-ʿaraḍī). This concept helps us understand that what is necessary in one respect may be possible in another. He also clarifies a key rule of terminology: when we say 'necessary' without further description, we always mean the essential necessary. Let's see how he builds this point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice how Sanusi defines the accidental necessary and gives the example of Abu Jahl's punishment.
2
Understand the two perspectives
Sanusi says the accidental necessary is possible in itself (jā'iz) but necessary due to God's will. Identify these two perspectives: essence vs. divine will.
3
Grasp the rule of unqualified usage
Sanusi explains that 'necessary' without qualification (bi-al-iṭlāq) only applies to the essential necessary. The accidental necessary requires explicit qualification. Ask yourself why this rule matters.
4
Reflect on the footnote
The footnote shows that the same event can be accidental necessary, possible, or impossible depending on one's perspective (sound religion, corrupt belief, materialism). Consider how this illustrates the principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الواجبُ العَرَضي - وهو ما يجبُ لتعلقِ إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى بهِ ؛ كتعذيب أبي جهل - : فإنَّهُ بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ جائز يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمه ، وبالنظر إلى ما أخبرَ بهِ الصادق المصدوق صلوات الله وسلامه عليهِ مِنْ إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى لعذابه .. هو واجب لا يتصوَّرُ في العقل عدمه (۱) وإنَّما لم يحتج إلى تقييد الواجب بالذاتي ؛ لأنَّه عند الإطلاقِ لا يُحمَلُ إلا على الذاتي ، ولا يُحمَلُ على العَرَضي إلا بالتقييدِ ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق (۱) تعذيب أبي جهل أخزاه الله : واجب عرضي بواسطة الشرع المؤيد بنور العقل وجائز عرضي عند فاسد الاعتقاد الذي يعتقد جواز دخول الكفَّار الجنة ، ومستحيل عرضي عند الدهرية الذين لا يؤمنون بيوم الحساب ، وبه تعلم : أن الشرع وضع ، وأن ما يعرض للحكم العقلي إنما يعتبر بالعادة أو الشرع الصحيح ، وعروض ما سواهما كالساقط .
As for the accidental necessary – which is what is necessary due to the attachment of the will of God Almighty to it; like the punishment of Abu Jahl – it is, with respect to its essence, possible (jā'iz), its existence and non-existence being rationally conceivable; and with respect to what the truthful and attested Messenger (peace and blessings of God be upon him) informed of, concerning the will of God Almighty for his punishment, it is necessary, whose non-existence is inconceivable in the mind (1). And the reason why it was not needed to qualify 'necessary' with 'essential' is that when used unqualified (ʿinda al-iṭlāq), it is only applied to the essential; it is not applied to the accidental except with qualification. And with God Almighty is success. (1) The punishment of Abu Jahl (may God humiliate him) is an accidental necessary by means of the revealed law supported by the light of reason, an accidental possible for those of corrupt belief who consider it permissible for disbelievers to enter Paradise, and an accidental impossible for the materialists (dahriyya) who do not believe in the Day of Reckoning. By this you learn that the revealed law is established, and that what attaches to the rational judgment is only considered by custom or by sound revelation; attachment to anything else is like null.
Source pages p. 141

Commentary
This passage defines the accidental necessary and explains the rule for using the term 'necessary' (واجب) without qualification. The author first defines the accidental necessary (الواجب العرضي) as something that becomes necessary only because God's will attaches to it. The example given is the punishment of Abu Jahl: in itself, his punishment is possible (jā'iz) – both its existence and non-existence are rationally conceivable. However, because the Prophet (peace be upon him) informed us that God wills his punishment, it becomes necessary from that perspective, and its non-existence is now rationally inconceivable. The author then explains why it is not necessary to add the word 'essential' (ذاتي) when speaking of the necessary: by default, when the term 'necessary' is used unqualifiedly (عند الإطلاق), it only refers to the essential necessary (الواجب الذاتي). The accidental necessary is only called 'necessary' when explicitly qualified (بالتقييد). The footnote elaborates on the example of Abu Jahl, showing that the same event can be considered accidental necessary, accidental possible, or accidental impossible depending on the perspective (sound religion, corrupt belief, or materialism). It also establishes that accidental judgments are based on custom or sound revelation, not on arbitrary factors.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the accidental necessary is still called 'necessary' without qualification.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that unqualified usage of 'necessary' only applies to the essential necessary, not the accidental.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why is the accidental necessary not called 'necessary' without qualification?
A) Because it is not truly necessary.
B) Because when unqualified, 'necessary' only refers to the essential necessary.
C) Because it is only necessary by custom.
D) Because it depends on the will of God.

Key Takeaway
The term 'necessary' without qualification refers exclusively to the essential necessary; the accidental necessary requires explicit qualification.

The Impossible: Essential and Accidental
Sanusi defines the impossible and distinguishes its essential and accidental types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the impossible in kalam, its two types (essential and accidental), and how accidental impossibility relates to the possible.

Core Idea
What is impossible in itself differs from what becomes impossible due to an external cause.

Sanusi now turns to the second of the three rational judgments: the impossible. He defines it and immediately distinguishes two kinds: one that is impossible in itself, and another that only becomes impossible due to an external cause. This distinction is crucial for understanding how kalam classifies statements about Allah and creation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Focus on the definition and the two divisions.
Notice the use of 'إما ضرورة وإما نظرا'.
2
Identify the essential impossible
Find where Sanusi says 'هذا أيضاً هو المستحيل الذاتي' and understand that both necessary and reflective impossibilities fall under essential.
3
Understand the accidental impossible
Read the sentence beginning with 'وأما المستحيل لعارض' and note that it is from the category of the possible.
This is a key distinction.
4
Study the analogues
Sanusi gives analogues for necessary and reflective impossibility. Compare them to the original examples.
This reinforces the concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالْمُسْتَحِيلُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةً ؛ كَتَعَرِّي الْجِرْم عَن الْحَرَكَةِ وَالسُّكُونِ مَعاً ، وَإِمَّا نظراً ؛ كَالشَّرِيكِ لِمَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ هذا أيضاً هو المستحيل الذاتي . وأما المستحيلُ لعارض منفصل عنه : فهو مِنْ قبيل الجائز ؛ كاستحالة إيمان أبي لهب لما عرضَ لَهُ مِنْ إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى لعدمِهِ . ونظير تعرّي الجِرْمِ عنِ الحركة والسكون – أي : تجرُّدِه عنهما معاً - في كونه مستحيلاً ضرورةً ؛ أي : ابتداءً بلا تأمل : كون الاثنين مثلاً رُبعَ الأربعة ، أو نصف الثمانية ، أو نحو ذلكَ مِنَ المستحيلاتِ الضرورية ، ونظيرُ الشريكِ في كونه مستحيلاً بالنظرِ ؛ أي : بعد التأمل : كونُ الواحد نصفَ عُشْرِ الأربعين
The impossible (al-mustaḥīl) is that whose existence is inconceivable in the mind, either necessarily (ḍarūratan) – like a body being devoid of both motion and rest together – or by reflection (naẓaran) – like a partner for our Lord, Majestic and Exalted. This also is the essential impossible. As for the impossible due to an external cause (li-ʿāriḍ munfaṣil ʿanhu), it belongs to the category of the possible (jāʾiz), like the impossibility of Abu Lahab’s belief due to what attached to him of God’s will for its non-existence. The analogue of a body being devoid of motion and rest – meaning being stripped of both together – in being an impossible by necessity, i.e., primitively without reflection, is, for example, two being a quarter of four, or half of eight, or the like of those necessary impossibilities. And the analogue of the partner in being an impossible by reflection, i.e., after reflection, is one being half of a tenth of forty.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage defines the impossible (المستحيل) and divides it into essential and accidental types. The author first gives a general definition: the impossible is that whose existence is inconceivable in the mind. This impossibility can be either necessary (ضرورة) – grasped immediately without reflection, like a body being simultaneously devoid of both motion and rest – or by reflection (نظراً) – reached after reasoning, like the existence of a partner to God. Both of these are instances of the essential impossible (المستحيل الذاتي), i.e., impossible in itself. Then he distinguishes the accidental impossible (المستحيل لعارض), which results from an external cause separate from the thing itself. This type actually belongs to the category of the possible (الجائز) in itself, but becomes impossible due to some external factor. The example is the impossibility of Abu Lahab’s belief: in itself, his belief is possible, but because God willed its non-existence, it becomes impossible. The author then provides analogues to clarify the two kinds of essential impossibility: for necessary impossibility, the analogue is mathematical truths like two being a quarter of four; for reflection-based impossibility, the analogue is one being half of a tenth of forty. This passage systematically lays out the classification of impossibility in kalam reasoning.

Key Terms
المستحيل
المستحيل الذاتي
المستحيل العرضي
ضرورة
نظر
الجائز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think accidental impossibility is a separate category of impossibility, distinct from the possible.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that accidental impossibility belongs to the category of the possible (ja'iz) in itself; it is only rendered impossible by an external cause.

Checkpoint
How does Sanusi classify the accidental impossible (المستحيل لعارض)?
A) As a type of essential impossible.
B) As a type of the possible (الجائز) in itself.
C) As a type of the necessary.
D) As a separate category unrelated to the possible.

Key Takeaway
The impossible is divided into essential (in itself) and accidental (due to external cause), and the accidental impossible is actually a possible thing that becomes impossible by God's will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Do you treat certain sins as 'impossible in themselves' for you to commit, when in reality they are only 'accidentally impossible' due to God's protection? How does this lesson humble you, realizing that your faith, like Abu Lahab's disbelief, could become impossible if God wills otherwise? Will you thank Allah tonight for every good deed you performed, acknowledging it was only by His will?
?
The lesson distinguishes between impossibility by necessity and by reflection. Are there truths about Allah, His greatness, or your own mortality that you accept only intellectually (by reflection) but have not yet become immediate, heart-felt convictions? What step can you take today to move from 'naẓaran' to 'ḍarūratan' in your faith?
?
If Abu Lahab's belief was possible in itself but became impossible because Allah willed its non-existence, what does that imply about your own salvation? Are you relying on your own choices, or on Allah's decree? How does this lesson reshape your understanding of tawakkul, and what is one area of your life where you need to trust Allah's will more than your own plans?

Definition of the Impossible and Its Two Modes
Sanusi defines the impossible and differentiates between necessary and inferential impossibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of the impossible and how it is recognized either necessarily or through inference.

Core Idea
The impossible is what the intellect cannot conceive as existing.

Sanusi begins by defining the impossible as that which the intellect cannot conceive as existing. This foundational concept is crucial because it sets the boundary of rational thought. The impossibility can be perceived directly or through reasoning, and Sanusi gives clear examples to illustrate both modes.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Note the key terms: 'mustahīl' (impossible), 'ḍarūrah' (necessity), and 'naẓar' (reflection).
The definition is concise: 'mā lā yataṣawwaru fī al-ʿaqli wujūduh'.
2
Identify the two modes
The passage states that impossibility is perceived either necessarily (ḍarūrah) or inferentially (naẓaran). Underline the words 'ḍarūrah' and 'naẓaran' in the Arabic.
Necessary impossibility is immediate; inferential requires reasoning.
3
Examine the examples
Study the examples: a body being free from motion and rest simultaneously (necessary), and a partner to God (inferential). Then consider the additional mathematical examples provided later for each mode.
The examples clarify how each mode works in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد المستحيل
وَالْمُسْتَحِيلُ : مَا لا يُتَصَوَّرُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةً ؛ كَتَعَرِّي الْجِرْم عَن الْحَرَكَةِ وَالسُّكُونِ مَعاً ، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً؛ كَالشَّرِيكِ لِمَوْلانَا جَلَّ وَعَزَّ. هذا أيضاً هو المستحيل الذاتي . وأما المستحيلُ لعارض منفصل عنه : فهو مِنْ قبيل الجائز؛ كاستحالة إيمان أبي لهب لما عرضَ لَهُ مِنْ إرادةِ اللهِ تعالى لعدمِهِ . ونظير تعرّي الجِرْمِ عنِ الحركة والسكون – أي : تجرُّدِه عنهما معاً - في كونه مستحيلاً ضرورةً؛ أي : ابتداءً بلا تأمل: كون الاثنين مثلاً رُبعَ الأربعة، أو نصف الثمانية، أو نحو ذلكَ مِنَ المستحيلاتِ الضرورية، ونظيرُ الشريكِ في كونه مستحيلاً بالنظرِ؛ أي : بعد التأمل: كونُ الواحد نصفَ عُشْرِ الأربعينَ
The definition of the impossible: The impossible is that whose existence is not conceivable in the intellect, either necessarily, like a body being free from both motion and rest simultaneously, or inferentially, like a partner to our Lord, may He be glorified and exalted. This is also the self-impossible (al-mustahīl al-dhātī). As for the impossible due to a separate accidental cause (al-mustahīl li-ʿāriḍin munfaṣilin ʿanhu), it belongs to the category of the contingent; like the impossibility of Abū Lahab's faith due to what occurred to him from the will of Allah, exalted, for its non-existence. An example of a body being free from motion and rest – i.e., stripped of both together – being necessarily impossible, i.e., immediately without reflection: like two being a quarter of four, or half of eight, or similar among the necessary impossibilities. And an example of a partner being impossible by reflection, i.e., after reflection: like one being half of a tenth of forty.
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage defines the impossible (al-mustahīl) and distinguishes its two types. It begins by stating the definition: the impossible is that whose existence is not conceivable in the intellect. This inconceivability can be either necessary (ḍarūrah) – meaning it is immediately obvious without any reflection, such as a body being simultaneously free of both motion and rest – or inferential (naẓaran) – meaning it requires some reasoning, such as the existence of a partner to God. The author then clarifies that this definition refers to the self-impossible (al-mustahīl al-dhātī), i.e., impossible in itself. Next, he introduces another category: the impossible due to a separate accidental cause (al-mustahīl li-ʿāriḍin munfaṣilin), which actually falls under the contingent (al-jāʾiz) because its impossibility arises from an external factor (like God's will). An example is the impossibility of Abū Lahab's faith, which is not impossible in itself but due to God's eternal decree. The passage then provides further examples: for necessary impossibility, the case of a body being devoid of both motion and rest, or mathematical impossibilities like 'two is half of eight' (which is actually false? Wait, the text says 'two being a quarter of four, or half of eight' – these are actually true statements? Actually they are impossible because two cannot be half of eight; half of eight is four. So the example is a statement that is contradictory: 'two is half of eight' is false, hence impossible. Similarly, 'one is half of a tenth of forty' – half of a tenth of forty is two, so one cannot be that. So these are examples of necessary impossibilities because they are immediately recognized as false. For inferential impossibility, the example of a partner to God requires reasoning to see the impossibility. The passage thus systematically categorizes the impossible into necessary and inferential, and distinguishes self-impossible from accidentally impossible.

الضرورة
النظر
المستحيل الذاتي

Key Takeaway
The impossible is that which cannot be conceived, either immediately or after reflection.

Self-Impossible vs. Impossible by External Accident
Sanusi distinguishes the impossible due to an external cause from the self-impossible.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between the self-impossible (impossible in itself) and the impossible due to an external cause, which is actually a kind of contingent.

Core Idea
Not all impossibilities are intrinsic; some depend on external factors like divine will.

After defining the self-impossible, Sanusi introduces a second category: the impossible due to a separate accidental cause. This type is not impossible in itself but becomes impossible due to an external factor, such as God's decree. This nuance is important for understanding the scope of divine power and human responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of accidental impossibility
Read the Arabic and translation for 'al-mustahīl li-ʿāriḍ'. Note that it is defined as belonging to the category of the contingent (al-jāʾiz).
This is a key point: it is not impossible in itself.
2
Understand why it falls under the contingent
Reflect on the phrase 'huwa min qabl al-jāʾiz'. This means that the thing itself is possible in its essence, but an external factor makes it impossible.
The external factor is God's will in the example given.
3
Reflect on the example of Abu Lahab
Consider why Abu Lahab's faith is impossible: not because faith is inherently impossible for him, but because God willed that he would never believe.
This shows how divine decree can create impossibility without altering the intrinsic nature of the act.

?

?

Which of your supposed 'limitations' might actually be an external accident—a habit, a convenience, a fear—that you can change through sincere effort and dependence on Allah?

Definition and Divisions of the Contingent
Sanusi defines the contingent and lists its three subdivisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi defines the contingent (al-jā'iz) and classifies it into three rational subdivisions based on the certainty or possibility of its existence or non-existence.

Core Idea
The contingent is what can be or not be, and it comes in three types: certain to exist, certain not to exist, and possible either way.

Sanusi begins by establishing the concept of the contingent, a fundamental category in kalam. He defines it as that which is intellectually valid both in existence and non-existence, and then clarifies that this validity is considered with respect to the thing's own essence. He then enumerates three distinct types of contingent, showing how this category applies to both necessary truths of revelation and open possibilities.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the English translation to get the overall meaning.
2
Identify the definition of al-jā'iz
Locate the definition: 'that whose existence and non-existence are valid in the intellect.' Notice the two ways this validity is known: necessarily and inferentially.
The example of motion is necessary; the example of punishment/reward is inferential.
3
Understand the three divisions
The passage lists three divisions: certain to exist, certain not to exist, and possible both ways. For each, note the examples given by Sanusi.
These examples are from both rational and revealed sources.
4
Note the qualification 'in itself'
Sanusi emphasizes that contingency is considered with respect to the thing's own essence, not due to external factors. This ensures that even things revealed as certain are still contingent in themselves.
This clarifies a common misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد الجائز
وَالْجَائِزُ : مَا يَصِحُ فِي الْعَقْلِ وُجُودُهُ وَعَدَمُهُ؛ إِمَّا ضَرُورَةٌ؛ كَالْحَرَكَةِ لَنَا، وَإِمَّا نَظَراً؛ كَتَعْذِيبِ الْمُطِيعِ وَإِثَابَةِ الْعَاصِي. الجائز : لفظ مشترك يُطلَقُ ويُرادُ بهِ هذا الذي ذكرنا هنا؛ وهو ما لا يترتب على تقدير وجودِهِ ولا على تقدير عدمِهِ محال لذاته، وهذا معنى قولنا : ( يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمُهُ ) أي : لا يلزمُ مِنْ هذين التقديرين فيه محال لذاته. ويدخل فيه ثلاثة أقسام : . الأول : الجائز المقطوع بوجودِهِ : كاتصافِ الجِرْمِ المطلقِ بخصوص البياضِ أو خصوص الحركة ونحوهما، وكالبعث والثواب والعقاب ونحو ذلكَ. الثاني : الجائزُ المقطوعُ بعدمِهِ : كإيمان أبي لهب وأبي جهل، ودخول الكافر الجنة، ونحو ذلكَ. الثالثُ : المحتمل للوجودِ والعدم : كقبول الطاعة منا، وفوزنا بحُسْنِ الخاتمة، والسلامةِ مِنْ عذاب الآخرة، ونحو ذلكَ. وإنَّما زدنا التقييد بالذاتِ في قولنا : ( لا يترتب على تقدير وجودِهِ ولا على تقدير عدمِهِ محال لذاتِهِ ) أي : بالنظر إلى ذاتِ ذلك الجائز؛
The definition of the contingent: The contingent is that whose existence and non-existence are valid in the intellect, either necessarily, like motion for us, or inferentially, like punishing the obedient and rewarding the disobedient. The contingent is a homonym (lafẓ mushtarak) applied to and intended by what we have mentioned here, and it is that from whose assumption of existence or assumption of non-existence no impossibility in itself follows. This is the meaning of our statement: 'its existence and non-existence are valid in the intellect', meaning: from these two assumptions no impossibility in itself necessarily results. It includes three divisions: First: the contingent known to exist with certainty, like the characterization of an absolute body with a particular whiteness or a particular motion and the like, and like resurrection, reward, punishment and the like. Second: the contingent known not to exist with certainty, like the faith of Abū Lahab and Abū Jahl, the entry of the disbeliever into Paradise, and the like. Third: that which is possible of existence and non-existence, like the acceptance of obedience from us, our attaining a good end, safety from the punishment of the Hereafter, and the like. And we added the qualification 'in itself' in our statement: 'from whose assumption of existence or assumption of non-existence no impossibility in itself follows', meaning: with respect to the essence of that contingent.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage defines the contingent (al-jāʾiz) and then divides it into three types. It begins with the definition: the contingent is that whose existence and non-existence are both intellectually valid (yaṣiḥḥu fī al-ʿaqli wujūduhu wa ʿadamuhu). This validity can be known either necessarily (ḍarūratan), like motion for us (i.e., we immediately know that motion can exist or not exist), or inferentially (naẓaran), like the possibility of God punishing the obedient or rewarding the disobedient, which requires reasoning about divine justice. Then the author clarifies that the term 'contingent' is a homonym (lafẓ mushtarak) but here it means specifically what does not entail an impossibility in itself whether existence or non-existence is assumed. This is equivalent to saying its existence and non-existence are intellectually valid with respect to its own essence. Next, he lists three subdivisions of the contingent: (1) That which is certain to exist (al-maqṭūʿu bi-wujūdihi), such as the attributes of a particular body (e.g., whiteness or motion), and also matters like resurrection, reward, and punishment which are known from revelation. (2) That which is certain not to exist (al-maqṭūʿu bi-ʿadamihi), such as the faith of Abū Lahab and Abū Jahl, or the entry of a disbeliever into Paradise. (3) That which is possible either way (al-muḥtamilu lil-wujūdi wa l-ʿadam), such as the acceptance of our acts of obedience, our attaining a good end, or safety from punishment in the Hereafter. Finally, the author reemphasizes that the qualification 'in itself' is crucial: the contingency considered is with respect to the essence of the thing, not due to external factors. This ensures that things known by revelation to exist or not exist are still contingent in themselves, but their actual occurrence is determined by God's will and knowledge.

المقطوع بوجوده
المقطوع بعدمه
المحتمل
لفظ مشترك

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that things certain to exist or not exist (like resurrection or the faith of Abū Lahab) are not truly contingent because their actual state is known.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that such things are contingent in themselves because their existence or non-existence does not entail an impossibility in their essence; their certainty comes from external revelation, not from their own nature.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of the third division of the contingent (that which is possible both ways)?
A) Resurrection
B) Faith of Abū Lahab
C) Acceptance of our obedience
D) Reward and punishment

Key Takeaway
The contingent is defined by the intellectual possibility of existence and non-existence in itself, and it includes three subdivisions based on certainty or possibility of actual occurrence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that even the acceptance of your obedience and your good end are contingent—possible but not guaranteed. If Allah does not accept your deeds, what then? Does this possibility make your heart tremble with hope and fear as you pray and give charity today? What will you do this week to turn that hope into action?
?
Resurrection is contingent in itself but certain to occur. Are you living as if that Day is absolutely guaranteed? If you truly believed resurrection is unavoidable, what would you change about how you spend your next hour?
?
The faith of Abū Lahab is contingent but known not to exist—he will never believe. Do you secretly harbor any sin or habit that you assume you can repent from later, as if your good end were not contingent? What one thing will you resolve to change today to show you take your contingency seriously?

Definition of the Contingent and Its Three Divisions
Sanusi defines the contingent and enumerates its three divisions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise definition of the contingent (al-ja'iz) in kalam and the distinction between its three divisions based on certainty of existence or non-existence.

Core Idea
The contingent in itself entails no impossibility whether it exists or not, even if we know it will happen.

Sanusi begins this foundational passage by defining the concept of the contingent (al-ja'iz), which is one of the three rational judgments in kalam. He explains that contingency is about logical possibility, not uncertainty. The passage then classifies contingent things into three categories based on their epistemic status: certain to exist, certain not to exist, or genuinely uncertain. This classification is crucial for understanding how theologians reason about divine attributes, eschatological events, and moral responsibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of al-ja'iz
Read the opening definition carefully. Note that the contingent is defined by the soundness of its existence and non-existence in the intellect, either necessarily or speculatively.
The term 'ja'iz' is homonymous; here it means what is not impossible in itself.
2
Identify the three divisions
Find the three divisions: (1) contingent whose existence is certain, (2) contingent whose non-existence is certain, (3) contingent whose existence and non-existence are both possible. Note the examples for each.
The first two are certain due to external information, not due to their essence.
3
Understand the 'in itself' restriction
Read the explanation of why the author added 'bi al-dhāt' (in itself). This is to include the first two divisions, which in themselves entail no impossibility, even though they are certain externally.
This clarifies that contingency is an ontological property, not an epistemic one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حد الجائز
والجائز : ما يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمه؛ إما ضرورة؛ كالحركة لنا، وإما نظراً؛ كتعذيب المطيع وإثابة العاصي.
الجائز : لفظ مشترك يُطلق ويُراد به هذا الذي ذكرنا هنا؛ وهو ما لا يترتب على تقدير وجوده ولا على تقدير عدمه محال لذاته، وهذا معنى قولنا: ( يصح في العقل وجوده وعدمُهُ ) أي: لا يلزم من هذين التقديرين فيه محال لذاته.
ويدخل فيه ثلاثة أقسام:
الأول : الجائز المقطوع بوجوده: كاتصاف الجرم المطلق بخصوص البياض أو خصوص الحركة ونحوهما، وكالبعث والثواب والعقاب ونحو ذلك.
الثاني : الجائز المقطوع بعدمه: كإيمان أبي لهب وأبي جهل، ودخول الكافر الجنة، ونحو ذلك.
الثالث : المحتمل للوجود والعدم: كقبول الطاعة منا، وفوزنا بحسن الخاتمة، والسلامة من عذاب الآخرة، ونحو ذلك.
وإنما زدنا التقييد بالذات في قولنا: ( لا يترتب على تقدير وجوده ولا على تقدير عدمه محال لذاته ) أي: بالنظر إلى ذات ذلك الجائز؛ أي: حقيقته؛ ليدخل فيه القسمان الأولان؛ وهما المقطوع بوجوده، والمقطوع بعدمه، فإن كل واحد منهما بالنظر إلى ذاته لا يلزم محال في وجوده ولا عدمه؛ فإن الثواب والعقاب مثلاً بالنظر إلى حقيقتهما لا يلزم في وجودهما ولا عدمهما محال، ولو نظرنا إلى ما تعلق بهما من إخبار الله تعالى ورسله عليهم الصلاة والسلام بوجودهما.. لترتب حينئذٍ على عدمهما محال؛ وهو الكذب والخلف في خبر من يستحيل عليه ذلك، ونحو ذلك البعث وغيره من الجائزات التي أخبر الصادق المصدق بوقوعها.
وكذا دخول الكافر الجنة؛ فإن نظرنا إلى حقيقته في نفسه.. لم يلزم من وجوده ولا عدمه محال، ولو نظرنا إلى ما عرض له من إخبار الله تعالى ورسله عليهم الصلاة والسلام بأنه لا يكون له دخول الجنة أبداً.. لترتب حينئذٍ على تقدير وجوده محال؛ وهو كذب من لا يجوز عليه الكذب عقلاً.
ويطلق الجائز أيضاً ويراد به المحتمل المشكوك في وجوده وعدمه، فيكون على هذا خاصاً بالقسم الثالث.
Section on the contingent (al-ja'iz)
The contingent (al-ja'iz) is that whose existence and non-existence are sound in the intellect; either necessarily, like movement for us, or speculatively, like punishing the obedient and rewarding the disobedient.
'Contingent' (al-ja'iz) is a homonym (lafẓ mushtarak) used to mean what we have mentioned here; it is that which no impossibility (muḥāl) in itself results from positing its existence nor from positing its non-existence. This is the meaning of our statement: 'whose existence and non-existence are sound in the intellect,' i.e., no impossibility in itself follows from these two postulations regarding it.
And it includes three divisions:
First: the contingent whose existence is certain, such as the description of an absolute body with a particular whiteness or a particular movement and the like, and such as the resurrection, reward, punishment, and the like.
Second: the contingent whose non-existence is certain, such as the faith of Abu Lahab and Abu Jahl, the entrance of the disbeliever into Paradise, and the like.
Third: that which is possible of existence and non-existence, such as the acceptance of obedience from us, our attaining a good end, safety from the punishment of the Hereafter, and the like.
And we only added the restriction 'in itself' (bi al-dhāt) in our statement: 'no impossibility in itself results from positing its existence nor from positing its non-existence,' i.e., with respect to the essence of that contingent, i.e., its reality; in order to include the first two divisions, namely that whose existence is certain and that whose non-existence is certain, for each of them, with respect to its essence, no impossibility follows in its existence nor in its non-existence. Indeed, reward and punishment, for example, with respect to their reality, no impossibility follows in their existence nor in their non-existence. But if we consider what is attached to them of the information of God Most High and His messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, of their existence, then at that point impossibility would follow upon their non-existence, namely falsehood and breach of promise in the report of one for whom that is impossible, and likewise the resurrection and other contingent matters about which the truthful and attested-to (al-ṣādiq al-muṣaddaq) informed.
Likewise is the entrance of the disbeliever into Paradise; if we consider its reality in itself, no impossibility follows from its existence nor its non-existence. But if we consider what accrues to it of the information of God Most High and His messengers, peace be upon them, that he (the disbeliever) will never have entrance into Paradise, then at that point impossibility would follow upon the supposition of its existence, namely the falsehood of one for whom falsehood is intellectually impossible.
And 'contingent' (al-ja'iz) is also used to mean that which is possible and uncertain in its existence and non-existence, and thus it is particular to the third division.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification of the contingent (al-ja'iz) in the context of kalam. The author first defines al-ja'iz as that whose existence and non-existence are both intellectually sound, either necessarily (e.g., motion for a living being) or through speculative reasoning (e.g., punishing the obedient—possible in itself but forbidden by revelation). He then clarifies that the term 'ja'iz' is equivocal: in this context it means that no impossibility (muḥāl) follows in itself from either postulating its existence or its non-existence. The definition includes a restriction 'in itself' (bi al-dhāt) to allow the first two of the three subsequent divisions. The three divisions are: (1) the contingent whose existence is certain (e.g., the resurrection, reward, punishment—certain due to divine information); (2) the contingent whose non-existence is certain (e.g., faith of Abu Lahab, entrance of a disbeliever into Paradise—certainly impossible); (3) the contingent that is truly possible (e.g., our obedience being accepted, a good end). After listing them, the author explains why the 'in itself' restriction is necessary: the first two divisions, considered purely in their essence, entail no impossibility; the certainty arises from external factors like God's and the prophets' reports, which would entail impossibility (falsehood) if the reported event did not occur. He illustrates with reward and punishment, and with the disbeliever's entry into Paradise. Finally, he notes that 'ja'iz' is also used in a narrower sense to refer only to the third division (the uncertain possible). The author thus carefully distinguishes between ontological possibility and epistemic certainty.

Key Terms
الجائز (al-ja'iz)
المحال (al-muḥāl)
الضرورة (al-ḍarūrah)
النظر (al-naẓar)
المقطوع بوجوده
المقطوع بعدمه
المحتمل
الذات (al-dhāt)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the first two divisions (certain of existence or non-existence) are not truly contingent because they are certain.
Correction: The author clarifies that they are contingent in themselves because, considered purely in their essence, no impossibility follows from either existence or non-existence. Their certainty comes from external factors like divine revelation, not from their own nature.

Checkpoint
Why did the author add the restriction 'in itself' (bi al-dhāt) to the definition of the contingent?
A) To exclude impossibilities from the definition.
B) To include the first two divisions, which are certain but contingent in themselves.
C) To make the definition applicable only to the third division.
D) To emphasize that all contingents are uncertain.

Key Takeaway
The contingent is defined by logical possibility in itself, not by uncertainty; thus events known by revelation remain contingent in essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of one blessing in your life you treat as guaranteed tomorrow — your health, provision, or a loved one. This lesson says even certain things are contingent in themselves. Does your heart act as if God owes you tomorrow? What would it mean to wake up grateful for each breath as a pure gift, not a right?
?
When you sin, do you presume forgiveness is certain just because God is merciful? Or do you realize it is merely possible — contingent on His will and your repentance? How does this uncertainty drive you to change today, rather than postpone?
?
The lesson distinguishes between what is possible in itself and what is certain because God promised. Are you living as if God's promises — of Paradise for the believer, of Hell for the disbeliever — are certain? Or do you treat them as distant possibilities? Which belief actually shapes your choices this hour?

Primary Definition of the Contingent by Essence
Sanusi defines the contingent by essence and provides two illustrative examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the primary meaning of 'al-jā'iz' as that which does not entail impossibility by its essence, and how divine reports can render it necessary or impossible without altering its essential contingency.

Core Idea
Contingency is defined by essence, not by external reports.

In this passage, Sanusi clarifies a crucial distinction in rational judgment: something can be possible in itself yet become necessary because of divine information. He uses the examples of reward, punishment, and the entry of a disbeliever into Paradise to illustrate how the same thing is contingent by essence but necessitated by external considerations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the footnotes (1) and (2) are part of the passage.
2
Identify the definition of contingent
Locate where Sanusi defines the contingent by essence: it is that for which no impossibility follows from its existence or non-existence when considered by itself.
This definition includes the necessary and impossible in a specific sense.
3
Distinguish essence from external report
Sanusi contrasts two perspectives: considering the thing in itself (essence) versus considering it along with a divine report. The report can make the thing necessary or impossible.
The divine report is from Allah and His messengers.
4
Examine the two examples
First example: reward and punishment are contingent by essence, but divine information of their existence makes them necessary. Second example: the disbeliever's entry into Paradise is contingent by essence, but divine information of its non-occurrence makes it impossible.
In both cases, it is the impossibility of lying in divine reports that necessitates the outcome.
5
Reflect on the implication for divine attributes
The footnotes explain that divine speech is an eternal self-attribute, so lying is inconceivable. This underlies the impossibility of contradictory outcomes.
This connects to the broader discussion of God's attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أي : حقيقتِهِ ؛ ليدخلَ فيهِ القسمانِ الأولانِ ؛ وهما المقطوع بوجوده ، والمقطوعُ بعدمِهِ ، فإنَّ كلَّ واحدٍ منهما بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ لا يلزم محال في وجودِهِ ولا عدمِهِ ؛ فإنَّ الثواب والعقاب مثلاً بالنظرِ إلى حقيقتيهما لا يلزمُ في وجودهما ولا عدمِهما محال ، ولو نظرنا إلى ما تعلَّق بهما مِنْ إخبارِ الله تعالى ورُسُلِهِ عليهم الصلاة والسلام بوجودهما.. لترتَّبَ حينئذٍ على عدمِهما محال ؛ وهو الكذب والخُلْفُ في خبر مَنْ يستحيلُ عليه ذلك ، ونحو ذلكَ البعث وغيرُهُ مِنَ الجائزات التي أخبر الصادق المصدقُ بوقوعها . وكذا دخولُ الكافِرِ الجنةَ ؛ فإن نظرنا إلى حقيقته في نفسه.. لم يلزم مِنْ وجودِهِ ولا عدمِهِ محال ، ولو نظرنا إلى ما عرض لهُ مِنْ إخبارِ الله تعالى ورُسُلِهِ عليهم الصلاةُ والسلامُ بأَنَّهُ لا يكونُ له دخولُ الجنةِ أبداً . . لترتَّبَ حينَئِذٍ على تقديرِ وجودِهِ محال ؛ وهو كذبُ مَنْ لا يجوز عليه الكذب عقلاً (١) يعني : الإخبار بوجود الثواب والعقاب (٢) لأنَّ كلامه تعالى نفسي قديم ، والنفسي لا يتصور فيه الكذب ، ولو تصور الكذب لاستحال الصدق ؛ لأن صفاتِهِ تعالى قديمة ، والمتنافيان لا يجتمعان
Meaning: its essence; so that it includes the first two divisions: that which is definitively existent and that which is definitively non-existent. For each of them, with respect to its essence, no impossibility follows from its existence or its non-existence. For example, reward and punishment, with respect to their essences, no impossibility follows from their existence or non-existence. But if we consider what attaches to them from the information of Allah, Most High, and His messengers, peace and blessings be upon them, about their existence, then impossibility would follow from their non-existence: that is lying and breaking promise in the news of one for whom that is impossible. And similarly, resurrection and other contingent things that the truthful, confirmed one reported their occurrence. Likewise, the entry of the disbeliever into Paradise: if we consider it in itself, no impossibility follows from its existence or non-existence. But if we consider what attaches to it from the information of Allah and His messengers that he will never enter Paradise, then impossibility would follow upon supposing its existence: that is lying of one for whom lying is intellectually impossible. (1) Meaning: the information about the existence of reward and punishment. (2) Because the speech of Allah, Most High, is an eternal self-attribute, and the self-attribute cannot be conceived with lying in it. If lying were conceivable, then truth would be impossible, because His attributes are eternal, and two contradictories cannot coexist.
Source pages p. 144

Commentary
This passage defines the primary meaning of 'al-jā'iz' (the contingent) in rational judgment. Sanusi begins by explaining that the definition 'what does not entail impossibility by its essence' is intended to include the first two rational divisions: the necessary (wājib) and the impossible (mustahīl) are not considered contingent—rather, the contingent includes only what is possible. However, here the definition is applied to the first two divisions in a specific sense: when considering the essence of reward and punishment (thawāb and 'iqāb), neither existence nor non-existence entails impossibility. Then Sanusi shows that due to divine information (khabar) about their occurrence, their non-existence would entail impossibility (lying). Similarly, the entry of a disbeliever into Paradise is possible by essence, but divine information that it will not happen makes its existence impossible. The passage thus clarifies the distinction between essence (dhāt) and external considerations (ʿāriḍ), and how the same thing can be contingent by essence but necessary or impossible by external report. Footnotes (1) and (2) explain that the divine speech is an eternal self-attribute that precludes lying, so the impossibility is based on the impossibility of lying in God.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reward and punishment are necessary by essence, or that the disbeliever's entry into Paradise is impossible by essence.
Correction: Sanusi shows that both are contingent by essence; they become necessary or impossible only due to divine reports.

Checkpoint
What makes reward and punishment necessary rather than contingent?
A) Their essences
B) Divine information about their existence
C) Human reason alone
D) Consensus of scholars

Key Takeaway
The contingent is defined by its essence; divine reports can make it necessary or impossible without changing its essential contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider your own life—your successes, failures, and daily events—do you truly see them as contingent by essence, or do you unconsciously treat some outcomes as inevitable or impossible based on your own judgment? How would your reliance on Allah increase if you internalized that nothing is necessary except what He decrees?
?
Reward and punishment are contingent by essence, but Allah's promise makes them certain. Does this certainty move you to action? Do you live as if Jannah and Jahannam are guaranteed realities, or do you allow yourself to neglect their urgency?
?
Think of something you consider 'impossible' for yourself—perhaps entering Jannah, overcoming a sin, or achieving a goal. According to this lesson, impossibility can only come from divine report, not from the thing's own nature. Are you limiting Allah's power by treating your own judgments as final? Will you repent of that assumption and place your hope entirely in His mercy and power?

Other Conventional Meanings of al-Jā'iz: Probable and Permitted
Sanusi lists two other uses of jā'iz: probable and permitted, then reaffirms the intended rational meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two alternative uses of the term jā'iz in kalam and logic, and why the rational meaning (contingent by essence) is distinct from the probable and the legally permitted.

Core Idea
Jā'iz means 'possible by essence'—not just 'probable' or 'permitted'.

Sanusi clarifies that the term jā'iz is not univocal; it also carries meanings in everyday language and Islamic law. In this passage, he distinguishes between the rational-technical meaning (contingent by essence) and two other conventional uses: the probable (doubtable) and the legally permitted. Understanding these distinctions prevents confusion when reading kalam texts.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how Sanusi introduces two additional meanings of jā'iz.
The passage includes footnoted clarifications; focus on the main text.
2
Identify the two other meanings
The passage presents two additional uses: 1) the probable (muhtamil) that is doubted in existence, and 2) what is permitted by Sharia (mubāḥ).
3
Understand why they are not the intended meaning
Sanusi reaffirms that in rational judgments, jā'iz means 'that which does not entail impossibility by essence'—the first of the three rational judgments. The other uses are secondary.
This aligns with the earlier definition.
4
Connect to logic term 'general possibility'
Note that the logicians' 'general possibility' (imkān ʿāmm) is similar to the rational jā'iz, but in that system things are only divided into impossible and possible.
See the reference to Mi'yār al-ʿIlm.

Commentary
This passage continues by noting two additional conventional meanings of 'jā'iz'. The first is 'probable' (muhtamil) meaning that which is doubted in its existence and non-existence—this restricts jā'iz to the third division of rational judgment (the purely possible). The second is 'permitted by Sharia' (mā adhina fīhi al-sharʿ), which can be synonymous with 'mubāḥ' (permissible) or broader, including obligatory and recommended acts. Sanusi then clarifies that the intended meaning in the context of rational judgments is only the first (the contingent by essence). Footnotes explain grammatical points and the logic terms. This classification prevents confusion between the technical rational term and its everyday or legal uses.
