Jā'iz and Mumkin: One Concept, Two Terms
Sanusi equates jā'iz with mumkin and distinguishes general from special possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that jā'iz and mumkin are synonymous in kalam, and you will distinguish between special possible (rational contingent) and general possible (which includes the necessary and contingent).

Core Idea
In kalam, jā'iz and mumkin are two names for the same rational judgment.

Sanusi has just introduced the three rational judgments: necessary, impossible, and contingent. But the term 'possible' (mumkin) is used differently in logic and kalam. In this passage, he clarifies that in kalam, jā'iz and mumkin are synonyms, and then he explains how logicians further divide possibility into general and special. This will help you avoid confusion when reading later arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic carefully, noticing the key terms jā'iz, mumkin, mutakallimūn, and manṭiq. Then read the English translation to understand the distinctions.
The passage is incomplete; we only have the beginning of the division.
2
Identify the synonymity claim
Sanusi explicitly states that jā'iz ʿaqlī and mumkin are synonymous in mutakallimūn terminology. Understand that this means they can be used interchangeably in kalam contexts.
3
Distinguish special vs. general possible
Special possible (mumkin khāṣṣ) is exactly the rational contingent. General possible (mumkin ʿāmm) includes whatever is not impossible—so it includes both the necessary and the contingent. This is a key distinction between kalam and logic usage.
Memorize: special possible = contingent; general possible = not impossible.
4
Note the incomplete division
The passage ends by beginning a division of jā'iz into two types (e.g., al-jā'iz al-ḍarūrī). We don't have the full division here, but be aware that the contingent is further subdivided.
This incomplete part indicates that there is more to learn about types of contingent.

Commentary
This passage explains that 'jā'iz' (contingent) and 'mumkin' (possible) are synonymous in kalam terminology. Sanusi then distinguishes between 'special possible' (mumkin khāṣṣ), which is the same as rational contingent, and 'general possible' (mumkin ʿāmm), which includes both the necessary and the contingent—only the impossible is excluded. Finally, he begins a new division: the contingent is also divided into two types, such as 'the necessary contingent' (al-jā'iz al-ḍarūrī) exemplified by motion for us. This line is incomplete, but the passage indicates that there are further subdivisions. The footnote (3) cross-references al-Ghazali's 'Mi'yār al-ʿIlm'. This clarifies the technical usage and prevents confusion with logical possibility.

Defining the Rational Ja'iz
Sanusi clarifies the rational meaning of ja'iz by distinguishing it from legal meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the precise meaning of 'ja'iz' as a rational judgment in kalam and how it differs from its legal usage.

Core Idea
The rational ja'iz is defined by its inherent neutrality to existence, not by legal permission.

Sanusi begins by distinguishing multiple meanings of the term 'ja'iz'. In Islamic law, it can refer to what is simply permissible (mubah) or more broadly to any action permitted by the Law, including obligatory and recommended acts. However, in the science of kalam, 'ja'iz' takes on a specific technical meaning as one of the three rational judgments. In this lesson, we will isolate that rational meaning and see why it must be carefully separated from legal and everyday senses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the legal meanings of ja'iz
Notice that Sanusi first explains two legal meanings: one synonymous with mubah, and a broader one that includes wajib and mandub. This sets up the contrast with the rational meaning.
The legal meanings are not the focus of kalam.
2
Identify the rational definition
Sanusi then states the intended meaning in rational judgment: that which, in its essence, does not necessitate impossibility whether it exists or not. Underline this definition.
This is one of the three rational judgments alongside wajib and mustahil.
3
Contrast with other meanings
Sanusi explicitly denies that the rational ja'iz means probable/doubtful, legally permitted, or mubah. Reflect on why each of these is different.
Rational contingency is about essence, not about epistemic uncertainty or legal rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويُطلَقُ الجائز أيضاً ويُرادُ بهِ : ما أذنَ الشرعُ في فعله وتركه ، فيكون مرادفاً للمباح ؛ كالبيع والنكاح ونحوهما ، أو ما أذنَ الشرع في فعله وإن لم يأذن في تركِهِ (۱) .. فيكون على هذا أعمَّ مِنَ المباح ؛ لأنَّه حينئذ يصدقُ على الواجب والمندوب (۲)
وبالجملة : فالجائز الذي هو أحد أقسام الحكم العقلي إنَّما يريدونَ به المعنى الأول ؛ وهو ما لا يترتب على تقدير وجودِهِ ولا على تقدير عدمِهِ بالنظر إلى ذاتِهِ .. محال ، وليس بمعنى المحتمل المشكوك فيه ، ولا بمعنى المأذون فيه شرعاً ، ولا بمعنى المباح
·
ويُطلَقُ أيضاً على الجائز الذي هو أحد أقسام الحكم العقلي : الممكنُ ، فالممكنُ والجائز العقلي في اصطلاح المتكلمين مترادفان ، والممكن الخاص عند أهل المنطق هو المرادف للجائز العقلي ، وأما الممكن العام عندهم : فهو ما لا يمتنع وقوعه ، فيدخل فيه الواجب والجائز العقليانِ ، ولا يخرج منه إلا المستحيل العقلي (٣)
And 'al-ja'iz' is also used, and it is intended: what the Law permits to do and to leave, so it is synonymous with 'al-mubah' (permissible), like buying and marriage and the like; or what the Law permits to do even if it does not permit leaving it (1).. so on this basis it is more general than al-mubah; because then it applies to the obligatory and the recommended (2). In summary: al-ja'iz which is one of the divisions of rational judgment (al-hukm al-'aqli) they intend by it only the first meaning: that which, based on its essence, no impossibility results from the assumption of its existence nor from the assumption of its non-existence; and it is not in the sense of the probable/doubted, nor in the sense of what is permitted by Law, nor in the sense of al-mubah. And also 'al-mumkin' is applied to al-ja'iz which is one of the divisions of rational judgment. Thus al-mumkin and al-ja'iz al-'aqli in the terminology of the mutakallimun are synonymous. And al-mumkin al-khass (the particular possible) according to the logicians is synonymous with al-ja'iz al-'aqli. As for al-mumkin al-'amm (the general possible) according to them: it is that whose occurrence is not impossible, so it includes the wajib (necessary) and al-ja'iz al-'aqliyan (the two rational contingents), and nothing is excluded from it except al-mustahil al-'aqli (the rational impossible) (3).
Source pages p. 145

Commentary
This passage is a definition and classification passage. Sanusi begins by clarifying the multiple meanings of the term 'ja'iz'. He notes that in a legal (shar'i) context, 'ja'iz' can refer either to what is merely permissible (mubah) – e.g., buying and marriage – or more broadly to what the Law permits to do, even if it does not permit leaving it, which then includes obligatory and recommended actions. However, he then states that when 'ja'iz' is used as one of the three rational judgments (wajib, ja'iz, mustahil), it has a specific technical meaning: it is that which, considered in itself, does not entail impossibility whether it exists or not. It is not equivalent to the probable/doubtful, nor to the legally permitted, nor to the mubah. The passage then introduces an important terminological equivalence: in the usage of the mutakallimun, 'ja'iz' (in the rational sense) is synonymous with 'mumkin' (possible). Sanusi further distinguishes between 'mumkin khass' (particular possible), which is the rational ja'iz, and 'mumkin 'amm' (general possible), which includes anything not impossible, thereby including the necessary (wajib) and the two rational contingents (ja'iz and possibly wajib? Actually, 'ja'iz aqliyan' is the rational contingent, but here he says 'wajib and ja'iz aqliyan' are included in mumkin 'amm, so mumkin 'amm excludes only the rational impossible). This distinction is important for understanding kalam arguments about modality.

Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that the rational ja'iz is not the legally permitted nor the mubah; it is a metaphysical modality about the essence of something, not a legal ruling.

A) It is what the Law permits to do and leave.
B) It is what is probable or doubtful.

D) It is synonymous with al-mubah.

Key Takeaway
The rational ja'iz is a metaphysical modality defined by the lack of necessary relation to existence, distinct from legal permission and epistemic doubt.

Ja'iz and Mumkin: Terminology of Possibility
Sanusi equates rational ja'iz with mumkin and distinguishes logical categories of possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the relationship between 'ja'iz aqli' and 'mumkin', and the distinction between particular possible (mumkin khass) and general possible (mumkin 'amm).

Core Idea
In kalam, ja'iz and mumkin are synonyms, but logicians distinguish a broader possible that includes even the necessary.

Sanusi now reveals an important terminological equivalence: in the science of kalam, the rational contingent (ja'iz aqli) and the possible (mumkin) are synonymous terms. However, logicians further divide the possible into two categories: particular possible (mumkin khass) and general possible (mumkin 'amm). This lesson will clarify these distinctions and show how they relate to the other rational judgments of necessary (wajib) and impossible (mustahil).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on mumkin equivalence
Notice that Sanusi states that in mutakallimun terminology, al-ja'iz al-'aqli and al-mumkin are synonyms. This is a key point.
2
Understand mumkin khass
Sanusi says that mumkin khass (particular possible) according to logicians is exactly the rational ja'iz. So it is the same concept.
This is the modality of contingency.
3
Understand mumkin 'amm
Sanusi defines mumkin 'amm as 'that whose occurrence is not impossible'. This is a broader category that includes the necessary (wajib) and the two rational contingents (ja'iz aqliyan), and excludes only the rational impossible (mustahil aqli).
Mumkin 'amm is essentially the complement of impossibility.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that 'possible' (mumkin) in all contexts means the same as 'contingent' (ja'iz).
Correction: Sanusi shows that while in kalam they are synonyms, logicians distinguish between particular possible (mumkin khass = contingent) and general possible (mumkin 'amm = not impossible, which includes the necessary).

Checkpoint
According to the logicians, what is included in al-mumkin al-'amm (general possible)?
A) Only the rational contingent (ja'iz aqli).
B) Only the necessary (wajib).
C) The necessary (wajib) and the rational contingent (ja'iz aqli).
D) Only the impossible (mustahil).

Key Takeaway
In kalam, ja'iz aqli and mumkin are synonyms, but logicians' general possible (mumkin 'amm) includes the necessary and contingent, while particular possible (mumkin khass) is only the contingent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is capable of everything', do you mean everything that is not impossible? How does the distinction between the general and particular possible refine your understanding of divine power? This week, pause before saying 'possible' and ask: 'Is this truly possible in the general sense, or the particular?'
?
Your very existence is contingent—ja'iz aqli. Does that humble you, knowing that you could just as easily not be? How often do you live as if you are necessary? Commit to a daily moment of gratitude for your continued existence as a sheer gift.
?
The theologians equate ja'iz and mumkin, but logicians separate them. Have you ever assumed a word means the same in different contexts, leading to misunderstanding? Think of a time when imprecise language about faith caused confusion. Will you now be more careful with terms like 'possible' and 'necessary' in your own speech about God?

Two Types of Rational Contingency: Necessary and Reflective
Sanusi divides the rational ja'iz into two types: perceived necessarily and by reflection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Sanusi's classification of the rationally contingent (ja'iz) into two types: that which is perceived necessarily and that which requires rational reflection, and why divine reward and punishment fall into the second category.

Core Idea
Not all possibilities are obvious; some require deeper thought about God's attributes.

Sanusi now deepens our understanding of the rational contingent (ja'iz) by dividing it into two types. The first type is immediately obvious, like motion in a body. The second type requires careful rational consideration, as in the case of divine reward and punishment. This distinction is crucial for avoiding theological errors, such as those of the Mu'tazila, who incorrectly deemed certain divine actions impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then read the translation. Notice the clear two-part structure: the first type with its example, and the second type with its example and extended argument.
The footnotes provide helpful context but are not part of Sanusi's main argument.
2
Identify the two types
Identify the criterion Sanusi uses to divide the ja'iz: whether its possibility is perceived necessarily (without reflection) or only through reflection (ta'ammul).
The first type is immediate; the second requires reasoning.
3
Examine the second example
Focus on the example of punishing the obedient. Why does the intellect initially reject it? What reflection corrects this?
This ties into divine attributes like oneness and sovereignty.
4
Understand the theological argument
Trace the argument: after reflecting on God's uniqueness, exclusive creation, and lack of constraint, we realize that no defect follows from any arrangement of reward and punishment.
This refutes the Mu'tazila position.
5
Review the footnotes
Read the footnotes: one explains the specification 'we, the class of bodies', the other highlights al-Ghazali's classification of the Mu'tazila error.
These show scholarly engagement with the issue.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جائز تُدرَكُ صحة وجودِهِ وعدمِهِ ضرورةً ؛ أي : بلا تأمل ؛ كاتصافنا - معشر الأجرام (۱) ـ بخصوص الحركة ؛ فإنَّا بالمشاهدة نعلمُ صحة وجودها وعدمها للجرْمِ .
وجائز لا يُدرَكُ إلا بالتأمل ؛ كتعذيب مَنْ أطاعَ الله تعالى ولم يعصِهِ قطَّ ، فإنَّ هذا في الابتداء قد ينكرُ العقلُ جوازه ، بل يتوهَّمُهُ مستحيلاً كما توهَّمَتْهُ المعتزلة (۲) ، وأمَّا بعد النظر في وحدانية الله تعالى وانفرادِهِ بخلْقِ جميعِ الممكنات وإرادتها بلا واسطةٍ ، خيراً كانَتْ أو شرّاً ؛ وأنَّ الأفعال كلها بالنسبة إليهِ تعالى سواء ، لا نفع له تبارك وتعالى في طاعة ، ولا ضرر ولا نقص يلحقه جلَّ وعزّ بكفر كافر أو معصية عاص ، ولا حَجْرَ عليهِ ولا حُكْمَ لأحدٍ عليه.. فنعلم حينئذٍ على القطع أنَّ ما رتَّبَهُ سبحانه وتعالى على الكفرِ مِنَ العذاب الأليم ، وعلى الطاعةِ مِنَ النعيم المقيم ، لو عكس تعالى في ذلكَ ، أو لم يُرتِّبْ جلَّ وعلا عليهما شيئاً أصلاً .. لم يلزم عن ذلكَ بالنظر إلى حقيقة الطاعة والكفر والمعصية نقص ولا محال أصلاً ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
(1) الباعث على الاختصاص هنا : زيادة البيان ، وغالباً ما يكون مثل هذا الاختصاص للفخر أو للتواضع
·
(۲) وهو أحد أغلاط ثلاثة ذكرها حجة الإسلام في ( الاقتصاد » ( ص۳۰۹ ) ، ثانيها : سبق الوهم إلى العكس ، وثالثها : تعميم الأحكام ، وأحد مدارك سبعة للغلط في القياس ذكرها في " محك النظر » ( ص ١٥٢ )
A ja'iz whose existence and non-existence are perceived necessarily, i.e., without reflection; like our being characterized – we, the class of bodies (1) – with particular motion; for we know by observation the possibility of its existence and its non-existence for the body. And a ja'iz that is not perceived except by reflection; like the punishment of one who obeyed God and never disobeyed Him; for initially the intellect may deny its permissibility, rather imagine it impossible, as the Mu'tazila imagined it (2). But after reflecting on the oneness of God and His uniqueness in creating all possibles and willing them without intermediary, be they good or evil; and that all actions are equal with respect to Him, no benefit to Him in obedience, no harm or defect attaches to Him – may He be glorified and exalted – from the disbelief of a disbeliever or the disobedience of a sinner; and there is no constraint upon Him nor any judgment of anyone against Him... then we know with certainty that what He – glorified and exalted – has ordained of painful punishment for disbelief and perpetual bliss for obedience, if He were to reverse that or not ordain anything for them at all... then no defect nor impossibility would follow from that with respect to the reality of obedience, disbelief, and disobedience. And with God is success. (1) The reason for the specification here is greater clarity, and often such specification is for pride or humility. (2) And it is one of three errors mentioned by Hujjat al-Islam in al-Iqtisad (p. 309); the second of them: the priority of imagination to the opposite; the third: generalization of judgments; and one of the seven sources of error in analogy mentioned in Mihakk al-Nazar (p. 152).
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage is a classification passage with an illustrative proof. Sanusi divides the rational contingent (ja'iz) into two types. First, a contingent whose possibility (that it can exist or not exist) is perceived necessarily, without any reflection. The example is motion in bodies: we directly observe that a body can be in motion or at rest; no reasoning is needed. Second, a contingent whose possibility is not perceived except through reflection (ta'ammul). Sanusi provides a significant theological example: the punishment of a person who always obeyed God and never sinned. Initially, the intellect might deny this possibility, even imagining it impossible, as the Mu'tazila wrongly did. However, after reflecting on God's absolute oneness, His exclusive creation and will of all things (both good and evil), the fact that actions are equal with respect to Him (no benefit or harm to Him), and that no one has authority over Him, we realize that if God were to punish the obedient or reward the disobedient, or not ordain any consequence at all, no defect or impossibility would follow from the nature of obedience or disobedience themselves. Thus, the contingent nature of divine reward and punishment is established by rational reflection, not immediate intuition. The footnotes provide scholarly references: one explains that the specification 'we, the class of bodies' is for clarity or humility; the other notes that the Mu'tazila's error is one of three common fallacies discussed by al-Ghazali.

Key Terms
جائز
ضروري
تأمل
حركة
تعذيب
معتزلة
وحدانية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That divine reward and punishment are necessary properties of actions, so that punishing the obedient is inherently impossible.
Correction: Sanusi shows that reward and punishment are contingent on God's will; they are not intrinsically necessary. After reflection on divine attributes, we understand that no objective necessity attaches to them.

Checkpoint
What example does Sanusi give for the type of ja'iz that is perceived necessarily?
A) Punishment of the obedient.
B) Motion in bodies.
C) Reward for obedience.
D) The oneness of God.

Key Takeaway
The contingency of divine reward and punishment is not immediately obvious but is established through rational reflection on God's absolute oneness and sovereignty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that God 'should' treat people in a certain way based on your own sense of fairness? How does Sanusi's insistence that God is under no obligation challenge that assumption? Will you let go of that expectation today, or will you cling to it?
?
If God were to punish you for a good deed you did, would you still submit to His judgment without questioning His justice? This passage tells you that such an act entails no defect in God. Are you prepared to accept that with full trust, or does your heart resist?
?
When you reflect on the fact that your obedience brings no benefit to God and your sin causes Him no harm, how does that reshape the way you approach worship? Do you perform acts of obedience for His sake alone, or are you still seeking some return? What would it mean to truly worship without expectation?

Two Types of Possible (Ja'iz) Judgments and Refutation of Mu'tazila
Sanusi distinguishes between two kinds of possible judgments and rebuts the Mu'tazila's error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two types of possible (ja'iz) judgments and why the Mu'tazila's rejection of some possibilities is based on insufficient reflection.

Core Idea
Not all possibilities are self-evident; some require deep theological reflection.

Sanusi introduces a critical distinction within the category of 'possible' (ja'iz). Some possibilities are immediately obvious, like the motion of bodies. Others, like God punishing a lifelong obedient person, initially seem impossible and were even denied by the Mu'tazila. This lesson shows how proper reflection on God's oneness and sovereignty reveals such actions to be truly possible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Read the first part of the passage and note how Sanusi defines the first type (known by necessity) and the second type (known by reflection).
The first type does not require thought; the second requires ta'mul.
2
Examine the Mu'tazila's error
Notice that the second type initially leads the intellect to deny its possibility, and the Mu'tazila even considered it impossible. Understand why they made this mistake.
They relied on initial imagination without deeper reflection.
3
Study the corrective reflection
Read the part beginning with 'But after reflecting on the oneness of God...' and see how the author builds the argument from tawhid to show that God is not subject to restrictions.
Focus on the equality of actions for God and His lack of need.
4
Draw the conclusion
Understand that after this reflection, we know with certainty that punishing the obedient would not entail any deficiency or impossibility.
This refutes the Mu'tazila's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جائز تُدرَكُ صحة وجودِهِ وعدمِهِ ضرورةً ؛ أي : بلا تأمل ؛ كاتصافنا - معشر الأجرام - بخصوص الحركة ؛ فإنَّا بالمشاهدة نعلمُ صحة وجودها وعدمها للجرْمِ . وجائز لا يُدرَكُ إلا بالتأمل ؛ كتعذيب مَنْ أطاعَ الله تعالى ولم يعصِهِ قطَّ ، فإنَّ هذا في الابتداء قد ينكرُ العقلُ جوازه ، بل يتوهَّمُهُ مستحيلاً كما توهَّمَتْهُ المعتزلة ، وأمَّا بعد النظر في وحدانية الله تعالى وانفرادِهِ بخلْقِ جميعِ الممكنات وإرادتها بلا واسطةٍ ، خيراً كانَتْ أو شرّاً ؛ وأنَّ الأفعال كلها بالنسبة إليهِ تعالى سواء ، لا نفع له تبارك وتعالى في طاعة ، ولا ضرر ولا نقص يلحقه جلَّ وعزّ بكفر كافر أو معصية عاص ، ولا حَجْرَ عليهِ ولا حُكْمَ لأحدٍ عليه.. فنعلم حينئذٍ على القطع أنَّ ما رتَّبَهُ سبحانه وتعالى على الكفرِ مِنَ العذاب الأليم ، وعلى الطاعةِ مِنَ النعيم المقيم ، لو عكس تعالى في ذلكَ ، أو لم يُرتِّبْ جلَّ وعلا عليهما شيئاً أصلاً .. لم يلزم عن ذلكَ بالنظر إلى حقيقة الطاعة والكفر والمعصية نقص ولا محال أصلاً ، وبالله تعالى التوفيق .
Possible (ja'iz) that is known necessarily (darura) in the validity of its existence and non-existence; i.e., without reflection (ta'mul); like our being described – we, the bodies – with particular motion; for we know by observation the validity of its existence and non-existence for the body. And possible (ja'iz) that is only known through reflection (ta'mul); like punishing one who has obeyed God Almighty and never disobeyed Him, for this at the outset the intellect may deny its permissibility, rather it imagines it impossible, just as the Mu'tazila imagined it. But after reflecting on the oneness (tawhid) of God Almighty and His uniqueness in creating all possible things and willing them without intermediary, whether good or evil; and that all actions are equal in relation to Him, no benefit accrues to Him from obedience, nor harm or deficiency befalls Him from the disbelief of a disbeliever or the disobedience of a sinner; and there is no restriction upon Him nor any authority of anyone over Him – then we know with certainty that what He has ordained upon disbelief of painful punishment and upon obedience of permanent bliss, if He were to reverse that, or were not to ordain anything upon them at all, no deficiency or impossibility whatsoever would follow from that with regard to the reality of obedience, disbelief, and disobedience. And with God Almighty is success.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage classifies the possible (ja'iz) into two types based on how its possibility is known. The author begins by stating the first type: a possible that is known necessarily, without any reflection. The example given is the motion we observe in bodies – we intuitively know that motion can exist or not exist for a body, without needing to think. Then he states the second type: a possible that is known only after reflection. The example is the case of punishing a person who has always obeyed God and never disobeyed. Initially, the intellect might deny this possibility, and the Mu'tazila even considered it impossible. The author then explains the correct reflection that removes this error. One must reflect on God's oneness, that He alone creates all possible things and wills them without any intermediary, whether they are good or evil. All actions are equal with respect to God – obedience does not benefit Him, nor does disobedience harm Him or cause deficiency. No one has authority over Him. After this reflection, we know with certainty that whatever God has ordained – punishment for disbelief and bliss for obedience – if He were to reverse it or assign nothing at all, no deficiency or impossibility would follow from the nature of obedience, disbelief, or disobedience. Thus, the Mu'tazila's error was to judge based on initial imagination rather than proper theological reflection. The author concludes with 'And with God is success.'

جائز (ja'iz)
ضرورة (darura)
تأمل (ta'mul)
توهم (tawahhum)
مستحيل (mustahil)
وحدانية (tawhid)
المعتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazila believed that punishing a lifelong obedient person is impossible (mustahil) because it contradicts justice or wisdom.
Correction: Sanusi shows that after reflecting on God's transcendence and sovereignty, such an action is possible (ja'iz) and does not entail any deficiency in God or absurdity in the reality of actions.

Checkpoint

C) Known by revelation; example: resurrection
D) Known by consensus; example: attributes of God

Key Takeaway
The possibility of divine actions is not always self-evident; theological reflection grounded in tawhid shows that God's actions are not bound by human expectations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

In your own life, have you ever denied a possibility about God because it seemed 'unfair' or 'illogical'? What would it mean for you to truly accept God's absolute freedom in judgment?
?

Definition of Dalil and Its Logical Implication
Sanusi defines dalil and states its logical rule with an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical relationship between evidence (dalil) and the proven (madlul), and why absence of evidence does not imply absence of the proven.

Core Idea
Evidence proves its conclusion, but its absence disproves nothing.

Sanusi begins by establishing a foundational logical principle for all theological argumentation: evidence necessitates its conclusion but does not guarantee its absence. This distinction prevents a common fallacy and clarifies how we reason about God's existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Focus on the precise wording of the rule: what does existence of evidence entail? What does absence of evidence not entail?
2
Identify the two parts of the rule
Sanusi states two conditional statements: (1) if evidence exists, then proven exists; (2) if evidence is absent, proven is not necessarily absent. Notice the asymmetry.
3
Apply to the example of temporality
Consider the temporality (huduth) of the world as evidence for God. Temporality exists now, so God exists. But before the world, temporality did not exist, yet God existed. This shows that the absence of evidence does not affect the proven.
This example reinforces the logical distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي أنَّ الدليل يلزمُ مِنْ وجودِهِ وجود المدلول ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ عدمِ الدليل عدمُ المدلول ؛ كحدوثِ العالمِ مثلاً ؛ فإنَّهُ دليل على وجودِ مولانا جل وعزَّ ، فيلزمُ مِنْ وجودِ الحدوثِ وجود مدلوله الذي هو وجود مولانا جل وعزَّ ، ولا يلزمُ مِنْ عدمِ الدليل ـ الذي هو الحدوث - عدم مدلوله الذي هو وجود مولانا تبارك وتعالى ؛ فإنَّهُ كانَ الحدوث منفيّاً في الأزلِ ، ووجود مولانا جل وعزَّ واجب في الأزلِ وواجب فيما لا يزالُ
It is that the evidence (dalīl) necessitates, from its existence, the existence of the proven (madlūl), and it is not necessary, from the absence of the evidence, the absence of the proven; like the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world, for example; for it is evidence for the existence of our Master, Glorified and Exalted, so from the existence of temporality follows the existence of its proven, which is the existence of our Master, Glorified and Exalted, and it does not follow from the absence of the evidence—which is temporality—the absence of its proven, which is the existence of our Master, Blessed and Most High; for temporality was negated in pre-eternity (al-azal), and the existence of our Master, Glorified and Exalted, is necessary in pre-eternity and necessary in what never ceases.
Source pages p. 166

Commentary
This passage defines the logical relationship between evidence (dalīl) and the thing proven (madlūl). Sanusi states the rule: the existence of the evidence necessitates the existence of the proven, but the absence of the evidence does not necessitate the absence of the proven. He illustrates with the example of the temporality (ḥudūth) of the world: the fact that the world is temporal (hadīth) is evidence for the existence of God. From the existence of temporality, we necessarily conclude the existence of its proven, which is God. However, the absence of temporality (i.e., before the world existed) does not imply the absence of God, because God's existence is necessary in pre-eternity (al-azal) and remains necessary forever. This distinction is crucial in kalām logic: evidence proves the existence of the thing, but the absence of evidence does not disprove it.

Key Terms
دليل
مدلول
حدوث
أزل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence of evidence for something proves it does not exist.
Correction: Sanusi explicitly states that absence of evidence does not necessitate absence of the proven. The example of temporality shows that God existed before the world when temporality was absent.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi's definition of dalīl, if the evidence (dalīl) is absent, does that imply that the proven (madlūl) is absent?
A) Yes, because evidence is necessary for the proven.
B) No, absence of evidence does not imply absence of the proven.
C) It depends on the type of evidence.
D) Only if the proven is also absent in pre-eternity.

Key Takeaway
The existence of evidence compels the existence of the proven, but the absence of evidence leaves the proven's status unchanged.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
What evidence for God's existence do you rely on most? Are you prepared to trust even when that evidence is temporarily out of sight?

Reductio ad Absurdum: Denying Evidence
Sanusi applies reductio ad absurdum to the Christian's admission about evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Sanusi uses the Christian's own logical principle to expose the absurdity of denying the necessity of evidence for divinity.

Core Idea
If absence of evidence doesn't disprove existence, anything could be a god — even a beetle.

Sanusi continues his dialogue with the Christian interlocutor. He now turns the Christian's own principle against him: if the lack of evidence for divinity does not prove non-divinity, then even lowly creatures cannot be ruled out as gods. This leads to a conclusion that even the Christian finds unacceptable, exposing the flaw in his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Pay attention to the dialogue structure.
Notice how Sanusi first asks a question then uses the Christian's answer.
2
Follow the logical trap
Sanusi asks whether beetles could be gods. The Christian says no because of lack of evidence. Sanusi then applies the Christian's own principle about evidence to show that the Christian cannot consistently deny it.
This is a classic reductio ad absurdum argument.
3
Reflect on the Quranic verse
The verse 'So the one who disbelieved was dumbfounded' shows the Christian's inability to respond. Ask yourself why Sanusi ends with this verse.
The verse reinforces the argument's conclusiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قلتُ له : هل يجوز أن نكون نحن وهذه الحيوانات المحتقره كالخنافس ونحوها آلهة ؟ فقال : لا أُجوِّزَ ذلكَ ؛ لعدم دليل الألوهية فيها . فقلتُ له : كيف وقد سلَّمْتَ أنَّهُ لا يلزمُ مِنْ عدمِ الدليل عدم المدلول ؟! فلعلها تكونُ آلهةً في نفس الأمر على مقتضى أصلكم ولم يظهر لكم بعد دليلُ أُلوهِيَّتِها ؟! ﴿ فَبُهِتَ الَّذِي كَفَرَ وَاللَّهُ لَا يَهْدِى الْقَوْمَ الظَّالِمِينَ ﴾ [البقرة : ٢٥٨]
Then I said to him: Is it permissible that we and these despised animals like beetles and the like be gods? He said: I do not permit that, because of the absence of evidence of divinity in them. I said to him: How can that be, when you have acknowledged that from the absence of evidence does not follow the absence of the proven?! Perhaps they are gods in reality according to your principle, and the evidence of their divinity has not yet appeared to you?! So the one who disbelieved was dumbfounded, and Allah does not guide the wrongdoing people. [Al-Baqarah 2:258]
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage presents a further refutation, using the Christian's own admission about the logical rule. Sanusi asks whether humans or lowly animals like beetles could be gods. The Christian instinctively denies it because there is no evidence of divinity in them. Sanusi then reminds him that he earlier accepted that the absence of evidence does not prove the absence of the proven (madlūl). So, by the Christian's own principle, it is possible that these creatures are gods in reality, but the evidence for their divinity simply hasn't been discovered yet. This reduces the Christian's position to absurdity: if he insists that absence of evidence doesn't disprove divinity, he cannot reject the possibility that anything might be a god. The Christian is left speechless (bahita), and Sanusi cites the Quranic verse about the one who disbelieved being dumbfounded.

Key Terms
دليل (dalīl)
مدلول (madlūl)
إله (ilāh)
أصل (aṣl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Absence of evidence is proof of non-existence.
Correction: Sanusi shows that the Christian's own principle admits that lack of evidence does not disprove the existence of something; therefore one cannot rule out divinity solely on that basis.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does Sanusi derive from the Christian's acceptance that absence of evidence does not imply absence of the proven?
A) That beetles might be gods.
B) That evidence is unnecessary for divinity.
C) That the Christian must reject his own principle.
D) That the world could be eternal.

Key Takeaway
Denying the necessity of evidence leads to absurdity because you cannot rule out anything, even beetles as gods.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have likely held beliefs without being able to produce evidence for them. If you were pressed like the Christian, would you be willing to accept that anything without evidence could still be true? How does that challenge your certainty in the oneness of God?
?
Sanusi's argument leaves the Christian speechless. Think of a belief you hold that, if consistently applied, would lead to an absurd conclusion. Are you brave enough to examine that belief today and either abandon it or find a way to limit its application?
?
When you encounter someone who denies God due to lack of evidence, does your faith rely on evidence you can articulate? Or do you, like the Christian, hold a principle that would make any unproven claim equally valid? Search your heart: is your faith built on a foundation that can withstand such logical scrutiny?

God Alone Creates All Actions, Reward and Punishment Are His Will
Sanusi explains that God invents all human actions and ordains reward and punishment by His will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that, according to Sanusi, God alone creates all human acts of obedience and disobedience, and that reward and punishment are expressions of His will and justice, not obligations or emotional reactions.

Core Idea
God owes nothing, gains nothing; reward and punishment are pure favor and justice.

This lesson examines Sanusi's assertion that every act of obedience or disobedience is created by God in His servants. It clarifies that God does not benefit from obedience nor suffer from disobedience, and that reward and punishment are not obligations but expressions of His favor and justice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the emphasis on God's independence.
2
Identify the key claims about God's creation of actions
Look for the statement that acts are 'among His acts that He invents' and the implications that follow: no need, no purpose, no gain or loss.
3
Understand the nature of reward and punishment
See how Sanusi describes reward and punishment as 'favor and justice' and clarifies they are not due to any right or anger.
This reframes divine justice.
4
Reflect on the implications for human responsibility
Ask yourself how this view affects the concepts of human agency and moral responsibility. The commentary will help you connect the dots.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعبادته جل وعلا وخدمته ومعصيتُهُ تبارك وتعالى إنَّما هي أفعال مِنْ أفعاله المخترعة له في ذواتِ عبيده , ليس له حاجةٌ في طاعتِها ولا غرض , ولا ينالُ مِنْ إيجادها كمالاً كما لا ينالُ مِنْ خلقِهِ لأضدادها نقصاً , ثم رتَّبَ سبحانَهُ عليها ما شاءَ مِنْ ثواب وعقاب فضلاً وعدلاً , لا لقضاء حقٌّ في الثوابِ , ولا لإشفاء غيظ في العقاب . [ عجزُ العقولِ عن إدراك الأحكام الشرعيَّة ] فيلزمُ مِنْ هذا كلّهِ عجز العقول عن إدراك أحكامه الشرعيَّةِ مِنْ جهة فكرتها وقياساتها ; إذ لا مثلَ له تبارك وتعالى لا شاهداً ولا غائباً يُقاس عليهِ , وإنَّما تُدرَكُ أمارةُ الثواب والعقاب , وما يُباحُ وما لا يُباحُ ,
And His worship (mighty and exalted) and His service and disobedience to Him (blessed and exalted) are only acts among His acts that He invents in the essences of His servants; He has no need in their obedience nor any purpose, nor does He attain perfection from creating them just as He does not attain deficiency from creating their opposites. Then He (glorified) arranged upon them whatever He wills of reward and punishment, out of favor and justice, not to fulfill a right in reward, nor to satisfy wrath in punishment. [The Incapacity of Intellects to Comprehend Legal Rulings] So it necessarily follows from all this: the incapacity of intellects to comprehend His legal rulings through their thought and analogies, because there is nothing like Him (blessed and exalted) — neither something witnessed nor absent — by which He could be analogized. Rather, only the indication of reward and punishment is perceived, and what is permissible and what is not permissible.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage presents a theological argument about the nature of God's actions and the limits of human reason. Sanusi states that acts of obedience and disobedience are created by God in human beings. God does not need these acts nor does He gain any perfection or deficiency from them. He simply ordains reward and punishment by His will, as favor and justice, not because He is owed anything or to satisfy anger. This leads to the conclusion that human intellects cannot derive legal rulings through reasoning or analogy because God is unique and cannot be compared to anything, whether in the visible world or the unseen. The only way to know obligations is through revelation; reason can only perceive signs of reward and punishment but cannot independently determine the good or evil of actions.

Key Terms
أفعال العباد
ثواب
عقاب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think God rewards because He is obligated or punishes out of anger.
Correction: Sanusi clarifies that reward is pure favor and punishment is pure justice; God has no need or emotional reaction.

Checkpoint
According to Sanusi, why does God reward or punish?
A) Because He needs our obedience.
B) Because He is owed a right.
C) Out of favor and justice.
D) To satisfy His anger.

Key Takeaway
God creates all human actions, and reward/punishment are expressions of His will and justice, not obligations or emotional reactions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever secretly believe that your good deeds put God in your debt? How does the phrase 'not to fulfill a right in reward' expose that assumption, and what must you change in your heart?
?
When you sin, do you imagine God as a vengeful being whose anger must be satisfied? The passage says punishment is not to 'satisfy wrath' — how should this reshape your fear of God and your hope in repentance?
?
If God has absolutely no need of your worship, why does He command it? Is your worship motivated by a desire to please Him or by a subtle sense of doing Him a favor? How can you make your devotion purely for His sake?

Why Reason Cannot Determine Divine Law
Sanusi concludes that intellects are incapable of deducing legal rulings by thought or analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Sanusi argues that human reason cannot independently derive legal rulings due to the uniqueness of God and the consequent impossibility of analogy.

Core Idea
God is incomparable; analogy fails; only revelation guides.

Sanusi now draws the logical conclusion from the previous argument: since God is unique and unlike anything in the visible or unseen worlds, human reason cannot use analogy to determine His laws. The only way to know what is obligatory or forbidden is through divine revelation. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the causal connector 'فيلزم من هذا كله' (So it necessarily follows from all this).
2
Understand the logical consequence
Identify the conclusion: the incapacity of intellects. Then see the reason given: 'because there is nothing like Him.'
3
Identify why analogy fails
Sanusi says there is no similar entity, neither witnessed nor absent, to serve as a basis for analogy. Understand that this means we cannot compare God to anything.
4
Recognize the limited role of reason
Sanusi allows that reason can perceive 'indication of reward and punishment' but cannot independently determine what is permissible or impermissible.
This sets the stage for reliance on revelation.

Consensus on Unity of Truth in Rational Matters
Sanusi states the Ahl al-Haqq consensus on the unity of truth in rational matters and mentions al-'Anbari's opposing view.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sunni consensus that truth is singular in rational theological matters and that error in such matters is sinful, distinguishing this from the pluralist view of al-'Anbari.

Core Idea
In rational theology, truth is one and error is sinful.

This lesson examines a foundational consensus in Sunni theology (Ahl al-Haqq): that in matters accessible to reason, such as the existence and attributes of God, there is only one correct position. Those who hold a different view are not excused; they are sinful. This stands in contrast to a view attributed to al-'Anbari, who held that all interpretations in fundamentals of religion are equally valid. The passage affirms the unity of truth and the moral seriousness of theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the full English translation. Notice that the passage begins with a summary statement, states a consensus, mentions an opposing view, and concludes with a judgment.
2
Identify the two positions
The passage presents the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq (truth is one, error is sinful) and the opposing view of al-'Anbari (all interpretations are valid). Distinguish them clearly.
Al-'Anbari is identified as an innovator (mubtadiʿ).
3
Consider the moral and theological implications
Note the description of the one who errs: sinful (āthim), disobedient (ʿāṣī), wicked (fāsiq). Reflect on what this means for theological disagreement. The passage also mentions that scholars differed on whether such a person becomes an unbeliever (takfīr).
This shows that error is serious but does not automatically entail excommunication.
4
Reflect on the rejection of pluralism
Al-'Anbari's view (taṣwīb) would mean that multiple contradictory positions can all be correct. The consensus rejects this in rational matters (ʿaqliyyāt). Ask yourself: why would this be dangerous for theology?
The passage states al-'Anbari's view departs from the consensus of the umma.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فالذي أجمعَ عليهِ أهلُ الحقِّ : أنَّ الصواب والحق في العقليَّاتِ واحدٌ ، والمخطئ فيهِ آثم عاص فاسق (۲) ، ثم اختلفوا في التكفير على حسَبِ ما سبق . وقد ذهب العنبري مِنَ المبتدعة إلى تصويب أقوال المجتهدين في أصول الدين فيما كانَ عُرضةً للتأويل ، وفارق في ذلك إجماع الأمة (٣)
And in summary: what the people of truth have unanimously agreed upon is that correctness and truth in rational matters are one, and the one who errs therein is sinful, disobedient, and wicked, then they differed regarding declaring [such a person] an unbeliever according to what has preceded. And al-'Anbari, from among the innovators, adopted the view that the statements of the mujtahids in the fundamentals of religion (uṣūl al-dīn) are all correct as long as they are subject to interpretation, and thus he departed from the consensus of the umma.
Source pages p. 183

Commentary
This passage presents a doctrinal consensus of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth, i.e., Sunni scholars) regarding epistemic and moral judgment in rational matters (ʿaqliyyāt). It states that there is a single correct truth in matters that can be known by reason (such as the existence and attributes of God). Therefore, if someone arrives at a different conclusion, they are mistaken, and that error is sinful (āthim), disobedient (ʿāṣī), and wicked (fāsiq). However, the scholars differed on whether such a person is thereby rendered an unbeliever (takfīr) as previously discussed. The passage then mentions the view of ʿUbayd Allāh al-ʿAnbarī, whom they label an innovator (mubtadiʿ), who held that all mujtahids in uṣūl al-dīn are correct (taṣwīb) as long as their interpretations are plausible (on topics open to interpretation). Al-ʿAnbarī's position is presented as a departure from the consensus of the umma (ijmāʿ), which affirms that there is only one correct view and those who err are sinful. This highlights the Sunni commitment to the unity of truth in core theological matters against a form of theological pluralism.

Key Terms
عقليات
تصويب
أصول الدين
إجماع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All interpretations in fundamental theology are equally valid (taṣwīb).
Correction: The Sunni consensus is that there is a single correct truth; other views are erroneous and sinful.

Checkpoint
According to the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq, what is the status of someone who errs in rational matters (ʿaqliyyāt) of religion?
A) They are excused and their error is not sinful.
B) They are sinful, disobedient, and wicked.
C) They become unbelievers automatically.
D) They are considered mujtahids with a valid interpretation.

Key Takeaway
The Ahl al-Haqq consensus affirms a single truth in rational theology, making error a sin, and rejects the pluralist claim that all interpretations are equally valid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused a serious theological error by saying 'it's just a difference of opinion'? How does this lesson challenge that comfort zone?
?
If you truly believed that error in rational matters is sinful, would it change how you approach learning ʿaqīdah? What would you do differently starting today?
?
Are you willing to submit to the consensus of Ahl al-Ḥaqq even when it condemns a view you once found plausible? What holds you back from full submission?

The Consensus of Ahl al-Haqq on Truth in Rational Matters
Sanusi states the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq on truth in rational matters and the sin of error.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq regarding the oneness of truth in rational matters and the status of those who err.

Core Idea
Faith seeks unity: truth is one, error is sin – but not always disbelief.

This lesson presents a foundational agreement among Sunni scholars (Ahl al-Haqq). Before delving into disputed questions, they agreed that in rational theological matters, truth is singular. The one who misses it bears moral responsibility, but the further question of excommunication (takfir) remains open. Let's examine this key consensus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three key claims in order.
2
Identify the three key claims
The passage makes three claims: (1) truth in rational matters is one, (2) the one who errs is sinful, disobedient, and wicked, (3) scholars differed on takfir. Mark each in the text.
These three points build on each other.
3
Reflect on the significance
This consensus establishes a baseline: error in theology is not automatically disbelief. Why might this be important for intra-Sunni debate?
It prevents hasty takfir and allows for legitimate disagreement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبالجملة : فالذي أجمعَ عليهِ أهلُ الحقِّ : أنَّ الصواب والحق في العقليَّاتِ واحدٌ ، والمخطئ فيهِ آثم عاص فاسق (۲) ، ثم اختلفوا في التكفير على حسَبِ ما سبق .
In summary: what Ahl al-Haqq have agreed upon is that correctness and truth in rational matters are one, and the one who errs therein is sinful, disobedient, and wicked, then they differed regarding takfir according to what preceded.

This passage states the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq (the people of truth) on the nature of truth in rational matters. It begins by asserting that truth in such matters is singular and not multiple. The one who errs in these matters is described as sinful (āthim), disobedient (ʿāṣin), and wicked (fāsiq). Then it notes that scholars differed on the further question of whether such a person is a disbeliever (takfīr), but this difference depends on prior established principles. This establishes a baseline: error in rational theology is a sin but not necessarily kufr.

B) He is a sinner, disobedient, and wicked

Key Takeaway
The people of truth agree: truth in rational matters is one; the mistaken person is sinful but not necessarily a disbeliever.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Sanusi reports that scholars differed on takfir (declaring someone a disbeliever) despite agreeing on sinfulness. Does your own community or social circle treat differing theological opinions as automatically excommunicating? What step can you take today to embody the consensus of Ahl al-Haqq by separating error from disbelief?

Three Madhabs on Mutashabih Verses – Overview
The author states the existence of three scholarly positions on interpreting ambiguous verses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that scholars have classified three main approaches to interpreting ambiguous Quranic verses, and you will be able to identify the examples that necessitate such interpretation.

Core Idea
Ambiguous verses exclude the literal meaning, leading scholars to three different methods.

The Quran contains verses that, if taken literally, would imply impermissible attributes for Allah. Because these verses can bear multiple interpretations, scholars developed distinct methodologies to handle them. This lesson introduces the problem and the three broad schools that emerged.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Note the flow from examples to the statement of three schools.
The examples are crucial for understanding why interpretation is needed.
2
Identify the examples given
Find the three verses cited: 'Running by Our eyes,' 'with My hands,' and 'upon the Throne established.' Ask yourself what makes them ambiguous.
3
Notice the classification
The passage states that scholars differed into three madhabs. This sets up the detailed discussion in subsequent lessons.
The three madhabs are not described here, only announced.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان يقبل من التأويل أكثر من معنى واحد؛ كقوله تعالى: تَجْرِى بِأَعْيُنِنَا [القمر: ١٤]، وقوله جل وعلا: لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَىَّ [ ص: ٧٥] ، وقوله تعالى: الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ﴾ [طه: o] ونحوه .. فقد اختلف العلماء في ذلك على ثلاثة مذاهب :
المذهب الأول : وجوب تفويض معنى ذلك إلى الله تعالى بعد القطع بالتنزيه عن الظاهر المستحيل : وهو مذهب السلف ، ولهذا لما سأل السائل مالك بن أنس رضي الله تعالى عنه عن قوله تعالى : ( عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ) قال في جوابه : ( الاستواء معلوم ، والكيف مجهول ، والسؤال عن هذا بدعة ) ، وأمر بإخراج السائل يعني رضي الله عنه : أن الاستواء معلوم من لغة العرب محامله المجازية التي تصح في حق مولانا جل وعلا ، والمراد في الآية منها أو من غيرها بما لم نعلمه .. مجهول لنا ، والسؤال عن تعيين ما لم يرد نص فيه من الشرع بتعيينه .. بدعة ، وصاحب البدعة رجل سوء ، يجب مجانبته وإخراجه عن مجالس العلم ؛ لئلا يدخل على المسلمين فتنة بسبب إظهار بدعته
المذهب الثاني : جواز تعيين التأويل للمشكل ، ويترجح على غيره مما يصح بدلالة سياق أو بكثرة استعمال العرب للفظ المشكل فيه ؛ فتحمل العين على العلم أو البصر أو الحفظ ، وتحمل اليد على القدرة أو النعمة ، ويحمل الاستواء على القهر ، وهذا مذهب إمام الحرمين وجماعة كثيرة من العلماء
المذهب الثالث : حمل تلك المشكلات على إثبات صفات الله تعالى تليق بجلاله وجماله لا يعرف كنهها ، وهذا مذهب شيخ أهل السنة الشيخ أبي الحسن الأشعري رحمه الله تعالى ورضي عنه
قلت : والظاهر أن من احتاط ، وعبر بما يذكره من تأويل لذلك المشكل بلفظ الاحتمال ؛ فيقول : يحتمل أن يكون المراد من الآية والحديث كذا .. فقد سلم من التجاسر وسوء الأدب بالجزم بتعيين ما لم يقم الدليل القطعي على تعيينه ، والله تعالى أعلم
And if it accepts from interpretation more than one meaning; like His saying, the Most High: 'Running by Our eyes' [al-Qamar 54:14], and His saying, the Mighty and Majestic: 'For what I created with My hands' [Sad 38:75], and His saying, the Most High: 'The Most Merciful upon the Throne established' [Taha 20:5] and similar ... then the scholars differed concerning that into three schools: The first school: the obligation of delegating the meaning of that to Allah, the Most High, after decisively negating the apparent impossible meaning, and it is the way of the Salaf. For this, when the questioner asked Malik ibn Anas, may Allah be pleased with him, about His saying, the Most High: 'Upon the Throne established', he said in his answer: 'The establishment is known, the modality is unknown, and asking about this is innovation', and he ordered the expulsion of the questioner. He meant, may Allah be pleased with him: that 'al-istiwā' is known from the Arabic language with its metaphorical senses that are valid in respect of our Lord, the Mighty and Majestic, and the intended meaning in the verse from them or from other than them that we do not know ... is unknown to us, and asking about specifying something that no text from the Revelation has specified its specification is innovation, and the innovator is a bad man, it is necessary to avoid him and expel him from the circles of knowledge lest he cause discord among the Muslims by displaying his innovation. The second school: the permissibility of assigning a definite interpretation to the ambiguous expression, and it outweighs others that are valid, by indication of context or by the frequent usage of the Arabs of the ambiguous expression in that meaning; thus they interpret 'the Eye' as knowledge or sight or preservation, and they interpret 'the Hand' as power or blessing, and they interpret 'al-istiwā'' as domination, and this is the school of the Imam of the Two Sanctuaries and many scholars. The third school: carrying those ambiguous expressions to affirm attributes of Allah, the Most High, befitting His majesty and beauty, whose essence is not known, and this is the school of the Shaykh of Ahl al-Sunnah, Shaykh Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī, may Allah have mercy on him and be pleased with him. I say: And it appears that whoever is cautious, and expresses what he mentions of interpretation for that ambiguous expression with the word of probability, saying: 'It is possible that the intended meaning from the verse and hadith is such ...' then he has been safe from audacity and ill manners of asserting definitively what no definitive proof has established its assignment. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 208

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the three scholarly positions (madhāhib) regarding the interpretation of ambiguous Quranic verses (al-mutashābihāt) that could bear more than one meaning. The author begins by citing examples: 'Running by Our eyes,' 'with My hands,' and 'upon the Throne established,' which are verses where the apparent literal meaning is impossible for Allah. The scholars differed into three schools.

First school (madhhab al-salaf): Obligation of tafwīḍ (delegation) – after affirming that the apparent impossible meaning is negated (tanzīh), the true meaning is delegated to Allah. This is illustrated by the famous response of Imam Mālik when asked about istiwā': he said 'al-istiwā' is known, the modality is unknown.' Here 'known' refers to the linguistic senses that are valid for Allah metaphorically, but which one is intended is unknown. Therefore, asking to specify what the Law has not specified is an innovation, and the innovator should be expelled to prevent fitna.

Second school: Permissibility of ta'wīl (interpretation) – assigning a specific meaning to the ambiguous expression based on contextual evidence or common Arabic usage, e.g., 'eye' interpreted as knowledge or sight, 'hand' as power or blessing, 'istiwā'' as domination. This is attributed to Imām al-Ḥaramayn (al-Juwaynī) and many others.

Third school: Affirming the attributes without knowing their modality (tafwīḍ al-ma'nā?) – this is described as the position of Abū al-Ḥasan al-Ashʿarī: the ambiguous expressions are understood as affirming real attributes of Allah that befit His majesty, but their true reality is unknown.

Finally, the author (likely the commentator) adds a caution (qultu): the safest approach is to express potential interpretations in terms of probability (iḥtimāl) rather than definitive assertion, because one should not be audacious and decide what has no decisive proof.

Key Terms
التأويل
الاستواء
المذاهب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how many scholarly positions are there regarding the interpretation of ambiguous verses?
A) Two
B) Three
C) Four
D) One

Key Takeaway
The three schools of interpretation arise from the need to reconcile the literal impossibility of certain Quranic verses with the obligation to affirm Allah's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter a verse that seems to describe Allah in human terms, does your heart feel unease or do you dismiss it easily? What does that reaction reveal about your understanding of tawheed?
?
The author states that scholars differed into three schools. Have you ever honestly examined whether your own position on such verses is based on evidence or just following a group? Commit to studying each school before choosing.
?
If you were asked to defend one of the three madhabs to someone who rejects it, could you do so with respect and clarity using the exact words of this lesson? If not, what is holding you back from deeper learning?

Rational Judgments
beginner
6 min
The First Madhab: Tafwid of the Salaf
The author presents the first madhab (Salaf) and the story of Imam Malik's response about istiwā'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first madhab's position of tafwīḍ (delegation) and the famous response of Imam Malik regarding the verse of istiwā'.

Core Idea
The Salaf affirm the meaning is known but delegate its specification to Allah.

The first school, attributed to the Salaf, advocates delegating the true meaning of ambiguous verses to Allah after negating the impossible literal sense. This lesson examines Imam Malik's encounter with a questioner about istiwā', which illustrates this approach.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Identify the three parts: the statement of the madhab, the story of Imam Malik, and the explanation of his answer.
2
Understand the concept of tafwīḍ
Tafwīḍ means delegating the true meaning to Allah after negating the impossible literal sense. Notice that the Salaf still say the meaning is 'known' in a linguistic sense.
This differs from total agnosticism.
3
Analyze Imam Malik's response
Imam Malik said: 'al-istiwā' is known, the modality is unknown, asking about it is innovation.' Reflect on why asking about modality is considered innovation.
The reason is that the Law did not specify the exact meaning, so inquiry is blameworthy.
4
Recognize the ruling on the questioner
The questioner was ordered to be expelled to prevent fitna. This shows the seriousness of avoiding speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان يقبل من التأويل أكثر من معنى واحد؛ كقوله تعالى: تَجْرِى بِأَعْيُنِنَا [القمر: ١٤]، وقوله جل وعلا: لِمَا خَلَقْتُ بِيَدَىَّ [ ص: ٧٥] ، وقوله تعالى: الرَّحْمَنُ عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ﴾ [طه: o] ونحوه .. فقد اختلف العلماء في ذلك على ثلاثة مذاهب :
المذهب الأول : وجوب تفويض معنى ذلك إلى الله تعالى بعد القطع بالتنزيه عن الظاهر المستحيل : وهو مذهب السلف ، ولهذا لما سأل السائل مالك بن أنس رضي الله تعالى عنه عن قوله تعالى : ( عَلَى الْعَرْشِ اسْتَوَى ) قال في جوابه : ( الاستواء معلوم ، والكيف مجهول ، والسؤال عن هذا بدعة ) ، وأمر بإخراج السائل يعني رضي الله عنه : أن الاستواء معلوم من لغة العرب محامله المجازية التي تصح في حق مولانا جل وعلا ، والمراد في الآية منها أو من غيرها بما لم نعلمه .. مجهول لنا ، والسؤال عن تعيين ما لم يرد نص فيه من الشرع بتعيينه .. بدعة ، وصاحب البدعة رجل سوء ، يجب مجانبته وإخراجه عن مجالس العلم ؛ لئلا يدخل على المسلمين فتنة بسبب إظهار بدعته
المذهب الثاني : جواز تعيين التأويل للمشكل ، ويترجح على غيره مما يصح بدلالة سياق أو بكثرة استعمال العرب للفظ المشكل فيه ؛ فتحمل العين على العلم أو البصر أو الحفظ ، وتحمل اليد على القدرة أو النعمة ، ويحمل الاستواء على القهر ، وهذا مذهب إمام الحرمين وجماعة كثيرة من العلماء

Key Terms

البدعة
السلف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Tafwīḍ means we cannot know anything about the attribute, not even its linguistic meaning.
Correction: Tafwīḍ acknowledges the linguistic meaning is known but the specific intended sense is unknown.

Checkpoint
According to Imam Malik, what is the ruling on asking about the specific manner (kayfiyya) of Allah's attributes mentioned in ambiguous verses?
A) It is a praiseworthy act.
B) It is an innovation (bid'a).
C) It is obligatory to find an interpretation.
D) It is allowed but not recommended.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf method affirms linguistic meaning but delegates specification of the intended sense to Allah, treating definitive inquiry as innovation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read verses about Allah's attributes—like His hand, face, or coming—does your mind automatically try to imagine a physical image? How does the Salaf's teaching that 'the modality is unknown' challenge your natural curiosity? Will you discipline yourself to stop asking 'how' and simply affirm what Allah has affirmed?
?
Imam Malik ordered the questioner to be expelled from his gathering for asking about the modality of istiwā. Imagine you were that questioner. How would you feel being removed for a question that seemed sincere? Does your reaction reveal pride or submission? Are you willing to accept that some questions are not permitted, even if they come from a desire to understand?
?
The lesson states that the innovator should be avoided to prevent fitna. Is there any area of your faith where you have insisted on knowing more than what Allah has revealed? How can you adopt the humility of the Salaf, trusting that Allah's knowledge is greater than your own?
